Q001 Block 1 Answer: B
HematologyCoagulation

PE after long flight in young woman

Vignette Summary
27F post-cross-country flight → acute dyspnea, tachycardia, hypoxia (89%), unilateral calf swelling/tenderness → PE from DVT → underlying cause.
The Discriminator
Young woman with unprovoked DVT/PE. Think inherited thrombophilia. Factor V Leiden is the most common.
Why the Answer is Correct
Factor V Leiden is a point mutation (Arg506Gln) that renders activated factor V resistant to cleavage by activated protein C. Anticoagulant brake fails, thrombin generation continues unchecked, and venous stasis from prolonged immobility tips the balance to thrombosis. It is the single most prevalent heritable hypercoagulable state in people of European descent (\~5%).
Cognitive Demand
Prevalence ranking within a shared mechanism
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Prevalence Swap. All options cause VTE; NBME forces you to pick the most common inherited cause without giving you a direct frequency clue.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Antithrombin III deficiency. Real cause of unprovoked VTE but much rarer; classically presents with heparin resistance. Adjacent disease.
Antithrombin III deficiency. Is a real inherited thrombophilia, but it affects fewer than 1 in 500 people. Roughly ten times rarer than Factor V Leiden. The classic distinguishing clue NBME uses for ATIII deficiency is heparin resistance: if the vignette mentions that heparin fails to prolong the PTT appropriately, ATIII is the answer. No such clue appears here.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Factor V Leiden. Most common inherited thrombophilia; APC resistance → DVT/PE, especially after immobility.
(C)
Glanzmann thrombasthenia. GPIIb/IIIa defect → bleeding, not clotting. Reverse mechanism.
Glanzmann thrombasthenia. Is a platelet aggregation defect caused by absent or dysfunctional glycoprotein IIb/IIIa. It produces mucocutaneous bleeding. Epistaxis, gingival bleeding, heavy menses. Not thrombosis. The mechanism runs in the opposite direction from what this vignette describes. Any time you see a clotting presentation, every bleeding disorder is eliminated.
(D)
Protein C deficiency. Causes VTE and warfarin skin necrosis but far less common than FVL. Adjacent.
Protein C deficiency. Produces an identical clinical phenotype to Factor V Leiden. Unprovoked VTE in a young person. Because both conditions lead to unregulated thrombin generation. The distinguishing fact is epidemiology: Protein C deficiency affects roughly 0.2-0.4% of the population, while Factor V Leiden affects 3-5%. NBME defaults to the most common unless a specific clue (warfarin skin necrosis, family history of Protein C deficiency) points otherwise.
(E)
von Willebrand disease. Bleeding disorder (mucosal). Reverse mechanism.
von Willebrand disease. Is the most common inherited bleeding disorder, not a clotting disorder. It causes mucosal bleeding, menorrhagia, and prolonged bleeding time. This patient has a clot, not a bleed. VWD is listed here as a high-confidence elimination. If you chose it, you confused the direction of the hemostatic defect.
Costume Change
Reappears as: young woman on combined OCPs developing DVT (OCP + FVL = multiplicative risk). Also reappears as: recurrent spontaneous abortions in a young woman. FVL is one of the heritable thrombophilias associated with placental thrombosis. Also: post-surgical DVT in a young patient with no other risk factors.
Variable Pivot
If the vignette mentions heparin resistance (PTT fails to prolong with heparin) → Antithrombin III deficiency (A). If warfarin-induced skin necrosis is described at treatment initiation → Protein C deficiency (D). If the patient has mucosal bleeding instead of thrombosis → vWD or Glanzmann depending on platelet vs coagulation clues.
Tid Bit"Five Leiden". The 5% mutation that ignores Protein C.
Q002 Block 1 Answer: A
RheumatologyMusculoskeletal

Hand deformities in middle-aged woman

Vignette Summary
53F, 6-yr symmetric hand pain/stiffness, can't button shirt → photograph (MCP/PIP swelling, ulnar deviation, sparing DIPs) → confirmatory test.
The Discriminator
Symmetric small-joint arthritis with morning stiffness in a middle-aged woman = rheumatoid arthritis. Anti-CCP is the most specific serology (\>95%).
Why the Answer is Correct
Citrullination. Conversion of arginine to citrulline by PAD enzymes. Generates neoantigens in synovium. In genetically susceptible patients (HLA-DR4 shared epitope), anti-citrullinated peptide antibodies form, drive synovial inflammation, pannus, and erosive joint destruction. Anti-CCP outperforms RF in specificity and predicts erosive disease.
Cognitive Demand
Diagnostic specificity. Knowing which test confirms, not just which test is elevated
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Antibody lineup. Multiple real antibody tests offered; must know anti-CCP is the most specific for RA, not just associated.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Anti-citrullinated peptide antibody. Highly specific marker of RA; correlates with erosive disease.
(B)
Antimitochondrial antibody. Primary biliary cholangitis. Wrong disease.
Antimitochondrial antibody. Is the diagnostic test for primary biliary cholangitis (PBC), an autoimmune destruction of intrahepatic bile ducts. PBC presents with fatigue, pruritus, jaundice, and elevated alkaline phosphatase. This patient has no liver symptoms and has classic erosive polyarthritis of the small joints. No overlap.
(C)
HLA-DQ2 antibody assay. HLA-DQ2 is associated with celiac disease (and is a tissue type, not an antibody). Wrong territory.
HLA-DQ2. Is a tissue type, not an antibody. It confers susceptibility to celiac disease and is associated with dermatitis herpetiformis. Testing for it requires HLA typing, and it would not be an "antibody assay" in clinical practice. Beyond the nomenclature issue, this patient has no gastrointestinal symptoms or malabsorption, and celiac arthropathy is migratory and non-erosive, not chronic and deforming.
(D)
Erythroid precursor. Anemia/parvovirus. Right fact, wrong question.
Parvovirus B19. Preferentially infects erythroid precursor cells (BFU-E, CFU-E), causing aplastic crisis in patients with chronic hemolytic anemias. In adults it also causes an acute symmetric polyarthritis that closely mimics early RA. Same joints, same distribution. But the key distinction is time: parvovirus arthropathy is self-limiting over days to weeks, while this patient has had 6 years of progressive symptoms. Six-year duration rules out an acute viral arthritis.
(E)
Thrombopoietic precursor. Platelet disorders (ITP, MPN). Wrong territory.
The thrombopoietic cell line produces platelets. Abnormalities here produce essential thrombocythemia or ITP. Essential thrombocythemia causes erythromelalgia, thrombosis, and paradoxically also bleeding. ITP causes petechiae and mucosal bleeding. Neither produces chronic symmetric erosive small-joint arthritis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which antibody predicts erosive disease course in RA? → Anti-CCP (more predictive than RF). Or: woman with symmetric PIP/MCP swelling + morning stiffness > 1 hour + positive RF. Which additional test would be most specific? → Anti-CCP. Or: which antibody is more specific for RA. RF or anti-CCP? → Anti-CCP.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had fatigue + pruritus + elevated alkaline phosphatase → PBC → antimitochondrial antibody (B). If diarrhea + iron deficiency + dermatitis herpetiformis → celiac → HLA-DQ2 or anti-tissue transglutaminase (C). If acute migratory arthritis + facial rash (slapped cheek) in a child → parvovirus B19 (D). If the question asked about RF specifically → RF is HY but less specific than anti-CCP.
Tid BitAnti-CCP = "Confirms Clinically Probable" RA.
Q003 Block 1 Answer: B
NeurologyBrainstem Anatomy

Medial pontine syndrome (Millard-Gubler/Foville)

Vignette Summary
80F post-stroke. Right gaze intact. Left gaze: right eye adducts but left eye stays midline (left abduction failure). Right hemiparesis plus right vibration loss. Lesion in left medial pons involving abducens nucleus/fascicle and corticospinal tract.
The Discriminator
Failure of left eye abduction (not adduction) plus right hemiparesis equals left medial pontine syndrome. This is CN VI (abducens) palsy, not internuclear ophthalmoplegia. INO is adduction failure from MLF damage. Abduction failure is CN VI.
Why the Answer is Correct
A paramedian pontine infarct involving the abducens nucleus and the corticospinal tract also damages the genu of the facial nerve as it loops around CN VI. This produces an ipsilateral (left) lower motor neuron facial palsy, an asymmetric smile, combined with contralateral hemiparesis. This is the Millard-Gubler/Foville pattern.
Cognitive Demand
Anatomical localization to a specific brainstem level and structure
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Level confusion. All wrong answers are real cranial nerve deficits, but each belongs to a different brainstem level. NBME tests whether you know which structures co-localize.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Left facial anesthesia. Trigeminal sensory (CN V) is mid-pons lateral; not the territory here. Wrong territory.
Anesthesia of the left side of the face. Requires damage to the trigeminal nucleus or CN V fiber, located in the lateral pons. Lateral pontine lesions (AICA territory) produce ipsilateral facial pain/temperature loss plus contralateral body pain/temperature loss (crossed sensory syndrome). This lesion is medial, not lateral. The abducens palsy and hemiparesis place it firmly in the paramedian pons. The trigeminal nucleus is not in the shaded territory.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Asymmetric smile. CN VII fascicle wraps the abducens nucleus; both fall together.
(C)
Hoarseness. CN X (medulla). Wrong level.
Hoarseness. Results from damage to the nucleus ambiguus, which contains lower motor neurons for CN IX and X. This nucleus is located in the lateral medulla. Damage there produces Wallenberg syndrome. Ipsilateral facial pain/temperature loss, contralateral body pain/temperature loss, Horner syndrome, dysphagia, and hoarseness. The brainstem diagram shows a pontine lesion, not a medullary one.
(D)
Loss of accommodation. CN III/midbrain. Wrong level.
Loss of accommodation. Requires damage to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus or CN III parasympathetic fibers, located in the midbrain. Midbrain lesions produce Weber syndrome (ipsilateral CN III palsy + contralateral hemiparesis) or Benedikt syndrome (CN III + contralateral tremor/ataxia). CN VI palsy is the clue that this is pons, not midbrain. CN III and CN VI do not co-localize.
(E)
Tongue paralysis. CN XII (medial medulla). Wrong level.
Tongue paralysis (ipsilateral deviation toward the lesion). Results from hypoglossal nerve (CN XII) damage in the medial medulla. Medial medullary syndrome presents with ipsilateral tongue deviation, contralateral hemiparesis (corticospinal tract), and contralateral proprioception/vibration loss (medial lemniscus). This is one level too low. The lesion here is pons, evidenced by the CN VI palsy.
Costume Change
Reappears as: any brainstem stroke with a combination of ipsilateral CN palsy + contralateral hemiparesis (crossed syndrome) → localize the lesion level by which CN is involved. Pons = CN V, VI, VII. Midbrain = CN III, IV. Medulla = CN IX, X, XI, XII. Also reappears as: internuclear ophthalmoplegia questions (MLF lesion).
Variable Pivot
If the lesion caused a down-and-out eye with ptosis and blown pupil + contralateral hemiparesis → midbrain, Weber syndrome, CN III. If the lesion caused ipsilateral facial anesthesia + contralateral body pain/temperature loss + hoarseness + Horner → lateral medulla, Wallenberg, PICA. If the lesion caused tongue deviation + contralateral hemiparesis + contralateral proprioception loss → medial medulla, CN XII.
Tid BitPons CN VI + VII travel together. Lose lateral gaze, lose the smile.
Q004 Block 1 Answer: C
Infectious DiseaseHIV

Pharyngitis with pancytopenia, heterophile-negative

Vignette Summary
32M, 2 wk fever/sore throat, generalized lymphadenopathy, tonsillar exudate, pancytopenia, negative monospot → acute retroviral syndrome.
The Discriminator
Mononucleosis-like illness + pancytopenia + heterophile-negative = acute HIV seroconversion.
Why the Answer is Correct
Primary HIV infection produces a high plasma viremia with massive CD4 T-cell infection in lymphoid tissue, leading to fever, generalized lymphadenopathy, pharyngitis, and transient cytopenias (marrow suppression by cytokines and direct viral effect). Heterophile (Monospot) is negative because the antibody response is HIV-specific, not EBV-driven.
Cognitive Demand
Differential diagnosis of heterophile-negative mononucleosis syndrome
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Negative Monospot anchor. Most students eliminate HIV because they default to EBV for mononucleosis-like illness. The negative heterophile antibody is the pivot that forces you away from EBV.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
EBV. Classic mono, but heterophile is positive in \>85%. Half-truth.
Epstein-Barr virus. Causes the prototype mononucleosis syndrome and should be your first thought with fever, pharyngitis, and lymphadenopathy in a young person. What makes it wrong here is the combination of a negative heterophile antibody test and severe pancytopenia. The heterophile test is positive in over 85% of EBV mononucleosis cases. EBV also causes atypical lymphocytosis (lymphocytosis, not leukopenia), and does not typically produce the degree of marrow suppression seen here (WBC 1500, Hgb 9.6, platelets 60,000).
(B)
Gonococcal pharyngitis. Usually mild/asymptomatic; no pancytopenia. Wrong fit.
Gonococcal pharyngitis from Neisseria gonorrhoeae. Is a localized mucosal infection acquired through oral sexual contact. It presents with sore throat and sometimes purulent exudate, and can cause isolated anterior cervical lymphadenopathy. It does not produce systemic generalized lymphadenopathy involving axillae and inguinal nodes, nor does it cause pancytopenia. The bilateral tonsillar exudate might attract you to gonorrhea, but the systemic lymph node distribution and marrow suppression rule it out.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
HIV. Acute retroviral syndrome = mono-like with cytopenias, heterophile negative.
(D)
LGV (Chlamydia L1-L3). Inguinal buboes and genital ulcer, not pharyngitis with pancytopenia. Wrong disease.
Lymphogranuloma venereum. Is caused by Chlamydia trachomatis serovars L1-L3. It follows a three-stage course: a painless genital ulcer (often missed), followed by painful fluctuant inguinal lymphadenopathy (buboes), followed by fibrosis and rectal strictures. While it involves the inguinal lymph nodes, it does not cause pharyngitis as the primary presentation, and it certainly does not cause the severe pancytopenia seen here.
(E)
Streptococcal pharyngitis. Exudative tonsillitis but no diffuse lymphadenopathy or pancytopenia. Adjacent.
Streptococcal pharyngitis from Group A Streptococcus. Presents with sudden sore throat, high fever, tonsillar exudates, and anterior cervical lymphadenopathy. The Centor criteria. Crucially, Strep causes a neutrophilic leukocytosis (elevated WBC, not pancytopenia), and does not cause axillary or inguinal lymphadenopathy. The WBC of 1500/mm3 (leukopenia) is the clearest eliminator. A bacterial pharyngitis would show a high WBC with left shift.
Costume Change
Reappears as: young person with fever/rash/lymphadenopathy + negative monospot → order HIV RNA viral load. Also: which test is most sensitive for acute HIV infection? → HIV RNA PCR (p24 antigen or antibody test may be negative in window period). Also: patient 2-4 weeks after high-risk exposure with flu-like illness → acute retroviral syndrome.
Variable Pivot
If heterophile antibody test is positive → EBV (A). If the patient had atypical lymphocytosis WITHOUT pancytopenia + posterior cervical lymphadenopathy → still EBV (A). If patient had genital ulcer preceding the lymphadenopathy → LGV (D). If patient had sudden sore throat + fever + anterior cervical nodes ONLY + WBC elevated → Strep (E).
Tid BitMono symptoms + Monospot negative = check HIV.
Q005 Block 1 Answer: C
NephrologyDrug-Induced AKI

AKI on oxacillin with rash + eosinophils

Vignette Summary
60F, 3 wk into IV oxacillin for endocarditis → fever, rash, AKI (Cr 5.7), peripheral eosinophilia, urine eosinophils and WBC casts → acute interstitial nephritis.
The Discriminator
Drug + rash + eosinophils + WBC casts = AIN. Penicillins (esp. Methicillin/oxacillin) are textbook offenders.
Why the Answer is Correct
AIN is a type IV (and partly type I) hypersensitivity reaction in which the drug or its metabolite haptenizes tubular antigens. T cells and eosinophils infiltrate the renal interstitium, producing edema, tubulitis, and impaired concentrating/reabsorptive function. Hyperkalemia and acidosis follow tubular dysfunction.
Cognitive Demand
Histopathological pattern identification matched to clinical presentation
NBME Trap Fingerprint
AKI type identification. Multiple causes of AKI are offered; the urinalysis (WBC casts + eosinophils) is the definitive clue that places this in the interstitium, not the glomerulus or tubule.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Collapsing FSGS. HIV-associated nephropathy; nephrotic, no eosinophils. Wrong disease.
Collapsing focal segmental glomerulosclerosis. Shows collapse and sclerosis of the glomerular tuft with visceral epithelial cell hypertrophy. It is the hallmark lesion of HIV-associated nephropathy and also seen with parvovirus B19 and severe COVID-19. It presents as heavy proteinuria and nephrotic syndrome with rapid progression to ESRD. This patient has no nephrotic syndrome and the urinalysis shows WBC casts with eosinophils. A glomerular disease would produce red blood cell casts and proteinuria, not eosinophiluria.
(B)
Glomerular hypertrophy with hemorrhage/necrosis. Crescentic/RPGN picture; would show RBC casts, not eos. Wrong territory.
Glomerular hypertrophy with hemorrhage and necrosis. Describes the histology of crescentic glomerulonephritis (rapidly progressive GN), seen in ANCA-associated vasculitides (GPA, MPA), anti-GBM disease, or immune complex diseases. It presents as nephritic syndrome with hematuria, red blood cell casts, hypertension, and oliguria. The distinguishing urinalysis finding is RBC casts. This patient has WBC casts and eosinophils. A tubulointerstitial pattern, not glomerular.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Interstitial inflammatory infiltrate. Classic AIN histology. Lymphocytes and eosinophils in interstitium.
(D)
Mesangial expansion + GBM thickening. Diabetic nephropathy. Wrong tempo.
Mesangial expansion with glomerular basement membrane thickening. Is the hallmark of diabetic nephropathy on biopsy. It develops over years to decades of uncontrolled diabetes mellitus. Diabetic nephropathy begins with hyperfiltration, progresses through microalbuminuria, then overt proteinuria, and finally ESRD. This patient's acute presentation with a drug trigger, eosinophilia, and WBC casts is incompatible with the slow progression of diabetic nephropathy.
(E)
Proximal tubular dilation, brush border loss. ATN; would show muddy brown casts, no eos. Adjacent.
Proximal tubular dilation with loss of the brush border is the histopathological signature of acute tubular necrosis. ATN results from ischemia (hypotension, shock) or direct nephrotoxins (aminoglycosides, contrast, myoglobin). Urinalysis in ATN shows muddy brown granular casts. The gold standard distinguishing finding. This patient has no hemodynamic compromise and no exposure to classic tubular nephrotoxins. The presence of eosinophils in the urine is pathognomonic for AIN, not ATN.
Costume Change
Reappears as: NSAIDs causing AKI with rash and eosinophils. Or: PPI-induced AIN (increasingly tested. Often no rash, just unexplained AKI on PPI). Or: rifampin causing AIN. The triad of fever + rash + eosinophilia is classic but appears in only 10-30% of real cases. WBC casts with eosinophiluria is more reliable.
Variable Pivot
If muddy brown granular casts instead of WBC casts → ATN. If red blood cell casts + hypertension + hematuria → crescentic GN (B). If massive proteinuria + hypoalbuminemia + edema → nephrotic syndrome, think FSGS or membranous nephropathy. If normal urinalysis + high BUN/Cr after contrast or surgery → contrast nephropathy (ATN variant).
Tid BitDrug + rash + WBC casts with eosinophils = AIN. Stop the drug.
Q006 Block 1 Answer: D
NeurologySpinal Cord

Cervical syrinx + headache → Chiari

Vignette Summary
20F, progressive arm weakness + cape-like pinprick loss → MRI: cervical syringomyelia + back pain/headache → ask about what?
The Discriminator
Acquired syringomyelia in young adults is most often post-traumatic; congenital is from Chiari I. The headache (occipital, valsalva) plus syrinx points to Chiari, but the question asks what history to obtain. Trauma is the classic acquired cause that can be missed.
Why the Answer is Correct
Spinal cord trauma can obstruct CSF flow and create a fluid-filled cavity (syrinx) that expands over months to years. It enlarges centrally, destroying the crossing spinothalamic fibers in the anterior white commissure → cape-like loss of pain/temperature with sparing of dorsal columns initially.
Cognitive Demand
Etiology recognition for an acquired structural lesion
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Chiari vs trauma misdirect. The clinical picture (headache + syrinx) could suggest Chiari I, but the question asks what history to obtain, and trauma is the most common acquired cause that a patient would not volunteer.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Diet. Not linked to syrinx formation. Irrelevant.
Diet history. Is relevant when suspecting subacute combined degeneration of the spinal cord from vitamin B12 deficiency. B12 deficiency demyelinates the dorsal columns and lateral corticospinal tracts, producing paresthesias, sensory ataxia, and weakness with preserved reflexes early on. Crucially, B12 deficiency does NOT produce a syrinx on MRI. It causes diffuse signal change in the dorsal columns, not a fluid-filled central cavity. The MRI finding of a syrinx rules out a purely nutritional etiology.
(B)
Family illness. Syringomyelia is not Mendelian. Irrelevant.
Family illness history. Is relevant for hereditary neurological conditions such as familial ALS or hereditary spastic paraplegia. Familial ALS involves progressive motor neuron degeneration with upper and lower motor neuron signs but no sensory deficits. The prominent pinprick loss in this patient makes a pure motor neurodegenerative process very unlikely. More importantly, syringomyelia is not a hereditary Mendelian condition.
(C)
Recent travel. Infections (e.g., cysticercosis) rarely cause syrinx; not first-line history. Adjacent.
Recent travel history. Is important when considering infectious myelopathies. Polio (asymmetric flaccid paralysis from anterior horn cell destruction), schistosomiasis of the spinal cord, or transverse myelitis from arboviral infection. These infections cause diffuse myelopathy, not a discrete central fluid-filled cavity. A travel-related etiology would show cord swelling or enhancement on MRI, not a syrinx.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Trauma. Most common acquired cause of syringomyelia.
(E)
Weight loss. Implies malignancy, but intramedullary tumor would show as a mass, not classic central syrinx. Lower yield.
Unintended weight loss. Raises concern for a neoplastic process, including intramedullary spinal cord tumors (ependymoma, astrocytoma) which can obstruct CSF flow and generate a secondary syrinx. This is a legitimate differential, but the MRI description states a central syrinx without mention of an enhancing mass. If a tumor were suspected, the MRI would typically show the underlying lesion. Trauma is still the leading acquired cause, making it the history to obtain first.
Costume Change
Reappears as: athlete with progressively worsening hand weakness and cape-like sensory loss after a collision sport → post-traumatic syringomyelia (spinal cord injury years before symptoms). Also: patient with Chiari I malformation (occipital headache that worsens with Valsalva) + cervical syrinx → congenital version.
Variable Pivot
If the MRI also showed cerebellar tonsillar herniation through the foramen magnum → Chiari I malformation (congenital; different history needed. Birth history, developmental issues). If MRI showed an enhancing mass within the syrinx → intramedullary tumor (weight loss becomes relevant, E). If the patient had prior meningitis → post-inflammatory CSF obstruction.
Tid BitSyrinx = "cape" pain/temp loss; ask about old neck injury.
Q007 Block 1 Answer: E
CardiologyVascular Medicine

Intermittent claudication in diabetic

Vignette Summary
68M, CAD + DM + dyslipidemia → bilateral calf pain with walking, relieved by rest, hairless atrophic cool feet, absent pedal pulses → peripheral arterial disease.
The Discriminator
Classic intermittent claudication in an atherosclerotic patient = atherosclerosis of lower-extremity arteries.
Why the Answer is Correct
Atherosclerosis begins with endothelial injury and LDL infiltration into the intima. Macrophages take up oxidized LDL forming foam cells; smooth muscle migrates and a fibrous cap forms over a lipid-rich necrotic core. Luminal narrowing limits flow reserve; exercise demand exceeds supply → ischemic muscle pain that resolves with rest.
Cognitive Demand
Mechanism identification in atherosclerotic large-vessel disease
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Vasculitis vs atherosclerosis. Several options describe real vascular pathologies; must match the demographic (older diabetic male with multiple CV risk factors) to the correct mechanism.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Fibromuscular dysplasia. Young women, renal artery "string of beads." Wrong demographic.
Fibromuscular dysplasia. Is a non-inflammatory, non-atherosclerotic arterial disease affecting the media and intima. It occurs predominantly in young to middle-aged women and involves the renal, carotid, and vertebral arteries. It presents as resistant hypertension (renal artery) or stroke/TIA (carotid). It does NOT present as bilateral lower extremity claudication in a 68-year-old diabetic man with coronary artery disease.
(B)
Giant cell arteritis. Temporal artery, jaw claudication, age \>50 but vision/ headache, not legs. Wrong vessel.
Giant cell arteritis. Is a granulomatous vasculitis of large vessels in patients over 50. It preferentially involves branches of the external carotid artery, producing temporal headache, scalp tenderness, jaw claudication, and visual loss from ophthalmic artery involvement. It is associated with polymyalgia rheumatica and elevated ESR. It does not cause bilateral lower extremity arterial disease with absent pedal pulses.
(C)
Hyaline arteriosclerosis. Small-vessel HTN/DM change in arterioles (kidney), doesn't cause claudication. Wrong size vessel.
Hyaline arteriosclerosis. Affects small arteries and arterioles through deposition of hyaline material (plasma proteins), driven by benign hypertension and diabetes. It causes nephrosclerosis and contributes to lacunar strokes. It is a small-vessel disease and would not produce the large-vessel occlusion causing absent pedal pulses and intermittent claudication.
(D)
Segmental inflammation of medium vessels. Buerger disease (young smoker) or PAN. Wrong demographic.
Segmental inflammation of medium-sized vessels. Describes polyarteritis nodosa (PAN) or thromboangiitis obliterans (Buerger disease). PAN causes aneurysms and infarction of visceral arteries (intestinal, renal) with systemic symptoms. Buerger disease occurs in young heavy smokers and causes digital/limb ischemia but in a distinct pattern from atherosclerosis. This 68-year-old man with multiple traditional risk factors is a classic atherosclerosis patient. Not a young smoker with no other risk factors.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Vascular obstruction by lipid-rich plaques. Atherosclerotic PAD.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which test confirms PAD? → Ankle-brachial index (ABI) < 0.9. Or: management of claudication → supervised exercise program first, then cilostazol, then revascularization. Or: what is the mechanism of cilostazol? → phosphodiesterase-3 inhibitor → increased cAMP → vasodilation + anti-platelet.
Variable Pivot
If young woman + resistant hypertension + renal bruit → fibromuscular dysplasia of renal artery (A). If > 50 years old + temporal headache + jaw claudication + high ESR → giant cell arteritis (B). If young male smoker + digital ulcers + no other CV risk factors → Buerger disease (D). If small-vessel disease in a chronic hypertensive → hyaline arteriosclerosis (C).
Tid Bit"Hair-less, pulseless, cool" leg with exertional pain = atheromatous PAD.
Q008 Block 1 Answer: A
Behavioral ScienceCommunication

Smoking cessation counseling

Vignette Summary
58M with COPD, not ready to quit smoking → wife coughs from smoke → best physician response.
The Discriminator
Motivational interviewing for a precontemplation/contemplation patient: elicit the patient's own reasons for change (autonomy support), do not lecture or shame.
Why the Answer is Correct
Open-ended, ambivalence-exploring questions help the patient articulate intrinsic motivators, increasing readiness to change. Confrontational, guilt-based, or fact-dumping statements provoke resistance and reduce the likelihood of quitting.
Cognitive Demand
Application of motivational interviewing technique
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Righting reflex. Physicians instinctively want to give advice or guilt-trip the patient into changing. NBME tests whether you recognize the non-confrontational, exploratory approach of MI.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
"Are there any reasons you might want to quit?". Open- ended motivational interviewing prompt.
(B)
"Are you aware your lung condition is chronic?". Lecturing/scare tactic; doesn't elicit change talk.
"Are you aware that your lung condition is chronic?" is a confrontational, information-giving statement that presupposes ignorance and does nothing to explore the patient's ambivalence. In MI, providing unsolicited advice (the "righting reflex") typically produces reactance. The patient defends their behavior rather than considering change. This response does not open a conversation; it closes one.
(C)
"I'm sure you don't want your wife to suffer…". Guilt-tripping; damages alliance.
"I'm sure you don't want your wife to suffer" introduces guilt and third-party pressure. While it might feel compassionate, it is a manipulation tactic that shames the patient. Motivational interviewing is explicitly non-judgmental and collaborative. Guilt-based appeals typically increase resistance rather than motivation in pre-contemplative patients.
(D)
"Most of your health issues would improve…". Unsolicited advice; closed message.
"The majority of your health issues would improve if you quit smoking" is the classic righting reflex. The physician jumps to solutions before the patient is ready. The patient has already stated he is not ready to quit. Advice delivered to a non-receptive patient does not produce behavior change; it produces defensiveness.
(E)
"Why haven't you been able to stay off cigarettes?". Confrontational/ blaming; raises defensiveness.
"Why haven't you been able to stay off cigarettes?" is a confrontational question that implies failure and demands justification. The word "why" in this context puts the patient on trial. MI uses open-ended questions that invite reflection ("What would have to change for you to consider quitting?") rather than interrogative questions that create shame.
Costume Change
Reappears as any pre-contemplative patient (alcohol, drug use, diet, exercise). The correct MI opening is always an open-ended question exploring ambivalence or values, not advice, guilt, or confrontation. The stages are pre-contemplation → contemplation → preparation → action → maintenance.
Variable Pivot
If the patient were in contemplation ("I've been thinking about quitting") → explore ambivalence specifically, build on self-efficacy. If patient is in preparation ("I want to quit, what should I do?") → prescribe varenicline, NRT, bupropion and set a quit date. If patient refuses a clearly beneficial intervention and you need to assess capacity → assess cognition and decision-making ability.
Tid BitMI mantra. Explore ambivalence, don't argue for change.
Q009 Block 1 Answer: B
PediatricsNutrition

Failure-to-thrive toddler on excess formula

Vignette Summary
18-mo girl, was 25th %ile for both, now \<5th %ile weight, length intact, mid-parental height normal. Diet = two 8-oz bottles of formula + small table food → thin, otherwise normal.
The Discriminator
Drop in weight with preserved length = inadequate caloric intake. At 18 months she should be on whole milk + a varied table-food diet; formula alone is too low calorie/volume for her needs and she's eating little real food.
Why the Answer is Correct
Energy intake below metabolic demand depletes fat stores first (weight drops), then linear growth (length); finally head circumference. Her length and parental heights argue against endocrine or genetic causes. She has nutritional FTT.
Cognitive Demand
Growth pattern interpretation distinguishing organic from non-organic causes
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Weight-height discordance. Weight falling off while height is maintained means something is consuming calories without affecting linear growth. Organic causes typically affect both; caloric insufficiency hits weight first.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Adrenal insufficiency. Would show hypotension, hyperpigmentation, hyponatremia. Absent.
Adrenal insufficiency in children. Produces hypoglycemia, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hyperpigmentation (primary), and poor weight gain. While it can impair growth, it would produce systemic signs well before reaching the presentation described. Hypotensive crises, skin changes, lab abnormalities. This child has a completely normal examination without electrolyte or glucose abnormalities mentioned.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Caloric insufficiency. Inadequate solids → weight loss while height preserved.
(C)
Constitutional growth delay. Delays both height and bone age; usually shows in older children. Wrong tempo.
Constitutional growth delay. Is a normal variant producing slow, proportionate growth in both height and weight. The child tracks below average on BOTH curves proportionally, has delayed bone age, but ultimately reaches normal adult height. This patient maintained height at the 25th percentile while weight dropped below the 5th percentile. The DISCORDANCE between height and weight is the key finding that rules out constitutional delay.
(D)
Familial short stature. Parents are average/tall; height is normal anyway. Doesn't fit.
Familial short stature. Produces proportionate short stature consistent with parental heights. The child's parents are 5'6" and 6'0". Both above average. A child of these parents having a height at the 25th percentile and below the 5th percentile for weight does not fit familial short stature, which would predict a child tracking closer to average height.
(E)
GH deficiency. Would impair linear growth predominantly. Reverse pattern.
Growth hormone deficiency. Produces proportionate growth failure affecting both height and weight, with the height impact being more prominent. The typical presentation is a child who falls off both height and weight curves proportionally, with delayed bone age and low IGF-1. This patient's isolated weight loss with preserved height at the 25th percentile argues against GH deficiency.
Costume Change
Reappears as: any infant on excessive cow's milk formula displacing solid foods → iron deficiency + caloric insufficiency. Cow's milk at 18 months should be limited to 16-24 oz/day with solid food diet. Or: toddler with isolated low weight → food diary and feeding observation before labeling organic disease.
Variable Pivot
If BOTH height and weight fell below 5th percentile proportionally → think constitutional delay (C) or familial short stature (D) based on parental heights. If the child had vomiting + fatty stools + failure to thrive → cystic fibrosis or celiac disease. If electrolyte abnormalities + hyperpigmentation → adrenal insufficiency (A).
Tid BitWeight drops before height. Think calories first.
Q010 Block 1 Answer: A
BiostatisticsResearch Methods

Adding yogurt to a food-frequency questionnaire

Vignette Summary
Prospective cohort of dietary calcium vs osteoporosis. FFQ initially missing yogurt → adding it improves what?
The Discriminator
Missing a major calcium source = systematic measurement error (misclassification of exposure). Fixing it improves accuracy of the exposure measure, i.e., internal validity.
Why the Answer is Correct
Internal validity is the degree to which a study correctly measures what it intends to measure within its sample. Omitting yogurt would systematically underestimate calcium intake, biasing the calcium-osteoporosis association toward (or away from) the null. Including yogurt reduces measurement bias and strengthens internal validity.
Cognitive Demand
Identify which research quality metric is improved by adding a missing exposure variable
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Reliability vs validity confusion. Students confuse internal reliability (consistency) with internal validity (accuracy of measurement). Adding a food item doesn't change consistency; it changes completeness of measurement.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Internal validity. Better exposure capture = less measurement bias.
(B)
Inter-rater reliability. Concerns agreement between observers; FFQ is self-report. Wrong concept.
Inter-rater reliability measures the consistency of a measurement when applied by two different observers or raters. It is improved by training raters, standardizing the assessment process, or using objective over subjective measures. Adding yogurt to a food questionnaire does not change how consistently two researchers would score the same response. It changes whether the questionnaire captures the actual calcium intake accurately.
(C)
Response rate. Depends on willingness to participate, not item content.
Response rate. Refers to the proportion of invited participants who complete the study. It is improved by follow-up contacts, incentives, shorter questionnaires, or clearer instructions. Adding one food item to a questionnaire has essentially no effect on whether participants respond. It may marginally lengthen the questionnaire but not meaningfully change participation.
(D)
Type I error. α is set by the investigator; questionnaire content doesn't change it.
Type I error (alpha). Is the probability of falsely rejecting a true null hypothesis. It is controlled by the chosen significance threshold (p < 0.05) and is not affected by questionnaire completeness. Adding or removing a food item from the questionnaire does not change the statistical threshold for the study.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which quality of a diagnostic test does adding a new lab value improve? → If the new value helps it measure the true disease state better → validity. Or: screening test misses cases because a key symptom was excluded from the criteria → internal validity. Missing a confounder → threatens internal validity of conclusions.
Variable Pivot
If two raters score the same questionnaire differently → inter-rater reliability (B). If participants are dropping out → response rate (C). If the significance threshold is being adjusted for multiple comparisons → Type I error (D). If a key confounding variable (like smoking status) was not measured → this is a threat to internal validity in a different way.
Tid BitAccurate exposure measurement = internal validity.
Q011 Block 1 Answer: D
Infectious DiseaseParasitology

Chagas screening positive

Vignette Summary
40F from El Salvador, asymptomatic, donor blood positive for T. Cruzi → vector?
The Discriminator
Trypanosoma cruzi is transmitted by the reduviid ("kissing") bug, which defecates near the bite; the parasite enters when the host rubs feces into the wound.
Why the Answer is Correct
Reduviid bugs (Triatoma) feed at night around the face; their feces contain trypomastigotes that penetrate mucosa or broken skin. Acute infection may show a Romaña sign; chronic disease causes cardiomyopathy and megacolon/megaesophagus years later. She's in the indeterminate phase.
Cognitive Demand
Vector-pathogen pairing for a specific parasitic infection
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Latin America + immigration → Chagas should fire immediately, but students who don't know the reduviid bug will guess mosquito (too familiar).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Bedbug. Cimex; nuisance bites, no systemic disease.
Bedbugs (Cimex lectularius) cause intensely pruritic bites in linear groupings, often at night. They are not considered competent vectors for any significant systemic pathogen in developed countries. They do not transmit Trypanosoma cruzi or any other major parasite.
(B)
Black fly. Onchocerca volvulus (river blindness). Wrong parasite.
Black flies (Simulium species) transmit Onchocerca volvulus, the filarial nematode causing river blindness (onchocerciasis). This disease is endemic in sub-Saharan Africa and parts of Central/South America near fast-moving rivers. It presents with subcutaneous nodules, severe pruritus, and progressive blindness from microfilariae in the anterior chamber. It does not cause the cardiac and GI manifestations of Chagas disease.
(C)
Mosquito. Malaria, filariasis, arboviruses. Wrong parasite.
Mosquitoes transmit malaria (Anopheles species), dengue fever, Zika, yellow fever, and West Nile virus (Culex species), among others. They are not the vector for Trypanosoma cruzi. This is the most common wrong answer because mosquito = Latin America = tropical disease in many students' minds, but the specific vector for Chagas is the triatomine bug.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Reduviid bug. Vector of T. Cruzi.
(E)
Tick. Lyme, RMSF, babesia. Wrong vector.
Ticks transmit Borrelia burgdorferi (Lyme, Ixodes scapularis), Rickettsia rickettsii (Rocky Mountain spotted fever, Dermacentor), Ehrlichia, Anaplasma, and Babesia. They are important vectors for North American diseases but are not involved in Trypanosoma cruzi transmission.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Central/South American immigrant with dilated cardiomyopathy or arrhythmia + achalasia/megacolon → Chagas disease. Or: which drug treats Chagas? → Benznidazole or nifurtimox. Or: describe the mechanism of cardiac disease in Chagas → T. Cruzi destroys autonomic ganglia of the heart and GI tract.
Variable Pivot
If mosquito vector + cyclic fevers + ring forms on blood smear → malaria. If black fly + subcutaneous nodules + vision loss → onchocerciasis. If tick + bull's-eye rash → Lyme disease. If tick + petechial rash starting on wrists and ankles → Rocky Mountain spotted fever.
Tid BitKissing bug poops the parasite. You scratch it in.
Q012 Block 1 Answer: A
Behavioral SciencePatient Communication

Patient refusing meds with herbal tea

Vignette Summary
40M, recurrent IBS visits, drinks grandmother's herbal tea, refuses medication → best response.
The Discriminator
Unclear agenda. The patient keeps coming back yet refuses standard therapy. Use an open-ended question to clarify his expectations and values without judgment.
Why the Answer is Correct
Patient-centered interviewing recommends eliciting the patient's ideas, concerns, and expectations early. Asking what he hopes you can do opens dialogue, respects autonomy, and uncovers his goals (symptom validation? exclude serious disease? endorse his tea?).
Cognitive Demand
Therapeutic communication with a patient who uses alternative medicine and refuses conventional treatment
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The urge to educate or convince. Most wrong answers either push medication or express frustration. The correct answer explores what the patient actually wants from the visit.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
"Help me understand what you are hoping I can do for you today.". Open-ended, patient-centered.
(B)
"I'd like to prescribe medication if you'd reconsider…". Pushes agenda before understanding his.
"I'd like to prescribe some medication if you'd reconsider your decision" is still pushing the physician's agenda after the patient has repeatedly refused medication at prior visits. This response does not acknowledge the patient's autonomy or explore why he keeps returning. It applies the righting reflex. Trying to fix the problem the physician identified rather than the problem the patient brought.
(C)
"I'm not sure why you keep coming to see me.". Dismissive; ruptures alliance.
"I'm not sure why you keep coming to see me" is dismissive and potentially abandoning. It challenges the patient's right to seek care while refusing conventional treatment and threatens the therapeutic relationship. A patient with chronic illness who refuses specific treatments still deserves respectful ongoing care.
(D)
"Let's talk about the herbal tea…". Narrows prematurely.
"Let's talk about the herbal tea you have been drinking" redirects to a tangential topic rather than understanding the patient's current needs. While monitoring herbal remedy safety is legitimate, it doesn't address why the patient came in today or what support he is actually looking for.
(E)
"There's nothing I can do if you refuse medication.". Confrontational and false.
"There is nothing I can do for you if you continue to refuse medication" is a coercive ultimatum that constitutes conditional care. Care withheld unless the patient complies. This violates patient autonomy principles and is the most ethically problematic of the options.
Costume Change
Reappears as any patient who refuses standard treatment (Jehovah's Witness refusing blood, patient refusing chemotherapy, patient using alternative medicine). The correct first response is always to explore goals and understanding. Not to convince, guilt, threaten, or abandon.
Variable Pivot
If the patient refused treatment AND appeared to lack decision-making capacity → assess capacity first. If the patient were a minor → parents' decisions + child's best interest analysis. If the patient had a red-flag symptom (rectal bleeding, weight loss) suggesting something dangerous → address the red flag directly before exploring communication preferences.
Tid BitWhen confused about the visit, ask the patient what success looks like.
Q013 Block 1 Answer: B
UrologyPharmacology

BPH pharmacotherapy

Vignette Summary
72M, frequency, nocturia, weak stream, symmetric enlarged prostate without nodules → benign prostatic hyperplasia → drug mechanism?
The Discriminator
First-line symptomatic BPH therapy = α1-adrenergic antagonist (tamsulosin, terazosin).
Why the Answer is Correct
α1A receptors predominate on prostatic smooth muscle and bladder neck. Blocking them relaxes the dynamic component of bladder outlet obstruction, improving urinary flow within days. (5α- reductase inhibitors shrink the gland over months but weren't an option.)
Cognitive Demand
Receptor pharmacology. Correct receptor type and correct agonist/antagonist direction
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Alpha/beta + agonist/antagonist matrix. Four possible combinations on alpha, four on beta. Must know BPH uses alpha-1 antagonism specifically, not just "alpha blocker."
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
α1 agonism. Would worsen obstruction. Reverse mechanism.
Alpha-1 adrenergic agonism (e.g., phenylephrine, midodrine) contracts smooth muscle in vascular walls, the prostate capsule, and the bladder neck. This would worsen urinary obstruction in BPH by increasing the tone of smooth muscle that is already compressing the urethra. The mechanism is exactly opposite to what is needed.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
α1 antagonism. Relaxes prostatic/bladder neck smooth muscle.
(C)
α2 agonism. Clonidine; lowers central sympathetic tone, not BPH.
Alpha-2 adrenergic agonism (e.g., clonidine, dexmedetomidine) acts centrally to reduce sympathetic outflow and lower blood pressure. Alpha-2 receptors in the periphery are presynaptic. Their activation inhibits further norepinephrine release. Alpha-2 agonism has no direct therapeutic role in BPH.
(D)
α2 antagonism. Mirtazapine effect on mood; irrelevant.
Alpha-2 adrenergic antagonism. Blocks presynaptic inhibition of norepinephrine release, increasing sympathetic activity. Mirtazapine (antidepressant) uses this mechanism along with H1 blockade. This would increase sympathetic tone and worsen the smooth muscle contraction in the prostate, the opposite of what BPH treatment requires.
(E)
β1 agonism. Cardiac inotropy. Wrong system.
Beta-1 adrenergic agonism (e.g., dobutamine) increases heart rate and contractility. It has no direct role in prostate smooth muscle relaxation. Beta-1 receptors are predominantly cardiac.
(F)
β1 antagonism. Antihypertensive; no urinary benefit.
Beta-1 adrenergic antagonism (e.g., metoprolol, atenolol) reduces heart rate and blood pressure. It has no direct role in BPH management. Note: beta-3 agonism IS used for overactive bladder (mirabegron), but that is a distinct condition from obstructive BPH.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which drug class should be avoided in BPH? → Alpha-1 agonists (decongestants like pseudoephedrine) worsen obstruction. Or: man with BPH + hypertension → use an alpha-1 blocker (doxazosin, terazosin) to treat both simultaneously. Or: 5-alpha reductase inhibitor vs alpha-1 blocker. Which works faster? → Alpha-1 blocker (days vs months for 5-ARI).
Variable Pivot
If the question asks about shrinking prostate gland size → 5-alpha reductase inhibitor, finasteride (blocks DHT). If the prostate has a hard irregular nodule → biopsy before treating for BPH. If overactive bladder (urgency/frequency without obstruction) → antimuscarinic (oxybutynin) or beta-3 agonist (mirabegron).
Tid Bit"-osin" relaxes the prostate; "-asteride" shrinks it.
Q014 Block 1 Answer: A
PsychiatryDiagnostic Classification

Acute mania with psychotic features

Vignette Summary
33F, 2-mo grandiose delusion (married to president's twin), decreased sleep, pressured speech, loose associations, auditory hallucinations of "husband" → diagnosis.
The Discriminator
Distractibility, decreased need for sleep, grandiosity, pressured speech, and goal- directed behavior = manic episode. Psychotic features (delusions, hallucinations) are mood-congruent.
Why the Answer is Correct
DSM bipolar I manic episode requires ≥1 week of elevated/irritable mood plus ≥3 DIGFAST symptoms (Distractibility, Impulsivity, Grandiosity, Flight of ideas, Activity, Sleep decreased, Talkativeness). Psychotic features that occur exclusively during mood episodes still classify as bipolar with psychotic features. Not schizophrenia.
Cognitive Demand
Differentiating manic psychosis from primary psychotic disorders based on phenomenology
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Schizophrenia anchoring. The presence of hallucinations and delusions makes students immediately think schizophrenia. The manic features (decreased sleep, pressured speech, grandiosity, goal-directed behavior) are the discriminating elements.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Bipolar I, manic, with psychotic features. Mania + mood- congruent psychosis.
(B)
Brief psychotic disorder. \<1 month and lacks prominent mood symptoms. Duration and mood mismatch.
Brief psychotic disorder. Is defined by the sudden onset of psychotic symptoms lasting more than one day but less than one month. This patient has been stalking the president for two months, indicating a minimum two-month episode. Brief psychotic disorder is also typically precipitated by a major stressor and does not typically feature the prominent manic features (pressured speech, decreased sleep, hyperactivity) seen here.
(C)
Delusional disorder. Non-bizarre delusion ≥1 mo without prominent hallucinations or mood symptoms. She has both.
Delusional disorder. Is characterized by fixed, non-bizarre or bizarre delusions lasting at least one month without the broader psychotic features of schizophrenia. Crucially, delusional disorder does NOT include prominent hallucinations. The patient here reports auditory hallucinations ("I hear his voice") and visual phenomena ("dark eyes"). The presence of hallucinations rules out delusional disorder.
(D)
Psychotic disorder due to medical condition. No medical evidence; would need a causative illness.
Psychotic disorder due to a general medical condition. Requires an identifiable underlying medical or physiological cause (e.g., lupus cerebritis, thyroid storm, CNS infection). The vignette provides no such medical cause. The mention of "natural substances" is a red herring. No substance or medical condition is identified. This diagnosis requires confirming a cause, not just excluding it.
(E)
Schizophrenia. Requires ≥6 mo and psychosis outside mood episodes. Mood symptoms dominate here.
Schizophrenia. Requires symptoms lasting at least 6 months total (including prodromal and residual phases), with at least 1 month of active-phase psychotic symptoms. More importantly, schizophrenia does not feature prominent mood episodes. This patient has classic manic symptoms: days without sleep, rapid pressured speech, grandiosity, goal-directed behavior (rescuing her husband), and impulsivity. When mania and psychosis co-occur, the diagnosis is Bipolar I, not schizophrenia.
Costume Change
Reappears as: psychotic symptoms + prominent mood features → if mania, Bipolar I with psychotic features; if depression, MDD with psychotic features. The timeline of mood vs psychosis is critical. Also: duration rules. < 1 month = brief psychotic; 1-6 months = schizophreniform; > 6 months = schizophrenia.
Variable Pivot
If symptoms lasted < 1 month total → brief psychotic disorder (B). If only fixed delusions without hallucinations or disorganized speech → delusional disorder (C). If psychotic symptoms follow the mood episode and resolve with mood treatment → Bipolar I with psychotic features confirmed. If mood symptoms are brief relative to psychosis (< 30% of total duration) → schizophrenia.
Tid BitPsychosis only during mood episode = mood disorder, not schizophrenia.
Q015 Block 1 Answer: B
GIPharmacology

Cimetidine vs omeprazole on gastric pH

Vignette Summary
Research study measuring gastric pH after cimetidine and omeprazole → match curves.
The Discriminator
PPIs (omeprazole) irreversibly inhibit the H+/K+ ATPase. The final step. Producing the most profound and sustained pH rise. H2 blockers (cimetidine) only block histamine-driven acid secretion; gastrin and ACh still drive parietal cells. Result: PPI gives a higher, longer pH rise than cimetidine.
Why the Answer is Correct
Cimetidine competitively blocks H2 receptors on parietal cells, decreasing cAMP- mediated proton pump activation. Partial suppression. Omeprazole covalently inactivates the pump itself, eliminating acid secretion regardless of upstream stimulus until new pumps are synthesized (\~24-48 h). The omeprazole curve = highest, broadest pH; cimetidine curve = moderate rise.
Cognitive Demand
Matching drug mechanism to magnitude and duration of effect on gastric pH curves
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Curve ranking without mechanistic anchor. If you just guess which curve looks "bigger," you might pick the right answer accidentally, but NBME wants you to know WHY the PPI produces a higher, more sustained curve than the H2 blocker.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
B / D. Likely reversed magnitude or duration. Half-fit.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
B / A. Cimetidine = moderate pH rise; omeprazole = greatest, sustained rise.
(C)
B / D, (D) C / A, (E) C / D, (F) D / A. Each pairs a smaller-magnitude curve with omeprazole or overstates cimetidine. Wrong magnitude/duration.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which drug causes tolerance with chronic daily use? → H2 blockers (tachyphylaxis from receptor upregulation). Or: which drug must be taken 30-60 minutes before a meal? → PPI (pumps must be active to be irreversibly inhibited). Or: which drug interacts with clopidogrel? → PPIs that inhibit CYP2C19 (omeprazole, esomeprazole), reducing clopidogrel activation.
Variable Pivot
If the question were about a patient already on a PPI who needs additional acid suppression → add H2 blocker at night (different mechanism, additive). If asking which drug causes hypergastrinemia with chronic use → PPI (loss of acid feedback → increased gastrin). If asking which drug is safe in pregnancy → none clearly safe, but H2 blockers have more safety data than PPIs.
Tid BitPPIs hit the final common pump. Biggest pH bump.
Q016 Block 1 Answer: B
AnatomyOncologyVenous Drainage

Lung mets from colon cancer

Vignette Summary
65F, stage III right colon cancer post-op → 1 yr later, masses in liver and lungs → route to lungs?
The Discriminator
Colon → portal vein → liver (first). Once liver mets are established, tumor cells embolize from hepatic veins → IVC → right heart → lungs.
Why the Answer is Correct
Right colon drains via the SMV into the portal system, producing initial hepatic metastases. From the liver, tumor cells enter hepatic venous outflow into the inferior vena cava and then the pulmonary circulation, seeding lung parenchyma. This "cavoatrial" route explains the liver-first/lung- second pattern.
Cognitive Demand
Trace the complete venous metastatic route from right colon to lung
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Pulmonary vein misdirect. The pulmonary vein sounds like it should connect lungs to metastasis, but it carries oxygenated blood FROM the lungs BACK to the heart. It cannot carry tumor emboli TO the lungs.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
IMV. Drains left colon, and leads to portal vein/liver. Doesn't reach lungs directly.
The inferior mesenteric vein drains the left colon and upper rectum. The right colon is drained by the superior mesenteric vein (SMV), not the IMV. Selecting the IMV reflects geographic confusion about the colon's segmental blood supply.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
IVC. Hepatic veins → IVC → right heart → lungs.
(C)
Left colic vein. Portal tributary; ends in liver.
The left colic vein is a branch of the IMV draining the descending colon. This is doubly wrong. Wrong side of the colon AND a tributary rather than the main trunk of the venous drainage.
(D)
Middle colic artery. Arterial, not a metastatic pathway.
The middle colic artery is an artery, not a vein, and carries blood TO the transverse colon, not away from it. Tumor emboli travel in veins (away from the tumor), not arteries (toward organs). Arteries cannot carry hematogenous metastases toward the lungs.
(E)
Pulmonary vein. Carries blood away from lungs; wrong direction.
The pulmonary vein carries oxygenated blood from the lungs to the left atrium. It flows in the wrong direction to deliver metastatic emboli to the lungs. It empties INTO the systemic circulation, not into the pulmonary vasculature. Pulmonary metastases arrive via the pulmonary ARTERY.
(F)
SMA. Arterial supply; not a venous metastatic route.
The superior mesenteric artery supplies blood to the right colon but is an artery running in the wrong direction for metastatic spread. Metastases travel in the venous system, not the arterial system.
(G)
SVC. Drains head/upper extremities; not abdominal viscera.
The superior vena cava drains the upper body (head, neck, upper extremities) into the right atrium. Right colon venous blood travels through the SMV → portal vein → liver → hepatic veins → IVC → right heart → pulmonary artery → lungs. The SVC is not part of this pathway.
Costume Change
Reappears as: why does colorectal cancer typically produce liver metastases before lung metastases? → Portal venous drainage means the liver is the first-pass filter. Or: which colorectal cancer can bypass the liver and spread directly to the lungs? → Rectal cancer below the peritoneal reflection drains via internal iliac veins → IVC, bypassing portal circulation.
Variable Pivot
If tumor were in the lower rectum → could drain via internal iliac → IVC → lungs without liver involvement first. If tumor were in the stomach or pancreas → portal vein → liver → hepatic veins → IVC → right heart → pulmonary artery → lungs (same pathway as colon). If asking about brain metastases from lung cancer → lung → pulmonary vein → left heart → systemic circulation → brain.
Tid BitColon → portal → liver → IVC → lungs.
Q017 Block 1 Answer: E
DermatologyInfectious Disease

Itchy serpiginous burrows in webspaces

Vignette Summary
4M, 3-wk itching of hands/inner thighs, serpiginous epidermal tracks in webspaces, axillae, waist; vesicles at ends → scabies.
The Discriminator
Burrows in interdigital webs + intense itch (worse at night) = Sarcoptes scabiei.
Why the Answer is Correct
The female mite burrows into the stratum corneum, depositing eggs and feces. The host mounts a delayed-type hypersensitivity reaction to mite antigens after 2-6 weeks, producing pruritus and papules/vesicles. Linear burrows are pathognomonic; scrapings show mites/eggs/scybala.
Cognitive Demand
Morphological diagnosis from physical description of a skin lesion
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Serpiginous tracks in webspaces are so distinctive for scabies that this is a pattern recognition question, but students who don't know this description will guess chickenpox or Lyme by process of elimination.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Chickenpox. Vesicles in successive crops on trunk, fever. Not linear burrows.
Chickenpox (varicella). Produces a pruritic vesicular rash in multiple stages of healing simultaneously. Papules, vesicles, pustules, and crusts. Typically starting on the trunk and face before spreading to extremities. The rash is NOT described as serpiginous tracks or burrows. Vesicles are present here at the ends of some tracks, which is the mite and its eggs. Not varicella vesicles.
(B)
Ehrlichiosis. Tick-borne febrile illness, usually no rash. Wrong category.
Ehrlichiosis. Is a tick-borne bacterial infection causing fever, headache, myalgias, and a petechial or maculopapular rash on the trunk and extremities. It does not produce serpiginous tracks, webspace involvement, or the intensely pruritic burrowing pattern described here.
(C)
Lyme disease. Erythema migrans bull's-eye, not burrows.
Lyme disease's hallmark rash. Is erythema migrans. A single (or multiple) expanding annular rash with or without central clearing (bull's-eye pattern) at the tick bite site. It is not pruritic, not serpiginous, and does not occur in the webspaces between digits or around the waist.
(D)
Pediculosis. Lice cause itching but visible nits in hair, no burrows.
Pediculosis (lice infestation). Causes intense pruritus due to lice biting the skin and laying eggs (nits) on hair shafts. Pediculosis capitis affects the scalp, pediculosis pubis affects pubic hair. While intensely pruritic, it does not produce the linear serpiginous tracks in the webspaces of the digits. Those tracks are the burrows of Sarcoptes scabiei mites.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Scabies. Pathognomonic serpiginous burrows.
Costume Change
Reappears as: entire household with pruritus worse at night → scabies (nocturnal pruritus from mite activity is classic). Or: treatment question. Permethrin 5% cream from neck to toes, treat all household contacts simultaneously. Or: Norwegian (crusted) scabies in immunocompromised patient. Hyperkeratotic plaques, very contagious.
Variable Pivot
If the rash were on the scalp with nits visible on hair → pediculosis capitis (D). If multiple vesicles in different stages on trunk and face → varicella (A). If expanding annular rash after tick bite → Lyme disease (C). If petechial rash + fever + tick exposure → RMSF or ehrlichiosis.
Tid BitWebspace burrows + nocturnal itch = scabies; treat with permethrin.
Q018 Block 1 Answer: E
GIHematologyGallstone Pathophysiology

Pigment gallstones in sickle cell

Vignette Summary
18F sickle cell → biliary colic, gallstones on US → underlying mechanism.
The Discriminator
Sickle cell = chronic hemolysis → excess unconjugated bilirubin secreted in bile → calcium bilirubinate (pigment) stones.
Why the Answer is Correct
Chronic hemolysis from sickle cell disease releases hemoglobin which is catabolized to unconjugated bilirubin. Hepatic conjugation capacity is overwhelmed, raising bilirubin secretion into bile. Excess bilirubin precipitates with calcium to form calcium bilirubinate, the BLACK pigment stones characteristic of chronic hemolytic states (sickle cell disease, hereditary spherocytosis, thalassemia). Black stones form in the gallbladder and are radiopaque. Note: the beta-glucuronidase pathway produces a DIFFERENT stone type. Brown pigment stones, which form in the bile ducts from bacterial or parasitic infection (Clonorchis, bacterial cholangitis) when beta-glucuronidase deconjugates bilirubin in stagnant bile. Brown stones are not the sickle cell mechanism.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the specific biochemical pathway producing pigment stones in hemolytic anemia
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Fatty food trigger misdirect. Students anchor on the meal and jump to cholesterol stones. The underlying sickle cell disease is the essential context that changes the stone type entirely.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased hepatic lecithin secretion. Would promote cholesterol stones, not pigment. Wrong stone type.
Decreased hepatic secretion of lecithin. Would reduce the ability of bile to solubilize cholesterol, promoting cholesterol stone formation. Lecithin (phosphatidylcholine) is one of the three bile components (bile salts, lecithin, cholesterol) that must stay in balance. This mechanism produces cholesterol stones in the classic Fs patient. Fat, fertile, forty, female, fair. Sickle cell disease does not impair lecithin secretion.
(B)
Decreased bile salt reabsorption. Crohn/ileal resection physiology → cholesterol stones.
Decreased reabsorption of bile salts. Occurs when the terminal ileum is diseased or resected (classically Crohn's disease of the terminal ileum). Bile salts normally undergo enterohepatic recirculation via active transport at the terminal ileum. Reduced reabsorption depletes the bile salt pool, shifts the cholesterol/bile salt ratio toward stone formation, and produces cholesterol stones. Sickle cell disease does not damage the terminal ileum.
(C)
High cholesterol-to-bile-acid ratio. Cholesterol stones in obesity/estrogen, not sickle disease.
A high cholesterol-to-bile acid ratio in bile is the direct cause of cholesterol stone formation. This ratio is elevated in obesity, rapid weight loss, OCPs, pregnancy, and clofibrate use. Cholesterol stones are radiolucent and yellow. Sickle cell disease does not increase cholesterol production or decrease bile acid synthesis.
(D)
Parasites secreting β-glucuronidase. Clonorchis-related brown pigment stones; not typical for SCD.
Parasites secreting beta-glucuronidase (Clonorchis sinensis, Ascaris) deconjugate bilirubin glucuronide in bile, releasing free bilirubin that precipitates as calcium bilirubinate. Brown pigment stones. This is the correct stone type (pigment) but the wrong mechanism for this patient. Brown stones form in the biliary ducts from bacterial/parasitic infection and stasis, not from systemic hemolysis.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Overload of unconjugated bilirubin. Chronic hemolysis → black pigment stones.
Costume Change
Reappears as: any hemolytic anemia + RUQ pain → pigment (black) gallstones. Hereditary spherocytosis is another common NBME setup. Or: what type of bilirubin is elevated in hemolysis? → Unconjugated (indirect). Or: what type of gallstones are radiopaque? → Pigment stones (calcium bilirubinate = radiopaque). Cholesterol stones are radiolucent.
Variable Pivot
If obese multiparous woman + fatty food trigger → cholesterol stones (different mechanism. Lithogenic bile). If Crohn's disease of terminal ileum → cholesterol stones from bile salt depletion. If recurrent cholangitis + Asian patient → brown pigment stones from bacterial deconjugation. If rapid hemolysis → black pigment stones from bilirubin overload.
Tid BitHemolyzers make BLACK pigment stones (calcium bilirubinate from bilirubin overload). Ileum-losers make cholesterol stones. Beta-glucuronidase from biliary infection makes BROWN stones in the bile ducts. Three different mechanisms, three different stones.
Q019 Block 1 Answer: D
EndocrinologyMedical Genetics

MEN2A. Medullary thyroid carcinoma

Vignette Summary
34M, prior pheochromocytoma, mother with parathyroid tumor → neck mass biopsy = neuroendocrine tumor of parafollicular (C) cells = medullary thyroid carcinoma → MEN2A.
The Discriminator
MEN2A = MTC + pheochromocytoma + parathyroid hyperplasia, caused by activating germline mutation of the RET proto-oncogene.
Why the Answer is Correct
RET encodes a receptor tyrosine kinase. Gain-of-function point mutations cause ligand-independent dimerization and constitutive signaling, driving proliferation of neural-crest-derived endocrine cells (C cells, chromaffin cells, parathyroid). A single activated allele is sufficient. Classic proto- oncogene behavior.
Cognitive Demand
Gene type classification in MEN2A syndrome
NBME Trap Fingerprint
MEN1 vs MEN2 gene type confusion. MEN1 uses a tumor suppressor (MEN1 gene), MEN2 uses a proto-oncogene (RET). Students who know the wrong MEN syndrome will pick tumor suppressor.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Cell cycle regulation gene. E.g., RB, p53. These are tumor suppressors and require two hits. Wrong mechanism.
Cell cycle regulation genes include CDK4, cyclin D1, and RB1. Mutations in these produce retinoblastoma (RB1 loss. Tumor suppressor), melanoma (CDKN2A), and mantle cell lymphoma (cyclin D1 overexpression). They are not the mechanism for hereditary endocrine tumor syndromes like MEN2A.
(B)
DNA mismatch repair gene. Lynch syndrome (MLH1/ MSH2); colon/endometrial cancers. Wrong syndrome.
DNA mismatch repair genes (MLH1, MSH2, MSH6, PMS2) cause Lynch syndrome (hereditary non-polyposis colorectal cancer), characterized by colorectal cancer, endometrial cancer, and other Lynch-associated tumors. Microsatellite instability is the hallmark. This mechanism does not drive medullary thyroid carcinoma or pheochromocytoma.
(C)
Metastasis suppressor gene. Rarely tested; not the MEN2A driver.
Metastasis suppressor genes (nm23, KAI1) inhibit the spread of established tumors. Loss of metastasis suppressors contributes to distant spread but is not the germline mutation driving hereditary tumor syndromes. MEN2A is caused by a germline activating mutation, not a loss of a metastasis suppressor.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Proto-oncogene. Activating RET mutation.
(E)
Tumor suppressor gene. MEN1 (menin) follows two-hit logic; this is MEN2.
Tumor suppressor genes require two-hit inactivation (Knudson hypothesis). MEN1 is caused by inactivation of the MEN1 tumor suppressor on chromosome 11q13. MEN1 syndrome = parathyroid + pituitary + pancreatic tumors. This patient has medullary thyroid carcinoma + pheochromocytoma + parathyroid disease = MEN2A, which is driven by an activating (gain-of-function) mutation in the RET proto-oncogene, not loss of a tumor suppressor.
Costume Change
Reappears as: parafollicular cell tumor (calcitonin) + pheochromocytoma → think MEN2, order RET genetic testing. Or: medullary thyroid carcinoma in a young patient → always ask for family history and test for RET. Or: MEN1 vs MEN2 classification. MEN1 = parathyroid + pituitary + pancreas; MEN2A = medullary thyroid + pheo + parathyroid; MEN2B = medullary thyroid + pheo + mucosal neuromas.
Variable Pivot
If tumors are parathyroid + pituitary + pancreatic → MEN1 → MEN1 tumor suppressor gene (E). If medullary thyroid + pheo + mucosal neuromas + marfanoid habitus → MEN2B → RET proto-oncogene (D), different codon than MEN2A. If the question asks about which gene type explains an activating germline mutation → proto-oncogene (D).
Tid BitMEN2 = RET, proto-oncogene; MEN1 = menin, tumor suppressor.
Q020 Block 1 Answer: A
EndocrinologyPediatrics

Precocious puberty

Vignette Summary
6F, breast buds + pubic hair, vaginal bleeding → elevated estradiol (185 pg/mL), elevated FSH (15 mIU/mL, N<6.7), elevated LH (3.0 mIU/mL, N<0.2), normal ACTH/TSH → identify the cause of precocious puberty.
The Discriminator
Both LH and FSH are elevated. The pituitary is driving this. Elevated gonadotropins in a prepubertal girl = central (gonadotropin-dependent) precocious puberty = premature activation of the hypothalamic GnRH pulse generator.
Why the Answer is Correct
In central precocious puberty, neurons in the hypothalamus begin firing pulsatile GnRH prematurely. This drives pituitary FSH and LH secretion, which in turn stimulates ovarian estrogen and testosterone production. The key discriminator is that LH is elevated (3.0 vs N<0.2). The hypothalamic-pituitary axis is ON. In peripheral/autonomous causes (McCune-Albright, exogenous steroids), LH and FSH are suppressed by negative feedback from autonomous sex steroid production.
Cognitive Demand
Classify precocious puberty as central vs peripheral using gonadotropin levels
NBME Trap Fingerprint
LH misread. This was the critical lab error in the original guide. LH is 3.0 mIU/mL against a normal of <0.2. It is 15 times the upper limit of normal. Peripheral precocious puberty (the alternative) requires SUPPRESSED LH.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Central activation of neurons. CORRECT. Elevated LH confirms the hypothalamic-pituitary axis is firing. Central precocious puberty is the diagnosis.
(B)
Ectopic prolactin secretion. Causes galactorrhea and amenorrhea; does not drive FSH/LH or produce this picture.
Ectopic prolactin secretion from a prolactinoma. Causes hyperprolactinemia, which suppresses GnRH pulsatility → low LH and FSH → hypogonadism, galactorrhea, and amenorrhea in adults. It does not drive gonadotropin release or cause precocious puberty. In children, excess prolactin would be expected to delay puberty by suppressing the HPG axis, not accelerate it.
(C)
Exogenous sex steroid secretion. Would suppress LH/FSH via negative feedback. LH is elevated here, not suppressed. Reverse mechanism.
Exogenous sex steroid administration (OCPs, anabolic steroids, topical estrogen products) causes peripheral precocious puberty by providing sex steroids directly to peripheral tissues. Negative feedback suppresses LH and FSH. This patient has elevated LH (3.0, N<0.2) and elevated FSH (15, N<6.7). The pituitary is ON, not suppressed. Exogenous sex steroids suppress the axis; central activation elevates it.
(D)
Germline GNAS activating mutation (McCune-Albright). Causes autonomous ovarian estrogen secretion with SUPPRESSED gonadotropins. LH here is elevated, ruling this out completely.
Germline GNAS activating mutation (McCune-Albright syndrome). Produces autonomous cAMP-driven hormone production in multiple endocrine glands. Ovaries producing estrogen without LH stimulation, adrenal cortex producing cortisol without ACTH, thyroid producing T4 without TSH. Because ovarian estrogen is produced autonomously, it suppresses LH and FSH via negative feedback. LH would be LOW or undetectable. This patient has elevated LH. This answer requires suppressed LH, and we have the opposite.
(E)
21-Hydroxylase deficiency. Causes congenital adrenal hyperplasia with elevated androgens and ACTH. ACTH is normal here. Wrong mechanism.
21-Hydroxylase deficiency (congenital adrenal hyperplasia) impairs cortisol synthesis, causing ACTH hypersecretion that drives excess androgen production. It presents with ambiguous genitalia (virilization in 46,XX), salt-wasting crisis, elevated ACTH, elevated 17-hydroxyprogesterone, and elevated androgens. ACTH in this patient is 18 pg/mL. Completely normal. A normal ACTH rules out CAH from 21-hydroxylase deficiency.
Costume Change
Reappears as: LH + FSH elevated in prepubertal child → central precocious puberty → GnRH agonist therapy (paradoxically suppresses HPG axis via downregulation). Or: the distinction between central and peripheral precocious puberty by gonadotropin levels is a fundamental framework for all HPG axis questions.
Variable Pivot
If LH and FSH were suppressed → peripheral/autonomous cause: think McCune-Albright (GNAS), exogenous steroids, ovarian/adrenal tumor. If ACTH is elevated → CAH (21-hydroxylase deficiency). If TSH is elevated → hypothyroidism-associated precocious puberty (TSH cross-reacts with FSH receptor). If unilateral adrenal mass + elevated DHEA-S → adrenal tumor.
Tid BitLH elevated = central (pituitary on). LH suppressed = peripheral (autonomous). One lab value splits the entire precocious puberty DDx.
Q021 Block 2 Answer: E
EndocrinologyGenetics

Klinefelter syndrome

Vignette Summary
16-year-old boy → delayed puberty, tall with long extremities → small testes, gynecomastia → high FSH/LH, low testosterone → underlying cause?
The Discriminator
Hypergonadotropic hypogonadism (FSH 45, LH 34, T 45) + tall stature + gynecomastia = 47,XXY.
Why the Answer is Correct
Klinefelter syndrome results from meiotic nondisjunction of the sex chromosomes (most often maternal meiosis I), producing a 47,XXY karyotype. The extra X causes seminiferous tubule dysgenesis and Leydig cell dysfunction → low testosterone, loss of negative feedback → high FSH/LH. Low T:estrogen ratio drives gynecomastia; lack of epiphyseal closure produces tall stature with long extremities.
Cognitive Demand
Karyotype-based diagnosis in delayed puberty with elevated gonadotropins
NBME Trap Fingerprint
High FSH/LH in a teenage boy signals primary gonadal failure. The axis is trying but the gonads aren't responding. The phenotype (tall, gynecomastia, small testes) plus this hormonal pattern = Klinefelter.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Constitutional delay. Adjacent disease; would give LOW FSH/LH (delayed HPG axis), not sky-high gonadotropins.
Constitutional delay. Is the most common cause of delayed puberty in boys and produces LOW gonadotropins as the axis is simply slow to activate. This boy has FSH of 45 and LH of 34. Dramatically elevated, indicating the pituitary is firing maximally but the testes are not responding. Constitutional delay cannot explain elevated gonadotropins.
(B)
GnRH neuron hypoplasia (Kallmann). Reverse mechanism; hypogonadotropic, often with anosmia.
Hypoplasia of hypothalamic GnRH neurons (Kallmann syndrome). Causes hypogonadotropic hypogonadism. LOW LH and FSH with associated anosmia. The pituitary never receives adequate GnRH stimulation. This patient has markedly elevated gonadotropins, the opposite finding.
(C)
Inactivating FSH-receptor mutation. Half- truth; would elevate FSH but T is preserved or only mildly low in males, no tall/eunuchoid habitus pattern fits Klinefelter better.
An inactivating FSH receptor mutation would prevent FSH from signaling despite elevated levels. Consistent with elevated gonadotropins AND failure of spermatogenesis/testicular development. However, the clinical phenotype here (tall stature, long limbs, gynecomastia, small testes) combined with elevated LH AND FSH specifically describes Klinefelter. The FSH receptor mutation wouldn't explain LH elevation or the full phenotype.
(D)
21-hydroxylase mutation (CAH). Wrong territory; causes virilization or salt- wasting, not eunuchoid male.
21-Hydroxylase gene mutation causes CAH. Early virilization, early pubic hair, premature epiphyseal closure (SHORT stature), and elevated androgens. This boy is tall (90th percentile) with ABSENT secondary sexual characteristics. CAH is the opposite phenotype.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Nondisjunction of X during meiosis. Produces 47,XXY Klinefelter.
Costume Change
Reappears as: infertility workup in adult male → azoospermia + small firm testes + elevated FSH/LH → Klinefelter. Or: what is the karyotype in Klinefelter? → 47,XXY. Or: how does Klinefelter cause gynecomastia? → Excess peripheral aromatization of androgen to estrogen due to gonadal dysgenesis.
Variable Pivot
