If low FSH/LH with anosmia → Kallmann syndrome (B). If low FSH/LH without anosmia → constitutional delay (A) or another hypogonadotropic cause. If short stature + early pubic hair + elevated 17-OHP → CAH (D).
Tid BitTall, gynecomastia, tiny testes, high FSH = "extra X". Think nondisjunction.
Q022 Block 2 Answer: B
EndocrinologyNeonatology

Classic salt-wasting CAH (21-hydroxylase deficiency)

Vignette Summary
Neonate → poor feeding, lethargy → ambiguous genitalia → hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, low bicarb, low glucose → drug target?
The Discriminator
Salt-wasting crisis + virilized genitalia = 21-hydroxylase deficiency → no aldosterone, no cortisol.
Why the Answer is Correct
21-hydroxylase deficiency blocks synthesis of both aldosterone and cortisol; precursors shunt into androgens, virilizing 46,XX or causing ambiguity. Treatment requires both glucocorticoid replacement and a mineralocorticoid. Fludrocortisone. Which targets the aldosterone (mineralocorticoid) receptor in the renal collecting duct to retain Na+ and excrete K+/H+. This corrects the hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, and metabolic acidosis.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the primary hormone deficiency in a salt-wasting crisis + ambiguous genitalia
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The electrolytes tell the story. Hyponatremia + hyperkalemia + metabolic acidosis in a newborn with ambiguous genitalia = CAH with aldosterone deficiency → treat with mineralocorticoid targeting the aldosterone receptor.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
ACTH receptor. Wrong direction; ACTH is already high. No drug targets ACTH receptors here.
ACTH receptor. Is the target of ACTH itself. Therapy targeting ACTH receptors would increase cortisol production. But this patient lacks the enzyme (21-hydroxylase) to convert precursors to cortisol regardless of receptor stimulation. The problem is enzymatic, not receptor-level.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Aldosterone receptor. Fludrocortisone replaces missing mineralocorticoid activity.
(C)
Androgen receptor. Reverse mechanism; androgens are excess but blocking them does not fix salt-wasting.
Androgen receptor blockade. Would reduce virilization but does not address the life-threatening salt-wasting crisis or hypoglycemia. The immediate emergency is mineralocorticoid deficiency causing hyponatremia and hyperkalemia, not androgen excess.
(D)
GnRH receptor. Wrong territory; this is adrenal disease, not gonadal.
GnRH receptor therapy. Has no role in acute CAH management. GnRH agonists are used for central precocious puberty. The emergency here is adrenal, not gonadal.
(E)
GH receptor. Unrelated.
Growth hormone receptor targeting addresses linear growth, which is a long-term consideration in CAH but not the acute emergency. The child is presenting with a salt-wasting crisis requiring immediate hormone replacement.
Costume Change
Reappears as: newborn with ambiguous genitalia + hyperkalemia + hyponatremia → CAH → 21-hydroxylase deficiency → give hydrocortisone (cortisol) + fludrocortisone (aldosterone). Or: what is the screening test? → 17-hydroxyprogesterone level. Or: which sex chromosome? → 46,XX female virilized by androgens.
Variable Pivot
If the electrolytes were normal → non-salt-wasting CAH (simple virilizing form). If the karyotype came back 46,XY with ambiguous genitalia + normal androgens → androgen insensitivity. If hypoglycemia + normal electrolytes → cortisol deficiency without mineralocorticoid involvement.
Tid BitSalt-wasting CAH = give hydrocortisone AND fludrocortisone. Feed the missing mineralocorticoid receptor.
Q023 Block 2 Answer: C
NephrologyUremia

Indications for emergent dialysis

Vignette Summary
59-year-old with diabetic CKD → BUN 65, Cr 4.2 → which new finding mandates immediate HD?
The Discriminator
Pericardial friction rub = uremic pericarditis = absolute indication for urgent dialysis.
Why the Answer is Correct
"AEIOU" indications for emergent hemodialysis: Acidosis, Electrolytes (hyperkalemia), Ingestions, Overload (refractory pulmonary edema), Uremia. Uremic pericarditis manifests as a friction rub and risks hemorrhagic effusion/tamponade; it is a hard indication for prompt dialysis regardless of BUN level.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the EMERGENT indication for dialysis (not just an indication, but the one requiring immediate initiation)
NBME Trap Fingerprint
All options might theoretically be managed eventually, but NBME tests which complication of uremia cannot wait and requires same-day dialysis. Pericardial friction rub = uremic pericarditis = emergency.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Worsening anemia. Treat with EPO/iron, not dialysis.
Anemia of chronic kidney disease. Is managed with EPO and iron supplementation. It develops gradually and rarely requires emergent dialysis unless accompanied by cardiovascular compromise. Anemia alone is not an emergency indication for dialysis.
(B)
Moderate pedal edema. Diuretics; only refractory volume overload with pulmonary edema mandates HD.
Moderate pedal edema. Is managed with fluid restriction and loop diuretics initially. Volume overload becomes an indication for dialysis when refractory to diuretics or causing pulmonary edema. But "moderate" edema is not the threshold.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Pericardial friction rub. Uremic pericarditis = emergent dialysis.
(D)
Persistent HTN. Adjust antihypertensives; not an HD indication.
Persistent hypertension despite medications. Is a long-term complication of CKD requiring ongoing management. It may accelerate disease progression and eventually contribute to the need for dialysis, but it is not an acute emergency indication triggering same-day dialysis initiation.
(E)
Recurrent hypoglycemia. Adjust insulin/oral agents; not a dialysis criterion.
Recurrent hypoglycemia in a diabetic patient on dialysis actually improves as kidney function declines because the kidney normally degrades insulin. Renal failure prolongs insulin half-life, so less insulin is needed. This is not an indication for emergent dialysis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: the AEIOU mnemonic for emergent dialysis. Acidosis (refractory), Electrolytes (hyperkalemia refractory to treatment), Ingestion (toxic), Overload (pulmonary edema refractory), Uremia (encephalopathy, pericarditis, bleeding). Pericarditis is the classic NBME tested emergency indication.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had confusion + asterixis → uremic encephalopathy (emergent). If K+ > 6.5 despite medical management → emergent. If metabolic acidosis pH < 7.1 refractory to bicarbonate → emergent. If pulmonary edema not responding to diuretics → emergent.
Tid BitAEIOU. Uremic pericarditis is the "U" you cannot ignore.
Q024 Block 2 Answer: C
CardiologyCardiac Physiology

Acute MI on PV loop

Vignette Summary
65-year-old → 10 min of crushing chest pain, JVD, crackles → anterolateral STEMI with positive troponin → which PV-loop transition reflects the first 10 seconds?
The Discriminator
Acute ischemia drops contractility → ESPVR shifts down/right → larger end-systolic volume, smaller stroke volume.
Why the Answer is Correct
During an acute MI, infarcted myocardium loses contractile function. On the pressure-volume loop this manifests as decreased slope of the end-systolic pressure-volume relationship: at the same preload and afterload, the ventricle cannot empty as well, so end-systolic volume rises, stroke volume falls, and peak systolic pressure drops. Point W (baseline) shifts to Z (depressed ESPVR. Lower pressure, larger ESV). The acute drop also raises LVEDP, producing the pulmonary congestion and JVD seen here.
Cognitive Demand
PV loop interpretation: identify the transition that represents acute loss of contractility in the first 10 seconds of MI
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Preload vs contractility confusion. Acute MI drops CONTRACTILITY (ESPVR shifts down), not preload. The transition must show a downward shift of the operating point, not movement along the same curve.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
W→X. Increased contractility; opposite of ischemia.
(B)
W→Y. Increased preload only; doesn't capture the loss of contractility.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
W→Z. Acute ischemia = decreased contractility, depressed ESPVR.
(D)
X→W, (E) X→Y, (F) X→Z, (G) Z→W, (H) Z→X, (I) Z→Y. All start from a non-baseline state or move in wrong directions.
Costume Change
Reappears as: on the Starling curve, which direction represents contractility change vs preload change? → Vertical shift of the curve = contractility change. Moving along the curve = preload change. Also: what happens to stroke volume, end-systolic volume, and cardiac output in acute MI? → All worsen as ESPVR shifts down and to the right.
Variable Pivot
If the question asked about 2 hours after MI (compensatory response) → increased sympathetic tone would try to restore contractility. If the question asked about vasodilator therapy → decreased afterload shifts operating point to lower ESV with improved SV on same ESPVR.
Tid BitIschemia = a sick, lazy ventricle. ESPVR slope falls, ESV climbs.
Q025 Block 2 Answer: C
NeurologyPharmacology

Parkinson adjunct. MAO-B inhibitor

Vignette Summary
PD on carbidopa-levodopa → persistent "freezing" and rigidity → adjunct enzyme inhibitor?
The Discriminator
"Freezing"/wearing-off on L-DOPA → add selegiline/rasagiline (MAO-B inhibitor) to prolong dopamine action.
Why the Answer is Correct
MAO-B preferentially degrades dopamine in the CNS. Selegiline/rasagiline selectively inhibit MAO-B, raising synaptic dopamine and smoothing out "off"/freezing episodes. Carbidopa already blocks peripheral aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase; MAO-B inhibition adds central, downstream protection of dopamine.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the correct enzyme target for an adjunct therapy when carbidopa-levodopa is insufficient
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Decarboxylase anchoring. Carbidopa already inhibits aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase. Adding another AADC inhibitor does nothing. The adjunct must target a DIFFERENT enzyme in the dopamine metabolism pathway.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase. That's carbidopa; already on board.
Aromatic L-amino acid decarboxylase (AADC). Converts L-DOPA to dopamine. Carbidopa, already present in the patient's regimen, is a peripheral AADC inhibitor. Adding another AADC inhibitor provides no additional benefit. This enzyme is already blocked.
(B)
Dopamine β-hydroxylase. Converts DA→NE; blocking it depletes norepinephrine, not helpful for PD.
Dopamine beta-hydroxylase. Converts dopamine to norepinephrine. Blocking it would reduce NE synthesis in the periphery. This is not a therapeutic target in Parkinson disease and would not address the inadequate dopamine delivery to the striatum.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Monoamine oxidase B. Selegiline/rasagiline reduce DA breakdown.
(D)
PNMT. NE→Epi conversion (adrenal); irrelevant to PD.
Phenylethanolamine N-methyltransferase (PNMT). Converts norepinephrine to epinephrine in the adrenal medulla. This enzyme has no role in Parkinson pharmacotherapy.
(E)
Tyrosine hydroxylase. Rate-limiting step in DA synthesis; inhibiting it worsens PD.
Tyrosine hydroxylase. Is the rate-limiting enzyme for dopamine synthesis, converting tyrosine to L-DOPA. Inhibiting it would REDUCE dopamine synthesis. The opposite of the therapeutic goal in Parkinson disease.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Parkinson patient on carbidopa-levodopa with wearing-off → add MAO-B inhibitor (selegiline, rasagiline) or COMT inhibitor (entacapone). Or: which enzyme degrades dopamine in the synapse? → MAO-B and COMT. Or: which drugs are used for freezing episodes in Parkinson? → MAO-B inhibitors, dopamine agonists (pramipexole, ropinirole), COMT inhibitors.
Variable Pivot
If asking about peripheral DOPA breakdown → COMT inhibitor (entacapone). If asking about synaptic dopamine degradation → MAO-B inhibitor (selegiline). If asking about nausea from levodopa → carbidopa prevents peripheral conversion. If patient has hallucinations from dopaminergic medications → reduce dose or add quetiapine (low EPS risk).
Tid Bit"Off" episodes on L-DOPA. Add an MAO-B inhibitor to keep dopamine on the field longer.
Q026 Block 2 Answer: E
AnatomySurgery

Penetrating trauma at left 10th ICS

Vignette Summary
GSW to back at left 10th intercostal space → hypotensive, tachycardic, pale → CT shows intra- abdominal injury → which organ?
The Discriminator
Left posterior 9th-11th ribs overlie the spleen.
Why the Answer is Correct
The spleen sits in the left upper quadrant under ribs 9-11, posterolaterally. Penetrating injury at the left 10th ICS most commonly lacerates the spleen, which is the most commonly injured solid organ in left-sided thoracoabdominal trauma. Profuse intraperitoneal hemorrhage explains the hemorrhagic shock (HR 120, narrow pulse pressure, pale/clammy skin).
Cognitive Demand
Anatomical localization of a bullet from CT scan + entry wound description
NBME Trap Fingerprint
CT scan interpretation + anatomical position. Left posterior 10th intercostal space is below the diaphragm on the left. The large homogeneous left-sided organ in the upper abdomen posterior to the stomach is the spleen.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Adrenal gland. Retroperitoneal, small target; unlikely to cause exsanguinating shock alone.
The adrenal gland is superior to the kidney, small, and located retroperitoneally. A bullet at the 10th intercostal space posteriorly on the left would not reach the adrenal gland without traversing other structures first. The adrenal gland is also not large enough to be the primary structure seen in the CT mass effect described.
(B)
Colon. Possible but doesn't fit the acute shock picture as cleanly; descending colon is more lateral/anterior.
The colon (splenic flexure) is present in the left upper quadrant but is anterior and medial relative to the spleen. The CT findings and the posterior entry wound trajectory make the colon less likely than the spleen as the primary injured structure.
(C)
Kidney. Retroperitoneal at T12-L3; would bleed into Gerota fascia, less likely to drop BP so fast.
The left kidney sits retroperitoneally in the left flank, and a posterior left lower chest wound could reach it. However, the 10th intercostal space on the left is superior to the typical level of the kidney, and the CT hypodensity on the left is more consistent with the spleen at this level.
(D)
Lung. At the 10th ICS the pleura/diaphragm can be crossed, but CT showing abdominal injury localizes below the diaphragm.
The lung extends down to approximately the 9th-10th rib during expiration. While a 10th intercostal wound on the left could injure the lung, the CT scan of the ABDOMEN showing a mass places the bullet in an abdominal organ. Lung injury would show pneumothorax or hemothorax on chest imaging.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Spleen. Classic left lower thoracoabdominal injury.
Costume Change
Reappears as: left flank trauma + peritoneal signs → splenic injury → CT scan showing perisplenic hematoma. Or: most common organ injured in blunt abdominal trauma → spleen (left), liver (right). Or: non-operative management of splenic laceration. Grades I-III in hemodynamically stable patients.
Variable Pivot
If the entry wound were right posterior and similar level → hepatic injury or right kidney. If the patient had a distended abdomen with free fluid → splenic rupture with hemorrhage into peritoneum. If the O2 sat were <90% → consider hemopneumothorax (lung involved).
Tid BitLeft lower ribs broken or pierced. Assume the spleen until proven otherwise.
Q027 Block 2 Answer: B
PsychiatryNeurologyToxicology

Neuroleptic malignant syndrome

Vignette Summary
Psych inpatient on olanzapine → fever 40.3°C, autonomic instability, confusion → which exam finding?
The Discriminator
Antipsychotic + hyperthermia + autonomic dysregulation = NMS → "lead-pipe" rigidity.
Why the Answer is Correct
NMS results from central D2 dopamine receptor blockade by antipsychotics. Loss of dopaminergic tone in the nigrostriatum and hypothalamus produces severe muscle rigidity ("lead-pipe"), hyperthermia, autonomic instability, and altered mental status. Rhabdomyolysis with elevated CK is typical.
Cognitive Demand
Distinguish neuroleptic malignant syndrome from serotonin syndrome and alcohol withdrawal
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Psychiatric patient + fever + antipsychotic = NMS until proven otherwise. The combination of olanzapine initiation + fever + rigidity (not hyperreflexia) is the specific pattern.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Hyperreflexia. Feature of serotonin syndrome (SSRIs/MAOIs), not NMS.
Hyperreflexia. Is the cardinal finding of SEROTONIN SYNDROME, not NMS. Serotonin syndrome is caused by excess serotonergic activity (SSRI + MAOI, tramadol + SSRI, linezolid + SSRI). It presents with the triad of altered mental status, autonomic instability, and neuromuscular abnormalities (clonus, hyperreflexia, myoclonus). This patient is on olanzapine (an antipsychotic with NO serotonergic mechanism) and clonazepam. Not a serotonergic drug combination.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Muscle rigidity. Lead-pipe rigidity is the hallmark.
(C)
Mydriasis. Anticholinergic toxicity or serotonin syndrome.
Mydriasis (pupil dilation). Occurs in sympathomimetic toxidrome (cocaine, amphetamine) and anticholinergic toxidrome, not in NMS. NMS actually produces normal-sized or miotic pupils in some cases. Serotonin syndrome can produce mydriasis, but the mechanism here (antipsychotic initiation) points to NMS.
(D)
Petechial rash. Meningococcemia or fat embolism; not NMS.
Petechial rash. Suggests meningococcemia (Neisseria meningitidis), which can cause high fever and hemodynamic collapse. However, petechiae are not associated with NMS, and this patient has a clear pharmacological trigger (olanzapine).
(E)
Unilateral hemiparesis. Suggests stroke; not antipsychotic toxicity.
Unilateral hemiparesis. Indicates focal neurological disease. Stroke, brain mass, or Todd's paralysis. This patient has bilateral rigidity (a diffuse neuromuscular finding) and a drug-related trigger. Stroke does not explain the relationship to antipsychotic initiation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: NMS vs serotonin syndrome comparison. NMS = antipsychotic trigger, "lead pipe" rigidity, bradyreflexia, fever, autonomic instability (slow onset over days). SS = serotonergic trigger, clonus/hyperreflexia, fever, autonomic instability (rapid onset hours). Treatment: NMS → stop antipsychotic, dantrolene, bromocriptine. SS → stop serotonergic drug, cyproheptadine.
Variable Pivot
If started on an SSRI + tramadol and developed tremor + clonus + fever → serotonin syndrome. If alcoholic patient with tremors + tachycardia + hypertension + seizures → alcohol withdrawal (not fever from NMS). If started on antipsychotic + "lead pipe" rigidity + fever + elevated CK → NMS (B).
Tid BitLead-pipe + fever + neuroleptic = NMS. Clonus + diarrhea + serotonergic drug = serotonin syndrome.
Q028 Block 2 Answer: E
OncologyUrology

Urothelial (transitional cell) carcinoma

Vignette Summary
68-year-old → painless hematuria → naphthylamine exposure + heavy smoker → renal pelvis mass → tumor type?
The Discriminator
Naphthylamine + smoking + renal pelvis = urothelial carcinoma.
Why the Answer is Correct
Aromatic amines (β-naphthylamine, benzidine) and cigarette smoke are classic urothelial carcinogens. Hepatic activation produces electrophilic metabolites concentrated in urine that damage urothelial DNA throughout the urinary tract. The renal pelvis, ureter, and bladder are all lined by urothelium; "field defect" tumors are typical. Painless hematuria is the hallmark presentation.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most likely cancer type in a patient with specific occupational and lifestyle risk factors + renal pelvis mass
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The pelvicalyceal location (NOT renal parenchyma) + occupational exposure to naphthylamine + smoking history points to urothelial (transitional cell) carcinoma, not renal cell carcinoma.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Angiomyolipoma. Benign tumor associated with tuberous sclerosis; fat- density on CT; not in renal pelvis.
Angiomyolipoma. Is a benign hamartoma of the kidney composed of blood vessels, smooth muscle, and fat. It is classically associated with tuberous sclerosis and appears on CT as a fat-containing renal mass. It does not occur in the renal pelvis and is not malignant.
(B)
Metastatic melanoma. No primary; rare in renal pelvis.
Metastatic melanoma to the kidney. Is possible but uncommon. Melanoma metastases are typically multiple, bilateral, and found in the context of known primary melanoma. This patient has a primary renal mass, not a metastatic deposit.
(C)
Nephroblastoma (Wilms). Child \<5 yrs, not a 68-year-old.
Nephroblastoma (Wilms tumor). Is the most common renal tumor of CHILDREN (peak age 3-4 years). This is a 68-year-old man. The demographic alone eliminates this answer.
(D)
Oncocytoma. Benign cortical mass with central scar; arises from collecting duct cells, not pelvis urothelium.
Oncocytoma. Is a benign renal tumor composed of large eosinophilic cells. It classically shows a central "stellate scar" on imaging and does not occur in the renal pelvis. It is not associated with occupational chemical exposures.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Urothelial carcinoma. Pee-SAC: Phenacetin, Smoking, Aniline dyes, Cyclophosphamide.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which carcinogens cause bladder/urothelial cancer? → Aniline dyes, naphthylamine, benzidine (industrial exposure) + smoking (most common overall cause). Or: painless hematuria → bladder cancer or urothelial carcinoma until proven otherwise. Or: what is the treatment? → TURBT for superficial, cystectomy for invasive, BCG immunotherapy for superficial recurrence.
Variable Pivot
If the mass were in the renal parenchyma of an older man with polycythemia and hypercalcemia → renal cell carcinoma (clear cell, VHL mutation). If the patient were a child with flank mass and hypertension → Wilms tumor. If fat-containing mass on CT → angiomyolipoma.
Tid Bit"Painless hematuria + dye/rubber industry". Urothelial carcinoma until proven otherwise.
Q029 Block 2 Answer: A
DermatologyInternal Medicine

Rosacea

Vignette Summary
37-year-old woman → episodic facial flushing, burning, central erythema → drinks red wine → next history question?
The Discriminator
Central facial flushing with triggers = rosacea; alcohol (especially red wine) is a classic trigger.
Why the Answer is Correct
Rosacea is a chronic vasomotor/inflammatory facial dermatosis. Known triggers include heat, sun, spicy food, stress, and alcohol. Red wine in particular. Asking about alcohol's effect refines the diagnosis and helps target avoidance counseling. Steroids actually worsen long-standing rosacea, fitting her ambiguous response to hydrocortisone.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most relevant additional history for a patient with episodic facial flushing
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Type 1 DM anchor. The diabetes is there to distract. The rash description (episodic, stress-triggered, facial flushing/redness, burning) is classic rosacea, and the most important additional history for rosacea is alcohol trigger.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Effect of alcohol on the condition. Confirms a classic rosacea trigger.
(B)
Excessive sweating. Pheochromocytoma/menopause; doesn't fit central facial erythema.
Excessive sweating. Would be relevant for hyperhidrosis or autonomic dysfunction. Sweating is not a classic feature of rosacea. Asking about sweating would be more appropriate if considering systemic causes of flushing like carcinoid syndrome (which also causes diarrhea and wheezing).
(C)
Family history of adenomas. MEN/carcinoid flushing, but no GI symptoms or diarrhea here.
Family history of adenomas. Would be relevant if considering multiple endocrine neoplasia, hereditary cancer syndromes, or hyperparathyroidism. The episodic facial flushing in this context does not warrant adenoma screening history as the primary next question.
(D)
Numbness of fingers and toes. Diabetic neuropathy concern, but not relevant to the rash.
Numbness of fingers and toes. Would be relevant for evaluating peripheral neuropathy. Common in Type 1 DM. While this is important in the overall care of this patient, it does not address the specific dermatological complaint of episodic facial flushing.
(E)
Poor wound healing. Vitamin C/Cushing/diabetes complication; doesn't address rash etiology.
Poor healing of skin lacerations. Would be relevant for evaluating vasculopathy or immune dysfunction in the context of DM. Again, this is not the most specific history for the presenting rosacea-like condition.
Costume Change
Reappears as: triggers for rosacea flares → alcohol (especially red wine), spicy food, heat, sun, stress. Or: rosacea subtypes. Erythematotelangiectatic (flushing + telangiectasias), papulopustular (acne-like), phymatous (rhinophyma = nose thickening in men), ocular. Treatment: topical metronidazole, azelaic acid, brimonidine for erythema.
Variable Pivot
If facial flushing + watery diarrhea + wheezing → carcinoid syndrome (5-HT, measure 5-HIAA in urine). If flushing + hypertension + headache + diaphoresis → pheochromocytoma (catecholamines). If premenopausal woman + hot flashes without rash → menopause evaluation (FSH level).
Tid BitCentral face, flushing with wine and stress, worsens with topical steroids. Rosacea.
Q030 Block 2 Answer: B
EpidemiologyPublic Health

Unvaccinated child who stays healthy

Vignette Summary
5-year-old, never vaccinated, never sick → best explanation?
The Discriminator
A small unvaccinated minority is protected by vaccinated peers. Herd immunity.
Why the Answer is Correct
Herd immunity occurs when a sufficient proportion of the population is immune (via vaccination or prior infection) that transmission chains break, indirectly protecting the unvaccinated. This is the standard public-health explanation for why individual unvaccinated children frequently avoid disease.
Cognitive Demand
Explain why an unvaccinated child has not developed vaccine-preventable disease
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Herd immunity vs eradication. Community eradication means the disease is gone from that area completely; herd immunity means enough people are vaccinated that transmission chains break even for unvaccinated individuals. These are different concepts and only one applies here.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Community eradication. Only smallpox is eradicated; measles, pertussis, etc. Still circulate.
Community eradication means the disease. Has been permanently eliminated from that population and can no longer circulate. Like smallpox globally. Most vaccine-preventable diseases (measles, pertussis, etc.) have NOT been eradicated from the community; they still circulate, especially in under-vaccinated areas. If the disease were eradicated, no one would need the vaccine.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Herd immunity. Indirect protection from immunized peers.
(C)
Maternal antibodies. IgG transferred transplacentally wanes by \~6-12 months; not at age 5.
Maternal antibodies (IgG crossing the placenta) protect neonates in the first months of life. Typically 3-6 months. This child is 5 years old. Maternal antibody protection does not last 5 years.
(D)
Normal immune system. Doesn't explain absence of exposure; immunocompetence alone doesn't prevent infection.
A normal immune system means the child can respond to infections and clear them after exposure. But a normal immune system does not PREVENT exposure to the pathogen. It just fights the infection after it occurs. An unvaccinated child with a normal immune system can still contract and transmit measles, pertussis, etc.
(E)
Underdiagnosis. Not a recognized epidemiologic mechanism for protection.
Underdiagnosis of vaccine-preventable conditions. Implies the child HAS had these diseases but they weren't recognized. This is possible but is not the most likely explanation for a healthy-appearing child with no illness history.
Costume Change
Reappears as: herd immunity threshold questions (measles ~95%, polio ~80%). Or: outbreak in a school with high vaccination rates. Who is most at risk? → Unvaccinated individuals who have been protected by herd immunity. Or: herd immunity is lost when vaccination rates drop below the threshold.
Variable Pivot
If the question described global disappearance of smallpox → eradication (A). If the child were 3 months old → maternal antibodies (C) could be relevant. If the disease never appeared in that country → eradication possible. If the question asked why the child survived an exposure → normal immune system (D) + possibly herd immunity.
Tid BitThe unvaccinated freeload off the vaccinated. That's herd immunity.
Q031 Block 2 Answer: A
ObstetricsImmunology

Rh(D) immune globulin (RhoGAM)

Vignette Summary
Rh-negative G2P1 mother, prior pregnancy with positive indirect Coombs → mechanism of preventive drug?
The Discriminator
Anti-D immunoglobulin binds fetal Rh(D)+ RBCs in maternal circulation, preventing maternal sensitization.
Why the Answer is Correct
RhoGAM is exogenous IgG anti-D. It coats fetal Rh(D)+ erythrocytes that have entered maternal blood, leading to their rapid clearance before maternal B cells can mount a primary anti-D response. This prevents class switching to anti-D IgG that would otherwise cross the placenta in future pregnancies and cause hemolytic disease of the newborn.
Cognitive Demand
Mechanism of Rh immunoglobulin (RhoGAM) in preventing hemolytic disease of the newborn
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Anti-D IgG binding target confusion. RhoGAM works by binding to Rh-positive fetal red cells that have entered maternal circulation, preventing maternal sensitization. It does NOT block complement or change class switching.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Binds D antigen on fetal RBCs. Opsonizes them for clearance and prevents maternal sensitization.
(B)
Blocks complement on fetal RBCs. Wrong mechanism; not how RhoGAM works.
Complement blockade on fetal erythrocytes. Would prevent hemolysis but does not address the sensitization problem. The real issue is preventing maternal IgG anti-D formation in the first place. Blocking complement on fetal cells is not the mechanism of RhoGAM and is not a therapeutic strategy used in clinical practice.
(C)
Induces class switching in maternal B cells. Reverse mechanism; goal is to prevent maternal response.
Class switching by maternal B lymphocytes from IgM to IgG is what makes the maternal immune response dangerous (IgG crosses the placenta, IgM does not). However, RhoGAM works by preventing B cell activation entirely. Not by blocking class switching after it occurs. Preventing sensitization is upstream of class switching.
(D)
Inhibits fetal blood group A production. ABO unrelated; this is Rh disease.
Inhibiting fetal blood group A erythrocyte production. Would be dangerous and is not how RhoGAM works. Blood group A is the ABO system; Rh disease involves the D antigen. These are completely separate red cell antigen systems.
(E)
Prevents fetomaternal hemorrhage. Half-truth; doesn't stop the bleed, it neutralizes RBCs that crossed.
Preventing fetal erythrocytes from entering maternal blood. Would address the root cause. Fetal-maternal hemorrhage. But this is not what RhoGAM does. RhoGAM is administered AFTER fetal-maternal hemorrhage has occurred (e.g., after delivery, amniocentesis, trauma). It works by eliminating the Rh-positive fetal cells from maternal circulation before the immune system can recognize and mount a response to them.
Costume Change
Reappears as: when is RhoGAM given? → All Rh-negative women after any sensitizing event: delivery, miscarriage, abortion, amniocentesis, external cephalic version, any trauma during pregnancy. Also: the existing Coombs titer of 1:32 in this patient means she IS already sensitized. RhoGAM prevents future sensitization but cannot reverse existing antibodies. This fetus is at risk regardless.
Variable Pivot
If the patient already had a positive indirect Coombs titer (as this one does) → maternal sensitization has already occurred → RhoGAM will not help this pregnancy → monitor fetus with MCA Doppler and middle cerebral artery peak systolic velocity for fetal anemia. If Rh-negative mother with NO prior sensitization → give RhoGAM prophylactically at 28 weeks AND within 72 hours of delivery.
Tid BitRhoGAM = passive anti-D that hides fetal D antigen from mom's immune system.
Q032 Block 2 Answer: D
RheumatologyBiochemistry

Gout. Purine degradation

Vignette Summary
40-year-old → severe podagra after lobster and beer → big toe MTP red, swollen, exquisitely tender → which pathway's intermediates?
The Discriminator
Acute monoarticular first-MTP arthritis after purine/alcohol load = gout (uric acid = end product of purine degradation).
Why the Answer is Correct
Purine degradation: AMP/GMP → hypoxanthine/xanthine → uric acid (via xanthine oxidase). Shellfish (high purines) plus beer (high in guanosine and inhibits renal urate excretion via lactate) raise serum urate. Monosodium urate crystals deposit in cool peripheral joints, triggering NLRP3 inflammasome activation and a neutrophilic synovitis.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the metabolic pathway that accumulates intermediates causing gout
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The pathway must be one that produces URIC ACID as an end product. Uric acid is the final breakdown product of purines. Only the purine degradation pathway directly generates uric acid.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Chylomicron processing. Lipoprotein lipase deficiency causes pancreatitis, not arthritis.
Chylomicron processing. Involves lipoprotein lipase breaking down triglycerides in chylomicrons. This produces fatty acids and glycerol. Dyslipidemia may cause pseudogout (calcium pyrophosphate deposition) in some settings, but the chylomicron pathway does not generate uric acid.
(B)
Methionine regeneration. Homocystinuria/B12-folate biochemistry; unrelated.
Methionine regeneration (the methionine cycle). Converts homocysteine back to methionine via B12-dependent methionine synthase. Accumulation of homocysteine causes hyperhomocysteinemia. A cardiovascular risk factor, not gout.
(C)
Pentose phosphate pathway. G6PD deficiency causes hemolysis, not gout.
The pentose phosphate pathway generates NADPH and ribose-5-phosphate from glucose. It does not produce uric acid. PRPP (phosphoribosyl pyrophosphate) generated in this pathway feeds INTO purine synthesis, so it upstream influences uric acid, but the PPP itself does not accumulate uric acid intermediates.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Purine degradation. Uric acid accumulation → MSU crystals → gout.
(E)
Urea cycle. Hyperammonemia, not gout.
The urea cycle converts ammonia to urea for excretion. Defects in urea cycle enzymes cause hyperammonemia, encephalopathy, and elevated ammonia. Not hyperuricemia or gout.
Costume Change
Reappears as: which foods trigger gout? → High-purine foods (organ meats, shellfish, beer. Beer contains guanosine). Or: treatment of acute gout → NSAIDs, colchicine, steroids. Chronic → allopurinol (xanthine oxidase inhibitor) or febuxostat. Or: why does beer worsen gout more than wine? → Beer contains guanosine (purine) AND causes lactate production (competes with urate for renal secretion).
Variable Pivot
If the patient had calcium pyrophosphate crystals in knee fluid (rhomboid, positively birefringent) → pseudogout, not uric acid. If hyperuricemia + lymphoma treatment → tumor lysis syndrome (massive purine release). If the question asked about the enzyme target of allopurinol → xanthine oxidase.
Tid BitLobster + beer + big toe at 2 AM = gout. Blame xanthine oxidase.
Q033 Block 2 Answer: A
Behavioral ScienceAlcohol Use

Motivational interviewing. Open-ended question

Vignette Summary
Patient with DUI mentions drinking "a bit more than usual" → most appropriate initial response?
The Discriminator
Motivational interviewing favors open-ended, nonjudgmental questions that elicit the patient's own perspective.
Why the Answer is Correct
"Do you have any concerns about your alcohol use?" is open-ended, patient- centered, and avoids assumptions. It invites the patient to reflect on her behavior and explore ambivalence. The cornerstone of motivational interviewing.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the appropriate initial physician response to a patient who mentions alcohol-related consequences without expressing concern
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The patient has not identified alcohol as a problem. She mentioned the DUI incidentally. The physician's first response should be open-ended and non-confrontational to understand her perspective before screening or advising.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
"Do you have any concerns about your alcohol use?". Open-ended, nonjudgmental, patient-centered.
(B)
"More, less, or about the same?". Closed-ended, premature quantification.
"How has your alcohol use been this week compared to last week?" presupposes the physician already has baseline information and is tracking changes. This is a follow-up screening question, not an appropriate initial response to a new alcohol disclosure. Starting with a comparative question implies prior discussion that hasn't happened.
(C)
"So you're here because you're concerned…". Puts words in her mouth; she came for a routine exam.
"So you're here because you're concerned about your alcohol use" misrepresents the patient's stated reason for the visit. She came for a routine exam and mentioned the DUI. Summarizing her concern incorrectly is a reflection error and could damage trust.
(D)
"When was your last drink?". Closed-ended; assesses withdrawal risk but doesn't open dialogue.
"When was your last drink?" is a specific CAGE/AUDIT screening question. While important, it jumps to a screening question before exploring what the patient thinks about her alcohol use. This is premature and may feel accusatory before establishing rapport on this topic.
(E)
"Would you go to detox?". Skips assessment and stage of change; premature intervention.
"Would you be willing to go through a detoxification program?" is a treatment suggestion entirely premature after one DUI mention in a patient who drinks "socially." This is the righting reflex at its most aggressive. Immediately prescribing a solution to a problem the patient hasn't identified.
Costume Change
Reappears as any incidental disclosure of potentially problematic behavior (alcohol, drug use, gambling, unsafe sex). The initial response is always an open, non-judgmental question that invites the patient to share her perspective. Not advice, diagnosis, or screening before her frame is understood.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had expressed concern about her drinking → move to formal screening (AUDIT-C). If the patient had signs of physical dependence (tremor, tachycardia, diaphoresis) → assess for withdrawal risk before any other intervention. If the patient had elevated liver enzymes and macrocytic anemia → probe alcohol use more directly.
Tid BitLead with an open-ended question. Let the patient name their own concern.
Q034 Block 2 Answer: C
EmbryologyLymphatics

Lymphatic development. Mesoderm

Vignette Summary
4-year-old → progressive unilateral leg swelling → lymphoscintigraphy shows anomalous lymphatics → embryonic origin?
The Discriminator
Lymphatic vessels arise from mesoderm (lymphangioblasts budding from venous endothelium).
Why the Answer is Correct
All vascular structures. Arteries, veins, lymphatics, and the heart. Derive from mesoderm. Lymphatic endothelial cells bud from the cardinal veins under PROX1 control during early embryogenesis. Defects produce primary lymphedema (e.g., Milroy disease).
Cognitive Demand
Identify the embryological origin of lymphatic vessels
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Lymphatics from mesoderm is the answer, but the specific mechanism (budding from veins, mesodermal origin) is less known than the other germ layer associations.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Ectoderm. Epidermis and nervous system, not vessels.
Ectoderm. Gives rise to the nervous system (brain, spinal cord, peripheral nerves), skin epidermis, lens of the eye, and inner ear. Lymphatic vessels do not develop from ectoderm.
(B)
Endoderm. Gut epithelium and parenchyma of liver/pancreas/lungs.
Endoderm. Gives rise to the epithelial lining of the GI tract, respiratory tract, thyroid, parathyroid, thymus, bladder, and urethra. Lymphatic vessels do not develop from endoderm.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Mesoderm. All vasculature, including lymphatics.
(D)
Neural crest. Aortic arches' smooth muscle, melanocytes, enteric ganglia; not lymphatic endothelium.
Neural crest cells (a specialized population derived from neuroectoderm) give rise to dorsal root ganglia, autonomic ganglia, Schwann cells, melanocytes, craniofacial bones and cartilage, and the adrenal medulla. Neural crest does not produce lymphatic vessels.
(E)
Neuroectoderm. CNS neurons/glia.
Neuroectoderm. Gives rise to the CNS (brain and spinal cord) and retina. It does not produce lymphatic vessels.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Milroy disease (hereditary lymphedema from VEGFR3 mutation. Lymphatic vessel development). Or: what is the embryological origin of blood vessel endothelium? → Mesoderm (angioblasts). Or: lymph node primary lymphedema vs secondary (obstruction from tumor, infection, filariasis).
Variable Pivot
If the question were about peripheral nerve sheath tumor → neural crest (D). If about GI lining abnormality → endoderm (B). If about skin lesion → ectoderm (A). Mesoderm = muscle, bone, connective tissue, cardiovascular system, lymphatics, kidneys, gonads, spleen.
Tid BitVessels (blood and lymph) = mesoderm. Always.
Q035 Block 2 Answer: B
PulmonologyPhysiology

Obesity hypoventilation/OSA. Chronic hypoxemia

Vignette Summary
50-year-old, BMI 63, poorly adherent CPAP → cyanosis → ABG: pH 7.31, PCO2 70, PO2 50 → which additional finding?
The Discriminator
Chronic hypoxemia → renal EPO release → secondary polycythemia.
Why the Answer is Correct
Sustained tissue hypoxia upregulates HIF-1α in peritubular fibroblasts, raising erythropoietin output. EPO drives marrow erythropoiesis → increased hemoglobin and hematocrit. This is the textbook response to chronic hypoxemia in OSA/COPD.
Cognitive Demand
Predict lab findings in obesity hypoventilation syndrome (Pickwickian syndrome)
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Obese + hypercapnic + hypoxic = OHS. The question asks what ELSE would be found. Must reason from the physiology of chronic hypercapnia and hypoxia.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased serum bicarbonate. Reverse mechanism; chronic respiratory acidosis raises HCO3 (renal compensation).
In chronic respiratory acidosis (as seen in OHS), the kidneys compensate by retaining bicarbonate. The serum bicarbonate would be ELEVATED (metabolic compensation), not decreased. Decreased bicarbonate would indicate metabolic acidosis or hyperventilation compensation. Opposite of what happens in OHS.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Increased hemoglobin. Secondary polycythemia from chronic hypoxia.
(C)
Increased TLC. Obesity restricts the chest wall; TLC is typically reduced.
Increased total lung capacity. Describes an obstructive or hyperinflated pattern (emphysema, asthma). OHS is a restrictive ventilatory defect. The excess abdominal and chest wall fat restricts chest expansion, reducing TLC, FRC, and ERV. TLC is decreased, not increased.
(D)
Left ventricular hypertrophy. Chronic hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction causes RIGHT, not left, ventricular hypertrophy (cor pulmonale).
Left ventricular hypertrophy. Would result from systemic hypertension. OHS classically causes RIGHT ventricular hypertrophy (cor pulmonale) from pulmonary hypertension driven by chronic hypoxic vasoconstriction. The gray-blue discoloration of lips and nail beds describes central cyanosis from chronic hypoxemia. This is a pulmonary, not systemic, problem initially.
Costume Change
Reappears as: OHS physiology. Hypoxia → EPO release → erythrocytosis (elevated hemoglobin/hematocrit). Or: chronic hypoxia + right heart failure → cor pulmonale signs (JVD, peripheral edema, RV heave). Or: what is the key treatment for OHS? → CPAP/BiPAP, weight loss.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had emphysema instead of obesity → increased TLC, decreased DLCO, barrel chest (obstructive). If the patient were at high altitude → increased hemoglobin (erythrocytosis) as compensation, but normal or low PCO2. If hepatomegaly + peripheral edema + JVD + elevated RSVP → cor pulmonale from any cause.
Tid BitChronically blue = chronically high hemoglobin. Thank EPO.
Q036 Block 2 Answer: B
Infectious DiseasePediatrics

Hemolytic uremic syndrome. EHEC

Vignette Summary
5-year-old → bloody diarrhea → 5 days later → anemia, thrombocytopenia, AKI → infectious cause?
The Discriminator
Bloody diarrhea → HUS triad (MAHA + thrombocytopenia + AKI) = Shiga-toxin- producing E. Coli O157:H7.
Why the Answer is Correct
EHEC produces Shiga toxin, which binds Gb3 receptors on renal glomerular endothelium and inhibits the 60S ribosome (depurinates 28S rRNA). Endothelial injury triggers platelet microthrombi, mechanical RBC shearing (schistocytes), platelet consumption, and ischemic renal injury. Classic HUS.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the specific organism causing hemolytic uremic syndrome (HUS) in a child after bloody diarrhea
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The triad of microangiopathic hemolytic anemia + thrombocytopenia + renal failure in a child after bloody diarrhea = HUS = Shiga toxin-producing E. Coli O157:H7.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Campylobacter jejuni. Bloody diarrhea, yes; but linked to Guillain-Barré, not HUS.
Campylobacter jejuni. Causes bloody diarrhea in young adults and children and is associated with Guillain-Barré syndrome (ascending paralysis 2-4 weeks after infection, from molecular mimicry with gangliosides). It does not characteristically produce Shiga toxin and is not the classic cause of HUS.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
E. Coli (O157:H7). Shiga toxin → HUS.
(C)
Rotavirus. Watery, nonbloody diarrhea in infants; no HUS.
Rotavirus. Causes profuse watery (non-bloody) diarrhea in children under 2 years old. It is the most common cause of pediatric gastroenteritis requiring hospitalization worldwide. It does not produce bloody diarrhea or HUS.
(D)
Salmonella enteritidis. Self-limited bloody diarrhea, not HUS.
Salmonella enterica. Causes non-bloody diarrhea (typically) with abdominal cramping and fever. Typhoidal salmonella can cause rose spots. Non-typhoidal salmonella is the most common foodborne illness in the US. Neither produces the HUS triad.
(E)
Yersinia pestis. Plague. Buboes, sepsis; not enteric HUS (Y. Enterocolitica causes pseudoappendicitis but not HUS).
Yersinia pestis. Causes plague. Bubonic (lymphadenopathy/buboes), septicemic, or pneumonic forms. This is a rare, severe infection with a very different presentation. It does not cause the prodrome of bloody diarrhea followed by HUS in a child.
Costume Change
Reappears as: what toxin does STEC produce? → Shiga toxin (Stx) → damages renal tubular endothelium → thrombotic microangiopathy. Or: what is the treatment for HUS? → Supportive (dialysis if needed), NO antibiotics (worsen HUS by causing bacterial lysis and toxin release). Or: TTP vs HUS. TTP is adults + neuro symptoms + ADAMTS13 deficiency; HUS is children + renal predominant + STEC.
Variable Pivot
If ascending paralysis 2-4 weeks after diarrhea → Campylobacter → Guillain-Barré (A). If watery diarrhea in infant under 2 years → Rotavirus (C). If watery diarrhea after salad at a picnic + no bloody stools → Salmonella (D). If HUS in adult without diarrheal prodrome → atypical HUS (complement pathway mutation).
Tid BitBloody diarrhea + dropping platelets + rising creatinine = EHEC HUS. Don't give antibiotics.
Q037 Block 2 Answer: C
CardiologyPathology

Hypertensive LV hypertrophy

Vignette Summary
52-year-old → headaches, BP 154/100, displaced PMI, tall R waves in V5-V6 → what causes the LVH?
The Discriminator
Pressure overload (HTN) → concentric hypertrophy from sarcomeres added in parallel.
Why the Answer is Correct
In chronic pressure overload, increased wall stress upregulates contractile protein synthesis; sarcomeres are added in parallel, thickening the wall (concentric hypertrophy) to normalize wall stress (Laplace). This is myocyte HYPERTROPHY (cell enlargement), not hyperplasia. Adult cardiomyocytes are post-mitotic.
Cognitive Demand
Explain the cellular mechanism of left ventricular hypertrophy in chronic hypertension
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Hyperplasia vs hypertrophy. Cardiac myocytes are terminally differentiated and cannot divide. LVH is from hypertrophy (increased protein synthesis in existing cells), not hyperplasia.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Glycogen accumulation. Pompe disease or Danon. Infiltrative/lysosomal, not essential HTN.
Excessive glycogen accumulation. Occurs in glycogen storage diseases (Pompe, Danon) and produces cardiomegaly from glycogen-engorged myocytes. This is not the mechanism of hypertensive LVH. Standard LVH does not involve abnormal glycogen deposition.
(B)
Fibrosis of conduction pathways. Causes block/arrhythmia, not LVH.
Fibrosis of intraventricular conduction pathways. Produces bundle branch blocks, prolonged QRS, and conduction system disease. This patient's ECG shows left axis deviation and tall R waves. A hypertrophy pattern, not a conduction block pattern. Fibrosis alone does not explain the increase in wall mass.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Increased synthesis of contractile filaments. Sarcomeres in parallel = concentric LVH.
(D)
Misfolded cytoskeletal aggregates. Amyloidosis/desminopathies; restrictive picture.
Misfolding and aggregation of cytoskeletal proteins. Describes cardiac amyloidosis (AL or TTR) or desminopathies. Amyloid deposition causes a stiff, restrictive cardiomyopathy with sparkling appearance on echo. This patient has pressure-overload LVH from hypertension, not protein aggregation disease.
(E)
Myocyte hyperplasia from embryonic genes. Adult myocytes don't divide; hypertrophy via fetal-gene re- expression occurs but the cells grow, not multiply.
Myocyte hyperplasia from embryonic gene induction. Is wrong on two levels: cardiac myocytes cannot undergo hyperplasia (they are terminally differentiated), and while some fetal genes ARE re-expressed in hypertrophied myocytes (beta-myosin heavy chain, ANP), this describes gene expression pattern, not the mechanism of the hypertrophy itself.
Costume Change
Reappears as: what is the difference between concentric and eccentric LVH? → Concentric (pressure overload. Hypertension, aortic stenosis): increased wall thickness, normal or decreased cavity size, sarcomeres added in parallel. Eccentric (volume overload. Aortic regurgitation, MR): increased cavity size, increased wall thickness, sarcomeres added in series.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had amyloidosis → sparkling myocardium on echo, restrictive pattern on Doppler, low voltage on ECG despite thick walls. If the patient had HCM → asymmetric septal hypertrophy, dynamic LVOT obstruction, family history. If the cause were aortic stenosis → same concentric LVH but with systolic murmur + calcified valve on echo.
Tid BitPressure overload = parallel sarcomeres = concentric LVH. Volume overload = series = eccentric.
Q038 Block 2 Answer: B
BiochemistryPharmacologyToxicology

Carcinogen metabolic activation. CYP450

Vignette Summary
Polymorphisms in metabolic-activation enzymes increase lung cancer risk in smokers exposed to benzene → which enzyme?
The Discriminator
"Metabolic activation" of procarcinogens (benzene, PAHs) = Phase I oxidation by cytochrome P450s.
Why the Answer is Correct
CYP450 enzymes (notably CYP1A1, CYP2E1) bioactivate procarcinogens in tobacco smoke and benzene into reactive electrophilic epoxides that covalently bind DNA. Polymorphisms producing higher-activity CYP variants increase carcinogen activation and cancer risk.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the enzyme class responsible for metabolic activation of carcinogens like benzene
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Metabolic activation. Carcinogens are often pro-carcinogens that require enzymatic conversion to electrophilic intermediates. CYP450 enzymes perform this activation. Other enzymes listed either detoxify or are unrelated.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Aldehyde dehydrogenase. Detoxifies acetaldehyde; deficiency causes flushing, not activation of benzene.
Aldehyde dehydrogenase metabolizes aldehydes to carboxylic acids. It detoxifies acetaldehyde from alcohol metabolism. Some aldehydes can be carcinogenic, but ALDH is generally involved in DETOXIFICATION, not activation. Genetic variants in ALDH2 cause acetaldehyde accumulation (flushing reaction in East Asians). But this is not the mechanism for benzene-induced carcinogenicity.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Cytochrome P450. Phase I activation of procarcinogens.
(C)
Glutathione S-transferase. Phase II DETOXIFICATION; reverse direction.
Glutathione S-transferase (GST) conjugates electrophilic intermediates with glutathione, facilitating their urinary excretion. This is a DETOXIFICATION enzyme. Low GST activity would INCREASE cancer risk. GST polymorphisms that reduce activity are associated with increased cancer susceptibility, but the question asks about metabolic activation, not detoxification.
(D)
Methyltransferase. Generally inactivating/conjugating; not the activator here.
Methyltransferases transfer methyl groups and. Are involved in DNA methylation, catecholamine metabolism (COMT), and drug metabolism. They do not perform the oxidative metabolism that activates benzene to its carcinogenic epoxide intermediates.
(E)
Succinate dehydrogenase. TCA cycle/ETC complex II; unrelated.
Succinate dehydrogenase (Complex II of the ETC). Is a mitochondrial enzyme involved in the Krebs cycle and electron transport. It is mutated in hereditary paraganglioma/pheochromocytoma syndromes. It has no role in carcinogen activation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: CYP1A1, CYP1A2 polymorphisms and lung cancer risk from PAHs (polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons in cigarette smoke). Or: why does the same exposure (benzene, PAH) produce cancer in some people and not others? → CYP450 polymorphisms → different activation rates. Or: aryl hydrocarbon receptor (AhR) → induces CYP1A1 → activates PAHs.
Variable Pivot
If asking about ethanol metabolism and cancer risk → CYP2E1 (activated by chronic alcohol) generates reactive oxygen species. If asking about drug-drug interaction mechanisms → CYP3A4, CYP2C9, CYP2D6. If asking about detoxification of carcinogen intermediates → GST (C) or epoxide hydrolase.
Tid BitCYP450 = bioactivator. GST/UGT = detoxifier.
Q039 Block 2 Answer: B
PharmacologyDrug Development

Phase 1 clinical trial

Vignette Summary
New drug → 80 healthy volunteers → maximum tolerated dose and pharmacokinetics → which phase?
The Discriminator
Healthy volunteers + safety/PK = Phase 1.
Why the Answer is Correct
Phase 1 trials use a small number of healthy volunteers (typically 20-100) to establish safety, maximum tolerated dose, and pharmacokinetics/pharmacodynamics. Efficacy is not the goal here.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the correct FDA phase of drug development based on study characteristics
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Phase 1 = first in humans + healthy volunteers + safety/pharmacokinetics. The keywords: "healthy volunteers" + "maximum tolerated dose" + "pharmacokinetic properties" define Phase 1 unambiguously.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Phase 0. Microdosing in very few subjects for exploratory PK; not MTD.
Phase 0. Is a microdosing phase used by some drug developers to collect preliminary pharmacokinetic data. It involves sub-therapeutic doses in a very small number of subjects (fewer than 10-15) and is not a standard FDA phase. Studying 80 healthy volunteers at full doses exceeds Phase 0 scope.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Phase 1. Safety/dose in healthy volunteers.
(C)
Phase 2. Small patient cohort with the disease; assesses efficacy and side effects.
Phase 2 trials assess drug efficacy and side effects in a PATIENT population (people with the target disease), not healthy volunteers. Phase 2 studies determine optimal dosing for efficacy. This study is in healthy volunteers without the target disease. Phase 1.
(D)
Phase 3. Large patient RCTs comparing to standard of care.
Phase 3 trials. Are large-scale randomized controlled trials comparing the drug to existing treatment or placebo in patient populations. Typically hundreds to thousands of participants. This 80-person healthy volunteer study is too small and too early for Phase 3.
(E)
Phase 4. Post-marketing surveillance.
Phase 4. Is post-marketing surveillance conducted after FDA approval, when the drug is in clinical use. The purpose is to detect rare adverse effects not identified in smaller trials. This drug is not yet approved.
Costume Change
Reappears as: what is the purpose of each trial phase? Phase 1 = safety/PK in healthy volunteers. Phase 2 = efficacy/dosing in patients (small). Phase 3 = efficacy vs standard of care in large RCT (patients). Phase 4 = post-marketing surveillance. Or: which phase is most likely to detect rare adverse effects? → Phase 4.
Variable Pivot
If a study enrolled 500 patients with hypertension comparing the drug to amlodipine in an RCT → Phase 3. If monitoring for rare adverse effects after FDA approval → Phase 4. If only 12 healthy volunteers given sub-therapeutic doses → Phase 0 or Phase 1.
Tid Bit"Is it Safe? Does it Work? Does it work Well? Can it stay on the market?" = Phases 1-4.
Q040 Block 2 Answer: A
EndocrinologyBiochemistry

Hypoglycemia unresponsive to glucagon

Vignette Summary
21-year-old T1DM on insulin pump → marathon training → unresponsive, glucose 25 → glucagon given → glucose still 25 → why?
The Discriminator
Glucagon raises glucose only if liver glycogen is available; chronic exercise depletes glycogen stores.
Why the Answer is Correct
Glucagon acutely raises serum glucose primarily by stimulating hepatic glycogenolysis (Gs/cAMP/PKA → phosphorylase). With prolonged endurance training plus reduced insulin, hepatic glycogen is depleted; glucagon then has no substrate to mobilize. Glucagon's gluconeogenic effect is too slow to correct acute hypoglycemia.
Cognitive Demand
Explain why glucagon injection fails to correct hypoglycemia in a marathon runner with T1DM
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Glucagon works by stimulating hepatic GLYCOGENOLYSIS. If there's no glycogen to mobilize, glucagon can't raise glucose. Endurance exercise depletes hepatic glycogen stores.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Depletion of glycogen stores. No glycogen = no glucagon response.
(B)
Downregulated glucagon receptors. Not a recognized exercise effect.
Glucagon receptor downregulation by exercise. Is not a recognized physiological mechanism. Exercise actually increases glucagon secretion (counter-regulatory response to exercise-induced glucose consumption). Glucagon receptors are not downregulated by exercise. Glucagon signaling is intact; the substrate (glycogen) is simply absent.
(C)
Catecholamines inhibit glycogenolysis. Reverse mechanism; epinephrine STIMULATES glycogenolysis.
Catecholamines (epinephrine) actually STIMULATE glycogenolysis and gluconeogenesis through beta-adrenergic and alpha-adrenergic receptors. They do not impair glycogenolysis. If anything, the stress of hypoglycemia would increase catecholamines to promote glucose release. But without glycogen, this stimulus has no substrate.
(D)
Insulin inhibiting gluconeogenesis. He reduced his pump rate; insulin level is low. Also, glucagon's primary acute action is glycogenolysis, not gluconeogenesis.
Inhibition of gluconeogenesis from high circulating insulin does not apply here. This patient decreased his insulin pump rate before exercise. Circulating insulin is likely LOW. More importantly, even if gluconeogenesis were inhibited, that would not explain the failure of GLYCOGENOLYSIS to respond to glucagon. The question specifically says glucose does not rise at all after glucagon.
Costume Change
Reappears as: why must glucagon be given before glycogen depletion? → Overnight fast + hypoglycemia in T1DM → glucagon may work if prior glycogen stored. Or: what is the treatment when glucagon fails? → IV dextrose (D50). Bypasses glycogenolysis entirely by supplying glucose directly. Or: why does alcohol cause hypoglycemia? → Ethanol inhibits gluconeogenesis + may deplete glycogen → glucagon-resistant hypoglycemia.
Variable Pivot
If the glucagon response were delayed but eventually present → glycogen present but depleted partially. If glucagon failed in a patient with liver disease → impaired glycogenolysis from hepatic dysfunction. If the patient also had alcohol ingestion before hypoglycemia → combined glycogen depletion + gluconeogenesis inhibition.
Tid BitGlucagon is a key without a lock if the glycogen pantry is empty. Give IV dextrose. NBME Free 120 (2026). Block 3 Answer Explanations
Q041 Block 3 Answer: B
GIOncology

Breaking bad news. Likely pancreatic cancer

Vignette Summary
elderly woman → painless jaundice + weight loss → CT shows pancreatic head mass + liver lesions → biopsy pending → what to say next.
The Discriminator
SPIKES protocol. After Setting/Perception/Invitation, the physician should give honest, plain-language Knowledge with a warning shot, not hedge or minimize.
Why the Answer is Correct
Patient-centered communication requires honest disclosure framed in lay language. "I'm concerned the mass could be cancer. The biopsy will provide more information." names the suspicion plainly, owns the uncertainty, and points to the next concrete step. It avoids both false reassurance and withholding clinically important information.
Cognitive Demand
Select the appropriate level of uncertainty to share with a patient when biopsy results are pending
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Transparency calibration. The radiologist suspects cancer but hasn't confirmed it. The physician must be honest about concern without stating an unconfirmed diagnosis as fact.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
"Malignant mass". Uses jargon ("malignant") that many patients don't parse; Half-truth.
"I suspect you have a malignant mass" states an unconfirmed diagnosis. The biopsy is pending specifically because the diagnosis is not confirmed. Telling a patient she likely has cancer before biopsy results. Based only on radiological appearance. Is premature and potentially wrong. The correct approach communicates concern without stating a conclusion.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Honest, lay-language disclosure that names cancer concern and sets up biopsy. Models SPIKES.
(C)
"Let's not worry". False reassurance / minimization; blocks autonomy.
"This could be something serious, but let's not worry until we know" trivializes the patient's right to understand the gravity of the situation. It is evasive and paternalistic. Patients have the right to understand that cancer is being considered.
(D)
Wait for biopsy before sharing suspicion. Paternalistic withholding; violates honesty.
"This mass could be benign or malignant. I'd like to wait before sharing the suspected diagnosis" withholds the physician's clinical impression that this is likely cancer. The physician already suspects cancer (based on the radiologist's read and the CT findings) and has a duty to share that concern honestly while awaiting confirmation.
(E)
"We don't know yet". Technically true but evasive; conceals the working diagnosis the patient needs to plan.
"We don't know what this mass is yet" is not fully truthful. The physician DOES have a strong suspicion. It just hasn't been confirmed. This response fails to communicate the level of concern appropriately.
Costume Change
Reappears as any serious diagnosis pending confirmation. Communicating concern honestly without stating unconfirmed conclusions. The model is: state the concern, explain what you know, explain what you're doing to find out, invite questions.
Variable Pivot
If biopsy confirmed cancer → full disclosure with support resources. If patient asked "do you think it's cancer?" → honest answer: "I'm concerned it might be, which is why we're doing the biopsy." If patient had advance directive stating she didn't want to know a terminal diagnosis → ethics consultation.
Tid BitWarning shot + plain word "cancer" beats jargon every time on NBME ethics.
Q042 Block 3 Answer: B
HematologyMyeloproliferative Neoplasms

Polycythemia vera

Vignette Summary
middle-aged man → headache, fatigue, splenomegaly → Hb 19.5, Hct 59%, plt 450k → genetic mutation?
The Discriminator
Erythrocytosis + splenomegaly + thrombocytosis in a non-smoker = polycythemia vera, driven by JAK2 V617F.
Why the Answer is Correct
Polycythemia vera is a myeloproliferative neoplasm caused by an activating point mutation in JAK2 (V617F, exon 14). Constitutively active JAK2 makes erythroid precursors EPO- independent, producing autonomous RBC (and often platelet/granulocyte) overproduction. Splenomegaly reflects extramedullary hematopoiesis and sequestration.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mutation in polycythemia vera based on the CBC pattern
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Elevated hemoglobin + thrombocytosis + splenomegaly in a non-smoking, non-hypoxic patient = polycythemia vera = JAK2 V617F mutation.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
G protein. GNAS in McCune-Albright/somatotroph adenomas; not PV.
G protein mutations (GNAS activating mutations) cause McCune-Albright syndrome and some pituitary adenomas/somatotroph tumors. They do not drive clonal myeloproliferation.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
JAK2. \>95% of PV harbors JAK2 V617F; also \~50% of ET/PMF.
(C)
PDGF. PDGFRA/ B fusions cause hypereosinophilic syndromes/CML-like disease, not PV.
Platelet-derived growth factor (PDGFR) mutations. Are found in CEL (chronic eosinophilic leukemia) and some cases of systemic mastocytosis. PDGFR fusions respond to imatinib. They do not cause polycythemia vera.
(D)
Retinoic acid receptor. PML-RARα is acute promyelocytic leukemia (M3).
Retinoic acid receptor alpha (RARA) rearrangements (t[15;17]) cause acute promyelocytic leukemia (APL/AML M3). APL presents with pancytopenia and DIC, not isolated polycythemia and thrombocytosis.
(E)
Thrombopoietin receptor (MPL). Drives a minority of ET/PMF; in PV, EPO is suppressed and JAK2 is the lesion.
Thrombopoietin receptor (MPL) mutations cause essential thrombocythemia (ET) or primary myelofibrosis but NOT polycythemia vera. In ET, platelets are dramatically elevated while hemoglobin is normal. This patient has elevated hemoglobin as the dominant finding.
Costume Change
Reappears as: polycythemia vera treatment → phlebotomy (first line), hydroxyurea (reduce clot risk), low-dose aspirin. Or: PV complications → thrombosis (stroke, DVT), transformation to myelofibrosis or AML. Or: JAK2 V617F is present in ~95% of PV and ~50% of ET and MF.
Variable Pivot
If platelets are dominant finding + normal hemoglobin → essential thrombocythemia → MPL or JAK2 mutation (E or B). If massive splenomegaly + teardrop cells + leukoerythroblastic smear → primary myelofibrosis → JAK2/CALR/MPL. If all cell lines elevated (WBC + RBC + platelets) → think PV or CML (check BCR-ABL).
Tid BitLow EPO + high Hct + JAK2 = polycythemia vera. Phlebotomize and add hydroxyurea.
Q043 Block 3 Answer: E
NeurologyTemporal Lobe Anatomy

Mesial temporal lobe epilepsy

Vignette Summary
young woman → 3-yr complex partial seizures, olfactory aura ("burning rubber"), automatisms (lip smacking) → drug-resistant → localize for resection on coronal brain image.
The Discriminator
Olfactory aura + automatisms = uncinate/mesial temporal focus → hippocampus / medial temporal lobe.
Why the Answer is Correct
"Uncinate fits". Olfactory hallucinations originating in the uncus/amygdala. And oroalimentary automatisms localize to the mesial temporal lobe, classically with hippocampal sclerosis (neuronal loss + gliosis in CA1). This is the most common surgically remediable epilepsy; anterior temporal lobectomy targeting the hippocampus/amygdala is curative in most refractory cases.
Cognitive Demand
Localize a seizure focus producing olfactory aura + lip smacking + emotional behavior
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Olfactory aura = uncus = temporal lobe medial surface. The seizure type is complex partial with automatisms, originating from the mesial temporal lobe.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Frontal cortex. Frontal seizures are brief, nocturnal, with bizarre motor posturing, no olfactory aura.
Region A (frontal lobe cortex). Would produce frontal lobe seizures characterized by brief, bizarre motor automatisms, nocturnal occurrence, rapid secondary generalization, and no aura. Olfactory auras are not a feature of frontal lobe seizures.
(B)
Basal ganglia. Produces movement disorders, not focal seizures.
Region B (cingulate gyrus / superior frontal area). Is involved in emotional and motor functions. Cingulate seizures can have emotional features but not olfactory auras specifically. The combination of olfactory aura + automatisms (lip smacking) is the mesial temporal signature.
(C)
Thalamus. Generalized/absence circuitry; not focal automatisms.
Region C (basal ganglia/thalamic level). Is not seizure-generating tissue in the same way as cortex. Thalamic involvement is in absence seizures (3Hz spike-wave), not complex partial with olfactory aura.
(D)
Lateral temporal neocortex. Auditory auras; less common surgical focus.
Region D (temporal cortex lateral surface) could generate auditory or vertiginous auras from the superior temporal gyrus. Lateral temporal seizures do not produce olfactory auras.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Hippocampus / mesial temporal lobe. Classic site of mesial temporal sclerosis driving uncinate seizures with automatisms.
Costume Change
Reappears as: mesial temporal lobe epilepsy (MTLE) is the most common type of focal epilepsy and often involves hippocampal sclerosis. Or: treatment-resistant MTLE → temporal lobectomy achieves seizure freedom in ~70% of patients. Or: which aura predicts which lobe? → Visual = occipital, auditory = lateral temporal, olfactory = mesial temporal, somatosensory = parietal.
Variable Pivot
If the aura were auditory hallucinations → lateral temporal lobe. If the patient had a Jacksonian march (focal motor progressing distally) → primary motor cortex (frontal). If absence seizures (staring, 3Hz spike-wave, brief) → generalized epilepsy, thalamic involvement.
Tid BitBurning rubber + lip smacking = hippocampus. Resect it.
Q044 Block 3 Answer: A
Infectious DiseaseDermatologyEating Disorders

Bulimia-associated sialadenosis (pilocarpine for symptom relief)

Vignette Summary
20F admitted for bulimia. Bilateral parotid swelling, dental enamel erosion, dorsal hand calluses (Russell sign). Normal BMI rules out anorexia. Started on fluoxetine. Develops painful gland swelling needing symptomatic relief.
The Discriminator
Painful bilateral parotid enlargement in a patient with chronic vomiting is sialadenosis from repeated autonomic stimulation of the salivary glands. The symptom drug is a sialagogue. M3 muscarinic agonists (pilocarpine, cevimeline) drive saliva production through acinar cell receptors, relieving the obstructive gland pressure.
Why the Answer is Correct
Sialadenosis is a noninflammatory, non-infectious enlargement of the salivary glands seen in bulimia, alcohol use disorder, diabetes, and other states of chronic sympathetic dysregulation. The salivary acini hypertrophy and the gland swells. The pain in this case comes from the stretched capsule. Pilocarpine binds the M3 muscarinic receptor on acinar cells, increases secretion, lowers intraluminal pressure, and relieves the pain. Pilocarpine also has dental utility for treating xerostomia.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the antibiotic class treating oral candidiasis in an immunosuppressed patient with bulimia-related findings
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The finding that clinches the diagnosis is the combination of: bilateral parotid swelling + dental erosions + hand scarring (Russell's sign) = bulimia + oral thrush from fluoxetine-induced immunosuppression and altered oral flora.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Binding to muscarinic acetylcholine receptors. CORRECT. Pilocarpine and cevimeline are M3 agonists that increase salivary, sweat, and lacrimal output. The trigger for the symptomatic dose here is gland pain from sialadenosis, not infection.
(B)
Binding to nicotinic acetylcholine receptors. Nicotine, varenicline, and neuromuscular blockers act here. Not used for salivary gland disease.
Nicotinic acetylcholine receptors. Are targeted by agents like succinylcholine (depolarizing NMJ blocker). These have no antifungal activity.
(C)
Inhibition of protein synthesis via the 50S subunit. Macrolides (azithromycin, clarithromycin). Would be the answer if the vignette described an acute suppurative bacterial parotitis with a unilateral red tender gland, fever, and purulent ductal discharge.
50S ribosomal subunit inhibition (chloramphenicol, linezolid) treats bacterial infections. Candida is a fungus. Bacterial antibiotics have no activity against fungi.
(D)
Loss of DNA helical structure (metronidazole). Anaerobic and protozoal infections. Not the mechanism for a noninfectious gland.
DNA intercalation/loss of helical structure (metronidazole, fluoroquinolones to some extent) targets prokaryotic DNA. Antifungal therapy targets ergosterol, cell wall glucan, or fungal-specific enzymes. Not DNA in this manner.
(E)
Interference with bacterial cell wall synthesis. Beta-lactams. Again, would apply only to acute bacterial sialadenitis.
Bacterial cell wall synthesis inhibition (beta-lactams, vancomycin) has no activity against fungi, which have cell walls made of chitin and beta-glucan rather than peptidoglycan.
Costume Change
Reappears as: fluconazole mechanism → inhibits CYP51 (lanosterol demethylase) → blocks ergosterol synthesis. Muscarinic receptor binding → nystatin? No. Nystatin binds ergosterol directly (polyene). Or: bulimia signs: Russell's sign (dorsal hand calluses from induced vomiting), parotid hypertrophy, dental erosions, hypokalemia, metabolic alkalosis.
Variable Pivot
If oral white plaques that scrape off (pseudomembranous candidiasis) → fluconazole or clotrimazole troches. If angular cheilitis + white patches at corners of mouth → also Candida, same treatment. If patient had esophageal candidiasis → systemic fluconazole. If the question asked about the eating disorder complication → metabolic alkalosis from purging.
Tid BitSialadenosis from chronic vomiting is sterile and bilateral. Treat with rehydration, gland massage, and pilocarpine for symptomatic flare. If unilateral, painful, red, and febrile think bacterial parotitis and add a beta-lactam.
Q045 Block 3 Answer: D
ImmunologyDermatology

Poison ivy contact dermatitis

Vignette Summary
middle-aged man → cleared weeds → 1 day later severely itchy rash in linear/streaky distribution → photograph.
The Discriminator
Delayed rash (24-48h) after plant contact = urushiol-induced allergic contact dermatitis = Type IV hypersensitivity.
Why the Answer is Correct
Urushiol from Toxicodendron (poison ivy/oak/sumac) is a hapten that binds skin proteins, is presented by Langerhans cells, and sensitizes CD4+ Th1 cells. On re-exposure, memory T cells release IFN-γ → macrophage activation → vesicular, pruritic dermatitis 24-72h later. Classic delayed- type (cell-mediated) hypersensitivity.
Cognitive Demand
Classify the type of hypersensitivity reaction causing contact dermatitis from poison ivy exposure
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Delayed onset (1 day after exposure) + contact with a plant antigen = Type IV (T cell-mediated, delayed) hypersensitivity. Any reaction that develops over 24-72 hours after antigen contact and involves T cells is Type IV.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Type I. IgE/mast-cell; minutes, urticarial/anaphylactic; wrong tempo.
Type I (immediate hypersensitivity). Is IgE-mediated, occurring within minutes of allergen exposure. It requires prior sensitization and produces urticaria, angioedema, bronchospasm, or anaphylaxis. The 1-day delay here rules out Type I. IgE-mediated reactions occur within 15-30 minutes, not 24 hours.
(B)
Type II. Antibody-mediated cytotoxicity (e.g., AIHA); wrong mechanism.
Type II (cytotoxic) hypersensitivity. Involves IgG or IgM antibodies binding to cell surface antigens, activating complement and NK cells. It causes hemolytic anemia (drug-induced, autoimmune), Goodpasture syndrome, and Graves disease. It does not cause contact dermatitis.
(C)
Type III. Immune-complex deposition (serum sickness, SLE); wrong tempo and pattern.
Type III (immune complex-mediated) hypersensitivity. Involves antigen-antibody complex deposition in vessel walls or tissues, activating complement and causing inflammation. Classic examples: serum sickness, SLE nephritis, post-streptococcal GN. It causes vasculitis and glomerulonephritis, not contact dermatitis.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Type IV. T-cell- mediated delayed contact dermatitis (urushiol, nickel, PPD). - (No E listed.)
Costume Change
Reappears as: PPD tuberculin skin test (Type IV), transplant rejection (Type IV), nickel allergy, latex allergy (Type IV component). Or: hypersensitivity type classification for every major allergy. Type I = immediate, IgE, mast cells; Type II = cytotoxic, IgG/IgM; Type III = immune complex; Type IV = delayed, T cell.
Variable Pivot
If wheal and flare within 15 minutes of exposure → Type I (A). If hemolytic anemia from drug → Type II (B). If serum sickness 7-10 days after antigen exposure → Type III (C). If 48-72 hour induration at PPD injection site → Type IV (D).
Tid Bit"Four T's" of Type IV. T cells, Tuberculin (PPD), Transplant rejection, Touching (contact dermatitis).
Q046 Block 3 Answer: E
CardiologyPulmonologyHigh-Altitude Physiology

High-altitude acclimatization → cor pulmonale

Vignette Summary
park ranger living at 10,000 ft for 2 yrs → dyspnea, JVD, pedal edema at sea level visit → right- heart failure picture.
The Discriminator
Chronic hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction → pulmonary hypertension → RV hypertrophy → reduced RV diastolic compliance.
Why the Answer is Correct
Chronic alveolar hypoxia at altitude triggers sustained hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction and vascular remodeling, raising pulmonary vascular resistance. The right ventricle hypertrophies (concentric pressure-overload remodeling). Hypertrophied RV is stiffer → decreased diastolic compliance → elevated RA pressure, JVD, and peripheral edema (cor pulmonale).
Cognitive Demand
Identify which measurement DECREASES after 2 years at high altitude with chronic hypoxic pulmonary hypertension
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Chronic hypoxia → pulmonary vasoconstriction → pulmonary hypertension → right heart failure. The question asks what DECREASED. RV diastolic compliance decreases as the RV becomes stiff from pressure overload.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
P-wave height (lead I). RA enlargement actually increases P-wave amplitude (P pulmonale in II); not decreased.
P wave height in lead I. Reflects right atrial enlargement. In cor pulmonale from pulmonary hypertension, P waves become tall and peaked (P pulmonale) in leads II, III, and aVF. P waves in lead I may not be the primary finding and would not decrease.
(B)
R wave in V1. RVH increases R in V1; not decreased.
R wave height in V1. Reflects right ventricular hypertrophy. In cor pulmonale, R waves in V1 INCREASE (right ventricular dominance shifts the QRS axis rightward). This would increase, not decrease.
(C)
Hematocrit. EPO-driven erythrocytosis raises Hct at altitude; increases, not decreases.
Hematocrit INCREASES at high altitude as EPO-driven erythropoiesis compensates for hypoxia. This is an adaptive response, not a decrease.
(D)
PVR. Increases with chronic hypoxia.
Pulmonary vascular resistance INCREASES at high altitude due to hypoxic pulmonary vasoconstriction. Chronic hypoxia drives vascular remodeling that further increases PVR. This is the mechanism causing the cor pulmonale. It increases, not decreases.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
