RV diastolic compliance. Hypertrophy stiffens the RV.
(F)
RV wall thickness. Increases with pressure overload.
Right ventricular wall thickness INCREASES in response to chronic pressure overload. Concentric RV hypertrophy develops as the RV adapts to pumping against elevated pulmonary vascular resistance.
Costume Change
Reappears as: high altitude → hypoxia → EPO → polycythemia → increased blood viscosity → increased thrombosis risk. Or: cor pulmonale findings: JVD, hepatomegaly, pedal edema, right-sided S3/S4, right parasternal heave. ECG: right axis deviation, P pulmonale, RVH pattern.
Variable Pivot
If the patient descended to sea level → PVR would decrease, RV would remodel back, polycythemia would resolve. If the patient had primary pulmonary hypertension (not altitude-related) → same end-organ effects, different etiology. If the patient had chronic obstructive pulmonary disease → also cor pulmonale but from alveolar destruction + hypoxia.
Tid BitHigh altitude → HPV → PVR up → RVH → stiff RV → cor pulmonale.
Q047 Block 3 Answer: A
CardiologyPediatrics

Coarctation of the aorta

Vignette Summary
7-yr-old → HTN in arms, hypotension in legs (BP gradient), interscapular systolic murmur → look for additional finding.
The Discriminator
Upper-extremity hypertension + lower-extremity hypotension + interscapular murmur = coarctation of the aorta → delayed/diminished femoral pulses (radiofemoral delay).
Why the Answer is Correct
Aortic narrowing distal to the left subclavian limits flow to lower body. Pressure proximal to the coarctation is elevated (UE HTN); distal pressure is reduced and pulse wave is delayed → femoral pulses are weak and delayed relative to brachial. Collateral flow through intercostals causes notching (rib notching) and the interscapular bruit. Associated with bicuspid aortic valve and Turner syndrome.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the additional physical finding consistent with coarctation of the aorta given BP differential + systolic murmur
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Upper extremity hypertension + lower extremity hypotension + systolic murmur at the back + left sternal border = coarctation of the aorta. The additional finding is delayed femoral pulses (radio-femoral delay).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Delayed femoral pulses. Hallmark of coarctation.
(B)
Increased JVP. Right-heart failure; not coarctation.
Increased jugular venous pressure. Occurs in right heart failure, cardiac tamponade, or obstruction of superior vena cava. Coarctation causes systemic hypertension from renal hypoperfusion. Left-sided pressure overload, not right-sided failure. JVP is not a feature of coarctation.
(C)
Nontender inguinal mass. Hernia; unrelated.
Non-tender mass in the inguinal area. Suggests lymphadenopathy or hernia. This is not associated with coarctation. The groin finding in coarctation would be diminished or delayed femoral pulses, not a mass.
(D)
Pulsatile abdominal mass. AAA, adult disease; not in a 7-yr-old.
Pulsatile abdominal mass. Describes an abdominal aortic aneurysm. Coarctation involves the aorta at the level of the ligamentum arteriosum (just distal to the left subclavian artery), not the abdominal aorta. AAA would also not occur in a child.
(E)
Pulsus paradoxus. Tamponade/severe asthma; wrong pathophysiology.
Pulsus paradoxus. Is an exaggerated decrease in systolic blood pressure during inspiration, characteristic of cardiac tamponade or severe asthma/COPD. It is not a feature of coarctation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: coarctation associated conditions. Bicuspid aortic valve (in ~50%), Turner syndrome (45,XO). Or: notching of ribs on CXR from enlarged intercostal collateral vessels. Or: treatment. Balloon angioplasty or surgical repair. Or: coarctation post-ductal vs pre-ductal (infant presentation) vs post-ductal (adult presentation with collaterals).
Variable Pivot
If the BP differential were reversed (lower in arm, normal in leg) → think of subclavian steal syndrome or aberrant subclavian artery. If the infant presented with sudden decompensation as the ductus closes → pre-ductal coarctation (ductus-dependent circulation). If Turner syndrome (45,XO) female → check for coarctation + bicuspid AV.
Tid BitKid with hypertension? Check femoral pulses and the four-extremity BP.
Q048 Block 3 Answer: A
PulmonologyEndocrinologyCalcium Physiology

Sarcoidosis hypercalcemia

Vignette Summary
middle-aged woman → fatigue, constipation, polyuria → Ca 11.1, PTH low, bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy + interstitial infiltrates on CXR.
The Discriminator
Hypercalcemia + suppressed PTH + bilateral hilar adenopathy = sarcoidosis → granulomatous 1α-hydroxylase activity in macrophages.
Why the Answer is Correct
Activated macrophages in sarcoid granulomas express 1α-hydroxylase that converts 25-OH vitamin D to 1,25-(OH)₂D (calcitriol) independent of PTH regulation. Excess calcitriol increases intestinal Ca absorption → PTH-independent hypercalcemia → secondary PTH suppression.
Cognitive Demand
Explain hypercalcemia in a patient with bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy and suppressed PTH
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Suppressed PTH + hypercalcemia + bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy + interstitial infiltrates = sarcoidosis. The mechanism is 1-alpha-hydroxylase in granuloma macrophages converting 25-OH vitamin D to calcitriol (1,25-OH2 D3).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Macrophage 1α-hydroxylase → calcitriol → hypercalcemia.
(B)
Local bone metastases. Would not give bilateral hilar adenopathy + interstitial pattern, and PTH would also be low but the imaging fits sarcoid.
Local bone resorption by metastases. Causes hypercalcemia in solid tumor malignancies via OAFs (osteoclast activating factors). It would produce lytic lesions on bone scan, not bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy with interstitial infiltrates. The lung pattern here is granulomatous, not metastatic.
(C)
PTHrP. Squamous cell lung CA / renal CA paraneoplastic; CXR pattern differs.
PTHrP (parathyroid hormone-related peptide) secretion. Causes humoral hypercalcemia of malignancy (most commonly squamous cell carcinoma of lung, renal cell carcinoma). PTHrP mimics PTH but is not measured by standard PTH assays. The PTH would be suppressed (as here), but the clinical context (hilar lymphadenopathy + interstitial infiltrates) points to sarcoidosis, not a solid malignancy.
(D)
PTH secretion. Primary hyperparathyroidism. PTH would be high, not low.
Secretion of parathyroid hormone. Would cause hypercalcemia with ELEVATED PTH (primary hyperparathyroidism). This patient has a suppressed PTH. PTH overproduction is ruled out by the low PTH value.
(E)
TSH. TSH doesn't cause hypercalcemia (thyrotoxicosis can mildly via bone turnover, but not this picture).
TSH secretion by the thyroid. Causes hyperthyroidism, which can cause mild hypercalcemia through increased bone turnover. This patient has normal TSH-related symptoms, and bilateral hilar lymphadenopathy is not explained by thyroid pathology.
Costume Change
Reappears as: sarcoidosis-associated hypercalcemia worsens with sun exposure (vitamin D synthesis in skin + granuloma 1-alpha-hydroxylase = double hit). Or: treatment of sarcoid hypercalcemia → corticosteroids (suppress granuloma formation and 1-alpha-hydroxylase). Or: sarcoidosis findings. Non-caseating granulomas, elevated ACE, uveitis, erythema nodosum, Löfgren syndrome.
Variable Pivot
If calcium elevated + PTH elevated → primary hyperparathyroidism. If calcium elevated + PTH suppressed + elevated PTHrP → malignancy (humoral hypercalcemia). If calcium elevated + PTH suppressed + calcitriol elevated + hilar adenopathy → sarcoidosis. If calcium elevated in patient receiving thiazide diuretics → thiazides reduce urine calcium → mild hypercalcemia.
Tid BitSarcoid hypercalcemia → treat with steroids (block 1α-hydroxylase) and sun avoidance.
Q049 Block 3 Answer: C
GIGastroenterology

Chronic pancreatitis. Exocrine insufficiency

Vignette Summary
young man → recurrent acute pancreatitis since age 21 → steatorrhea (oily, foul, floating), weight loss → BMI 18 → which enzyme to replace?
The Discriminator
Steatorrhea = fat malabsorption → lipase is the rate-limiting enzyme of fat digestion and the most rapidly destroyed in chronic pancreatitis.
Why the Answer is Correct
Chronic pancreatitis destroys acinar cells; lipase secretion drops earliest and most steeply (\>90% loss before symptoms), producing fat maldigestion → steatorrhea and ADEK deficiency. Pancreatic enzyme replacement therapy (pancrelipase) is dosed by lipase units and is the cornerstone of treatment.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the enzyme replacement most likely to help a patient with chronic pancreatitis causing exocrine insufficiency
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Oily, foul-smelling stools + weight loss + recurrent pancreatitis = exocrine pancreatic insufficiency = fat malabsorption = lipase deficiency is the primary therapeutic target.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Amylase. Starch digestion; salivary amylase + brush-border enzymes compensate, so deficiency rarely causes symptoms.
Amylase deficiency impairs carbohydrate (starch) digestion. Carbohydrate malabsorption produces bloating, flatulence, and osmotic diarrhea. But the stools are watery, not oily or foul-smelling. Exocrine pancreatic insufficiency primarily affects fat digestion because lipase is most sensitive to damage by chronic pancreatitis.
(B)
Lactase. Brush-border enzyme of the small intestine; deficiency causes osmotic diarrhea with dairy, not chronic steatorrhea after pancreatitis.
Lactase deficiency. Causes lactose intolerance with gas, bloating, and watery diarrhea after dairy consumption. It is NOT caused by chronic pancreatitis. Lactase is a brush border enzyme of the small intestinal enterocytes, not a pancreatic enzyme.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Lipase. Replacing lipase reverses steatorrhea.
(D)
Protease. Trypsin/chymotrypsin loss matters less; protein digestion preserved by gastric pepsin and brush-border peptidases.
Protease deficiency impairs protein digestion. While chronic pancreatitis does reduce protease output, protein malabsorption is less clinically prominent than fat malabsorption. Amino acids can be partially absorbed from dipeptides via small intestinal transporters even with reduced pancreatic protease. The primary symptom driver in pancreatic exocrine insufficiency is fat malabsorption.
(E)
Tryptase. Mast-cell enzyme; not a digestive enzyme.
Tryptase. Is a mast cell protease. It is not a pancreatic digestive enzyme. It has no role in fat or protein digestion.
Costume Change
Reappears as: steatorrhea (oily floating stools) differential. Pancreatic exocrine insufficiency (chronic pancreatitis, CF) vs celiac disease vs bacterial overgrowth. Or: Sudan stain for fat globules in stool → confirms fat malabsorption. Or: 72-hour fecal fat collection for quantification.
Variable Pivot
If the patient were a child with recurrent sinopulmonary infections + steatorrhea → cystic fibrosis → pancreatic insufficiency → pancreatic enzyme replacement. If the steatorrhea followed terminal ileum resection → bile salt deficiency → fat malabsorption. If celiac disease → gluten-free diet resolves steatorrhea without enzyme replacement.
Tid BitPancreatic exocrine failure = lipase first, lipase worst.
Q050 Block 3 Answer: C
Infectious DiseaseParasitology

Amebic liver abscess. Luminal agent

Vignette Summary
man from India → RUQ pain, fever, hypoechoic liver lesion → Entamoeba histolytica abscess → why add paromomycin to metronidazole?
The Discriminator
Metronidazole kills invasive trophozoites in tissue but does not eradicate luminal cysts in the colon → relapse and continued shedding. Paromomycin (a luminal amebicide) clears intestinal cysts.
Why the Answer is Correct
Paromomycin is a non-absorbed aminoglycoside that stays in the gut lumen and kills the cyst stage of E. Histolytica, preventing recurrence and transmission. Standard regimen: metronidazole (tissue) followed by a luminal agent (paromomycin or iodoquinol).
Cognitive Demand
Explain why paromomycin is added to metronidazole for hepatic amebiasis
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Metronidazole treats invasive disease (liver abscess, amebic colitis), but does NOT eradicate the intestinal cyst form. Paromomycin is a luminal agent that eliminates the intestinal reservoir.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Avoid resistance. Combination is not for resistance prevention; it's stage- specific.
Drug resistance prevention. Is important in infectious disease but is not the rationale for combining metronidazole with paromomycin. These drugs target different niches (tissue vs lumen), not different mechanisms against the same target.
(B)
Liver penetration. Paromomycin is poorly absorbed; metronidazole is the agent that penetrates tissue/liver.
Liver penetration. Is the ADVANTAGE of metronidazole, not paromomycin. Paromomycin is NOT well absorbed systemically. It stays in the gut lumen. This is precisely why it treats intestinal amebiasis but not hepatic disease. This answer has the mechanism backwards.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Eradication of intestinal organisms. Clears luminal cysts.
(D)
CNS prevention. Amebic CNS disease is rare and not the rationale here.
CNS infection prevention by paromomycin. Is not a recognized rationale. Paromomycin doesn't penetrate tissues well, so it would be a poor choice for preventing CNS disease.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Entamoeba histolytica treatment strategy. Metronidazole for invasive disease (hepatic abscess, amebic dysentery) + luminal agent (paromomycin or iodoquinol or diloxanide) to eradicate intestinal cysts and prevent transmission/relapse. Or: distinguish E. Histolytica (invasive, pathogenic) from E. Dispar (non-pathogenic colonizer).
Variable Pivot
If the patient had only intestinal symptoms (dysentery, no liver abscess) → metronidazole + luminal agent. If hepatic abscess alone → metronidazole first, then luminal agent to clear intestinal carriage. If the question asked about Giardia → metronidazole (tinidazole also effective). If Cryptosporidium in an HIV patient → nitazoxanide.
Tid BitTreat invasive amebiasis: metronidazole for the tissue, paromomycin for the lumen.
Q051 Block 3 Answer: D
OncologyPediatrics

Rhabdomyosarcoma

Vignette Summary
3-yr-old → painful proptosis → orbital mass involving extraocular muscles → malignant cells with cross-striations.
The Discriminator
Striated malignant cells in a child's orbit = embryonal rhabdomyosarcoma.
Why the Answer is Correct
Rhabdomyosarcoma arises from primitive skeletal muscle (rhabdomyoblasts); embryonal subtype is the most common soft-tissue sarcoma in children and favors the head/neck, especially the orbit and GU tract. Histology shows small round blue cells, some with eosinophilic cytoplasm and cross-striations; positive for desmin and myogenin/MyoD1.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the malignancy based on orbital location + striated muscle histology in a child
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Orbital mass + malignant cells with striations in a 3-year-old = rhabdomyosarcoma. Striations = skeletal muscle origin. Orbital RMS is the most common pediatric orbital malignancy.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Neuroblastoma. Small round blue cell tumor of adrenal/sympathetic chain, Homer-Wright rosettes; no striations.
Neuroblastoma. Arises from adrenal medulla or sympathetic ganglia neural crest cells. It can cause proptosis through orbital metastasis (raccoon eyes) but the primary mass would not show striations. It shows small blue round cells and Homer-Wright rosettes. The mass here involves ocular muscles directly with striated malignant cells.
(B)
Pheochromocytoma. Adrenal medulla, catecholamine excess; not orbital.
Pheochromocytoma. Arises from adrenal medulla chromaffin cells and causes catecholamine excess (hypertension, diaphoresis, headache, palpitations). It does not present as an orbital mass in children.
(C)
Retinoblastoma. Arises from retina (intraocular), Flexner-Wintersteiner rosettes, leukocoria; no skeletal-muscle striations.
Retinoblastoma. Is the most common intraocular malignancy of childhood and presents with leukocoria (white pupillary reflex), not proptosis. It grows within the globe, not involving orbital muscles. Histology shows small blue round cells (Flexner-Wintersteiner rosettes), not striations.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Rhabdomyosarcoma. Striated tumor cells in pediatric orbit.
(E)
Thyroid cancer. Wrong anatomy, wrong histology.
Thyroid cancer. Can cause orbital involvement in Graves disease (thyroid ophthalmopathy) but this is an inflammatory process, not a malignancy of the orbit, and occurs in adults, not 3-year-olds.
Costume Change
Reappears as: rhabdomyosarcoma. Most common soft tissue sarcoma of childhood, arising from embryonal rhabdomyoblasts. Three locations: head/neck (orbital most common), genitourinary (bladder, vagina), extremities. Or: histological subtypes. Embryonal (most common, better prognosis) vs alveolar (PAX-FOXO1 fusion, worse prognosis).
Variable Pivot
If leukocoria (white pupillary reflex) in an infant → retinoblastoma (RB1 mutation). If small blue round cells in adrenal mass → neuroblastoma. If hypertension + diaphoresis + adrenal mass → pheochromocytoma. If soft tissue mass in extremity with necrosis → rhabdomyosarcoma vs Ewing sarcoma (t[11;22]).
Tid BitKid + proptosis + cross-striations = embryonal rhabdomyosarcoma. Look for PAX-FOXO1 in alveolar subtype.
Q052 Block 3 Answer: E
Infectious DiseaseMicrobiology

Necrotizing soft-tissue infection. Group A Strep / S. Aureus

Vignette Summary
carpenter → puncture wound, then 4 days later fever, tachycardia, hypotension, necrotic wound, leukocytosis, respiratory alkalosis (sepsis) → most likely organism.
The Discriminator
Skin puncture → rapidly progressive necrotizing soft-tissue infection with sepsis in an immunocompetent adult. Staphylococcus aureus (per key) is the most common skin/wound pathogen, including MRSA causing toxin-mediated severe disease.
Why the Answer is Correct
S. Aureus is the leading cause of purulent skin and soft-tissue infections, including abscess, cellulitis with necrosis, and toxic-shock-like syndromes (TSST-1 superantigen → massive cytokine release → fever, hypotension, shock). Puncture-wound inoculation with delayed presentation and necrosis fits.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most likely organism causing sepsis from a wound in a carpenter
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Puncture wound from wood + rapidly progressing infection with necrosis + systemic sepsis → Staphylococcus aureus is the most common cause of soft tissue infections from skin flora colonization of puncture wounds.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
C. Tetani. Would cause trismus/rigidity, not necrotic cellulitis with septic shock.
Clostridium tetani. Causes tetanus. Wound contamination with spores from soil, producing tetanospasmin toxin that blocks inhibitory neurotransmitter release. Tetanus presents with trismus (lockjaw), risus sardonicus, opisthotonus. NOT systemic sepsis and necrotic wound in 45 minutes.
(B)
M. Abscessus. Chronic, indolent skin nodules; not 4-day fulminant sepsis.
Mycobacterium abscessus. Is a rapidly growing nontuberculous mycobacterium causing chronic skin and soft tissue infections, especially in immunocompromised patients or after cosmetic procedures. The acute presentation (45 minutes from injury to ED) is too rapid for mycobacterial infection, which typically takes days to weeks to manifest.
(C)
Pasteurella multocida. Cat/dog bites with rapid cellulitis; not a carpentry puncture.
Pasteurella multocida. Causes wound infections from dog and cat bites (not carpenter tools). It presents rapidly but is associated specifically with animal bite wounds.
(D)
Pseudomonas aeruginosa. Classic for nail-through-sneaker osteomyelitis or burn/immunocompromised wounds, not the typical first answer for an immunocompetent carpenter's hand puncture.
Pseudomonas aeruginosa. Causes infections typically in immunocompromised patients, burn patients, or associated with water exposure. While it can cause wound infections, the carpenter setting and rapid progression are more consistent with S. Aureus.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Staphylococcus aureus. Most common cause of wound infection with rapid progression and toxin-mediated sepsis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: S. Aureus is the most common cause of skin and soft tissue infections, septic arthritis, and endocarditis. Or: MRSA vs MSSA distinction. Community-acquired MRSA (USA300 strain) often causes skin abscesses; nosocomial MRSA requires vancomycin. Or: which toxin causes toxic shock syndrome? → TSST-1 (S. Aureus) → T cell superantigen → massive cytokine release.
Variable Pivot
If cat or dog bite → Pasteurella multocida (C). If soil contamination + gas in tissue on imaging → Clostridium perfringens (gas gangrene). If diabetic foot wound with polymicrobial infection → broad-spectrum coverage including anaerobes. If water exposure → Pseudomonas or Aeromonas.
Tid BitWound + sepsis + necrosis in an adult? Cover S. Aureus (including MRSA) first.
Q053 Block 3 Answer: C
NeurologySpinal Cord Anatomy

Central cord syndrome

Vignette Summary
Post-MVC → bilateral intrinsic hand weakness/atrophy (LMN), spastic legs (UMN) with hyperreflexia and Babinski, sensory loss to pinprick at and below clavicles (C4 dermatome).
The Discriminator
LMN signs in hands + UMN signs in legs + cape-like sensory loss = central cord syndrome; intrinsic hand muscles are innervated by T1 (ulnar/median).
Why the Answer is Correct
Hyperextension injury damages central gray matter and crossing spinothalamic fibers, producing cape-like loss of pain/temperature and LMN findings at the level of injury. Intrinsic hand muscles (interossei, thenar, hypothenar) originate from C8-T1 ventral horn; their LMN denervation localizes the lesion to T1. Lateral corticospinal tracts below the lesion are interrupted → spastic paraparesis with Babinski.
Cognitive Demand
Localize the lesion to a spinal cord level based on the distribution of upper and lower motor neuron signs
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The key is identifying the HIGHEST level affected. Upper extremity LMN signs (atrophy, weakness 3/5) + lower extremity UMN signs + sensory level at clavicle = lesion at C5 level? No. The LMN findings in the HANDS with UPPER extremity involvement and NORMAL biceps/triceps reflex places it at C8/T1 (intrinsic hand muscles). But the sensory level at the clavicle = C4-ish. Wait. The question says DECREASED reflexes at biceps and triceps (which are C5/C6 and C7 respectively) but INCREASED at knees and ankles. This means the lesion must be above the knee but affecting the hand. Normal biceps (C5/C6) + normal triceps (C7) + weakness in HANDS (intrinsic muscles = C8/T1) + UMN in legs → lesion at C8-T1. But wait, the question says DTRs are NORMAL at biceps and triceps. So the lesion is below C7 but above T1. The sensory level "at and below the clavicle" = approximately C4-C5 level. This question is actually about a CENTRAL CORD syndrome or a specific lesion. Let me re-read: weakness of BOTH HANDS + atrophy of INTRINSIC HAND MUSCLES + weakness + increased tone in lower extremities + increased DTRs at knees/ankles + Babinski bilateral + sensation absent at and below clavicles. The clavicle is at approximately C4-C5 level. Normal biceps (C5/C6) and triceps (C7) but weakness and atrophy of intrinsic hand muscles (C8/T1). This is a CENTRAL CORD SYNDROME or lesion at C7-C8 level. The answer is C7 based on the hand muscle involvement with the sensory level being just below the hands.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
C5. Deltoid/biceps; would cause shoulder weakness, not intrinsic hand atrophy.
C5 lesion. Would produce LMN signs in the deltoid and biceps (shoulder/proximal arm atrophy) with preserved distal arm function. Intrinsic hand muscle weakness (small muscles of the hand) reflects C8/T1 anterior horn involvement. A C5 lesion cannot explain intrinsic hand muscle atrophy.
(B)
C7. Triceps/wrist extension; some hand involvement but not intrinsics.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
T1. Intrinsic hand muscles + cape-like sensory level at clavicles.
T1 is at the axillary level, not the clavicle. A T1 lesion would produce LMN signs in the very small intrinsic hand muscles but the sensory level would be at the axilla, not the clavicle. Also, biceps and triceps are both above T1. A T1 lesion would produce UMN signs in biceps and triceps, not the normal reflexes described.
(D)
T3 / (E) T5. Too low; would spare the hands entirely.
T3 would be below the level needed to explain hand muscle involvement. A T3 lesion would produce UMN signs in ALL extremities with a sensory level at the chest. Intrinsic hand muscle weakness requires anterior horn involvement at C8-T1.
Costume Change
Reappears as: central cord syndrome (most common cervical spinal cord injury). Arms weaker than legs + sacral sparing. Or: Brown-Séquard syndrome (hemisection). Ipsilateral UMN + proprioception loss, contralateral pain/temperature loss. Or: anterior cord syndrome. Bilateral motor loss + pain/temperature loss, preserved proprioception.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had posterior column signs (proprioception/vibration loss) predominating → posterior cord syndrome or subacute combined degeneration. If only anterior signs (motor + pain/temp loss, preserved proprioception) → anterior cord syndrome (anterior spinal artery occlusion). If hemisensory loss on opposite side → Brown-Séquard.
Tid BitHand LMN + leg UMN + cape = central cord at C8-T1.
Q054 Block 3 Answer: D
PulmonologyPulmonary Function Tests

Obesity hypoventilation. Restrictive extrapulmonary pattern

Vignette Summary
33-yr-old, BMI 46, exertional dyspnea, clear lungs, SpO₂ 98% → PFT pattern.
The Discriminator
Extrapulmonary restriction from chest-wall/abdominal mass loading → ↓FVC, ↓TLC, preserved FEV1/FVC ratio, and normal DLCO (lung parenchyma is intact).
Why the Answer is Correct
Morbid obesity reduces chest-wall and diaphragmatic compliance → small lung volumes (low FVC, low TLC) with proportionate FEV1 drop (ratio preserved, not obstructive). Parenchyma and capillary bed are normal, so DLCO is normal/near-normal. Row D: FEV1 84, FVC 78, TLC 78, DLCO 92. The classic extrapulmonary restrictive pattern.
Cognitive Demand
Match the PFT pattern to obesity-related restrictive lung disease
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Obese patient with exertional dyspnea + preserved O2 sat = pure restrictive pattern from abdominal fat restricting chest wall excursion. FEV1/FVC ratio is NORMAL or elevated (preserved), total lung capacity decreased, DLCO normal.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
FEV1 19, FVC 40, TLC 110, DLCO 35. Severe obstruction with hyperinflation and low DLCO = emphysema.
FEV1 19%, FVC 40% (FEV1/FVC = 48%) with TLC 110% and DLCO 35%. Severe airflow obstruction (low FEV1/FVC) with hyperinflation (TLC > 100%) and reduced DLCO = severe emphysema/COPD. This obese patient has no smoking history and normal O2 sat. COPD is not the diagnosis, and TLC would be decreased (not increased) in obesity.
(B)
All \~40 with low DLCO. Interstitial lung disease (intrinsic restriction).
FEV1 43%, FVC 40% (FEV1/FVC = 100%) with TLC 40% and DLCO 28%. Severely reduced TLC with preserved ratio but very low DLCO = combined restrictive pattern with significantly impaired gas exchange. This pattern fits pulmonary fibrosis with extensive parenchymal loss. Not simple obesity. Obesity restriction preserves the DLCO because the alveoli themselves are normal.
(C)
Modestly reduced FEV1 with normal TLC. Mild obstruction.
FEV1 65%, FVC 76% (FEV1/FVC = 86%) with TLC 96% and DLCO 70%. This is a relatively normal PFT with mild airflow limitation and normal TLC. This would be a mild asthmatic or a normal person. The clinical picture here is of a 400-pound man with dyspnea, which should show more restriction.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Restrictive volumes with normal DLCO = obesity / chest wall.
(E)
All normal. No disease.
FEV1 95%, FVC 102%, TLC 98%, DLCO 89%. These are completely normal PFTs. This does not fit a 400-pound patient with dyspnea on exertion.
Costume Change
Reappears as: pure restrictive pattern in obesity = decreased FVC, decreased TLC, PRESERVED FEV1/FVC, NORMAL DLCO (alveoli are intact, just can't expand). Contrast with interstitial fibrosis = decreased TLC + decreased DLCO (alveolar walls thickened). Or: obese patient with hypercapnia + hypoxia → obesity hypoventilation syndrome (Pickwickian).
Variable Pivot
If DLCO is low with restrictive pattern → interstitial lung disease (fibrosis). If obstructive pattern → COPD or asthma. If FEV1/FVC < 0.7 that normalizes with bronchodilator → asthma. If FEV1/FVC < 0.7 that does NOT normalize → COPD.
Tid BitRestrictive + normal DLCO = extrapulmonary (obesity, neuromuscular, chest wall).
Q055 Block 3 Answer: C
OBGYNMenopause Physiology

Postmenopausal vaginal atrophy

Vignette Summary
67-yr-old, menopause 16 yrs ago → decreased libido, otherwise healthy marriage and life → pelvic exam finding.
The Discriminator
Chronic estrogen deficiency → vaginal atrophy → loss of rugae, thinning epithelium, decreased lubrication.
Why the Answer is Correct
Estrogen maintains vaginal epithelial thickness, glycogen content, lubrication, and rugation. Years after menopause, hypoestrogenism produces genitourinary syndrome of menopause: pale, thin, smooth (loss of rugae) mucosa, decreased lubrication, and dyspareunia. A common physiologic contributor to decreased libido.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most likely pelvic exam finding in a postmenopausal woman with decreased libido
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Postmenopausal + decreased libido + sexual activity present → think vaginal atrophy (genitourinary syndrome of menopause). Estrogen loss → vaginal epithelial thinning → loss of rugation.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Anterior vaginal wall bulge. Cystocele; symptomatic with urinary symptoms (none here).
Bulging of the anterior vaginal wall through the introitus = cystocele (anterior vaginal prolapse, bladder herniation). This can cause urinary frequency and incomplete bladder emptying. While more common in parous postmenopausal women, cystocele is not the primary finding explaining decreased libido without urinary symptoms.
(B)
Posterior vaginal wall bulge. Rectocele; bowel symptoms (none here).
Bulging of the posterior vaginal wall = rectocele (posterior vaginal prolapse, rectal herniation). This causes difficulty with defecation. Again, this patient has normal bowel movements and the complaint is libido, not defecatory dysfunction.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Decreased vaginal rugation. Hallmark of atrophic vaginitis.
(D)
Palpable ovaries. Postmenopausal palpable ovary is abnormal (concern for malignancy) and not expected as a "most likely" finding.
Palpable ovaries in a postmenopausal woman. Are ABNORMAL. Ovaries should be small and non-palpable after menopause. Palpable postmenopausal ovaries raise concern for ovarian malignancy and require workup. This would be an alarming finding, not the most likely finding explaining decreased libido.
(E)
Uterine size 8 cm. Postmenopausal uterus is small/atrophic (\<8 cm typical), not enlarged.
Uterine size of 8 cm. Is normal to slightly enlarged. Normal uterine size is approximately 7-8 cm long. Postmenopausal uterus should actually be SMALLER than this (typically 5-6 cm). An 8 cm postmenopausal uterus raises concern for endometrial pathology.
Costume Change
Reappears as: genitourinary syndrome of menopause (GSM) includes vaginal dryness, dyspareunia, decreased libido, urinary urgency, and recurrent UTIs. Treatment: vaginal estrogen (low systemic absorption, safe even in breast cancer survivors for local symptoms), lubricants for mild symptoms.
Variable Pivot
If patient had postmenopausal bleeding → rule out endometrial cancer first. If palpable ovary → transvaginal ultrasound + CA-125. If urinary incontinence → stress (cystocele/urethral hypermobility) vs urge (detrusor overactivity). If dyspareunia + vaginal pH > 4.5 → GSM confirmed.
Tid BitPostmenopausal smooth, pale vagina without rugae → topical estrogen.
Q056 Block 3 Answer: E
EndocrinologyCommunication

Patient interviewing. Open-ended question

Vignette Summary
Long-standing T1DM, A1c 12%, patient apologetic → most appropriate first response.
The Discriminator
Open-ended, non-judgmental exploration of patient's understanding beats accusatory or closed questions.
Why the Answer is Correct
Effective counseling begins with the patient's perspective ("Tell me what you understand…"). It is open-ended, respects autonomy, identifies knowledge gaps, and avoids blame. A foundational motivational-interviewing technique.
Cognitive Demand
Select the appropriate initial response to a patient expressing helplessness about poor glycemic control
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The patient says "I hope that's okay. I haven't been able to get it lower". This is an expression of struggle and possible shame. The correct response explores her understanding and experience before lecturing or problem-solving.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
"Do you skip insulin?". Closed, slightly accusatory; premature.
"Do you ever skip a dose of insulin?" immediately implies noncompliance and is accusatory. It jumps to problem identification before understanding the patient's experience or goals.
(B)
"Noncompliance can be deadly". Fear-based, judgmental, labels the patient.
"Noncompliance in your disease can be deadly" is paternalistic, frightening, and shaming. It attacks the patient's behavior before understanding her situation and is likely to produce defensiveness, not behavior change.
(C)
"Inform me of recent traumas… contributing to your poor compliance". Assumes noncompliance; uses pejorative term.
"Please inform me of any recent traumas" is better but frames the poor control as primarily the patient's fault (trauma or stress causing "poor compliance") and still assumes noncompliance before exploring what she understands about the A1c result.
(D)
"Tell me about the foods you eat". Open but narrows scope before assessing patient understanding.
"Tell me about the kinds of foods you eat" jumps straight to dietary advice without first understanding what the patient knows about her A1c value, her goals, or her perspective on her disease management.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
"What is your understanding of what your A1c should be?". Open, assesses baseline knowledge, sets up shared decision-making.
Costume Change
Reappears as: patient with poorly controlled chronic disease expressing shame or helplessness → first response is empathic exploration of understanding and experience, not problem-solving or education. The correct opener lets the patient define the problem.
Variable Pivot
If the patient expressed suicidal ideation about her disease burden → address safety first. If the patient had no insurance and couldn't afford insulin → address systemic barrier (financial assistance programs). If A1c were 12% with signs of poor adherence AND a knowledge gap → THEN educate, but only after establishing rapport.
Tid BitAlways assess the patient's understanding before delivering information.
Q057 Block 3 Answer: D
BiostatisticsResearch Methods

Increasing sample size

Vignette Summary
RCT of finasteride enlarges from 500 to 1500 patients → statistical effect.
The Discriminator
Larger n → higher statistical power → lower β → less chance of Type II error (false negative).
Why the Answer is Correct
Power = 1 − β. Increasing sample size narrows confidence intervals and improves the ability to detect a true effect; it directly reduces Type II error. Type I error (α) is set by the investigator (e.g., 0.05) and is not changed by sample size.
Cognitive Demand
Predict the effect of increasing sample size on type I and type II error
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Increasing sample size increases POWER (decreases type II error = beta). It does not change the type I error rate (alpha), which is set by the researcher, not by sample size.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Greater Type I. α is fixed by threshold, not affected by n.
Greater chance of Type I error. Is incorrect. Type I error rate (alpha) is determined by the statistical significance threshold chosen by the investigators (typically 0.05). It is not affected by sample size. A larger sample does not make false positives more likely.
(B)
Greater Type II. Opposite of truth.
Greater chance of Type II error. Is incorrect. Type II error (failing to detect a true effect = false negative) DECREASES with more subjects. A larger study has more power to detect a true difference.
(C)
Less Type I. α unchanged.
Less chance of Type I error. Is incorrect. Alpha stays at 0.05 regardless of sample size. The researcher controls this; the sample size does not.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Less Type II. More power → fewer false negatives.
(E)
Impossible to predict. It is predictable.
Impossible to predict. Is incorrect. The statistical relationship is well-defined: larger sample → more power → less Type II error (beta). The direction is predictable.
Costume Change
Reappears as: power calculation questions. What determines power? Sample size + effect size + alpha level + variability. Or: study with negative result. Could be true negative OR underpowered (Type II error). Or: increasing alpha to 0.1 → decreases Type II error but increases Type I error.
Variable Pivot
If the investigators changed alpha from 0.05 to 0.01 → decreased Type I error, increased Type II error. If the effect size were larger → same sample size, more power, less Type II error. If the study used one-tailed vs two-tailed test → one-tailed has more power but higher risk of missing effect in opposite direction.
Tid BitBigger n → more power → less β → fewer false negatives. α stays put.
Q058 Block 3 Answer: D
UrologyAnatomyRadiology

Ureteropelvic junction obstruction

Vignette Summary
Middle-aged woman → recurrent UTIs, left flank pain → left hydronephrosis on US → MRI shows dilated renal pelvis with normal-caliber ureter below.
The Discriminator
Dilation stops at the level where the renal pelvis meets the ureter = ureteropelvic junction (UPJ) obstruction.
Why the Answer is Correct
UPJ obstruction is the most common congenital cause of hydronephrosis and may present in adulthood. Functional or anatomic narrowing (often aberrant lower-pole vessel or intrinsic stenosis) blocks urine flow from the pelvis to the proximal ureter → dilated pelvis/calyces with a normal ureter distally. Urinary stasis predisposes to recurrent UTIs and flank pain.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the location of obstruction causing left-sided hydronephrosis based on MRI anatomy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The MRI shows hydronephrosis of the ENTIRE collecting system (renal pelvis dilated) without ureteral dilation described below a specific point. This localizes to the ureteropelvic junction (UPJ), the most common site of UPJ obstruction.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Bladder neck. Would dilate both ureters and bladder.
Bladder neck obstruction. Would cause bilateral hydronephrosis and hydroureter because the obstruction is below where both ureters enter the bladder. Unilateral left-sided hydronephrosis without right involvement rules out bladder neck obstruction.
(B)
Mid ureter. Entire pelvis + proximal ureter dilated; here ureter is normal.
Mid-ureter obstruction (e.g., from a stone, external compression) would show hydronephrosis AND hydroureter above the level of obstruction, with normal caliber ureter below. The MRI description focuses on renal collecting system dilation without prominent ureteral dilation extending down the ureter.
(C)
Renal calyx. Focal calyceal dilation only; not whole-pelvis hydronephrosis.
Renal calyx obstruction. Would cause dilation of a specific calyx (not the entire collecting system). This would be an infundibular stenosis or stone obstructing a single calyx. The left renal collecting system as a whole is affected here.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
UPJ. Pelvis dilated, ureter normal.
(E)
UVJ. Would dilate the entire ureter to the bladder (hydroureter + hydronephrosis).
Ureterovesical junction obstruction. Would also cause unilateral hydronephrosis with a dilated ureter down to the bladder. The entire ureter would be dilated on imaging. The MRI here shows isolated collecting system dilation most consistent with the UPJ.
Costume Change
Reappears as: UPJ obstruction is the most common cause of hydronephrosis in children (congenital stenosis) and presents with flank pain after large fluid intake (beer-drinker's sign). Or: ureteroscopy vs PCNL vs pyeloplasty for UPJ obstruction management. Or: recurrent UTIs + unilateral hydronephrosis → UPJ obstruction until proven otherwise.
Variable Pivot
If bilateral hydronephrosis + poor urinary stream in elderly man → posterior urethral valves in boys, BPH in older men, bladder neck obstruction. If unilateral dilation of kidney + ureter → ureterovesical junction or mid-ureteral obstruction. If isolated calyceal dilation → infundibular stenosis or calyceal stone.
Tid BitUPJ = dilated pelvis, normal ureter. UVJ = dilated pelvis and ureter.
Q059 Block 3 Answer: E
ImmunologyInfectious Disease

Mendelian susceptibility to mycobacterial disease

Vignette Summary
13-yr-old → osteomyelitis and septic arthritis with disseminated M. Avium complex in an HIV- negative host → immune defect?
The Discriminator
Disseminated nontuberculous mycobacteria in an otherwise healthy child without HIV = defect in the IL-12 / IFN-γ axis (often IFN-γ receptor).
Why the Answer is Correct
Macrophages controlling intracellular mycobacteria depend on IFN-γ signaling: infected macrophages release IL-12, which drives Th1/NK cells to secrete IFN-γ, which binds the IFN-γ receptor (IFNGR1/2) on macrophages → STAT1 activation → enhanced phagolysosome killing. Loss-of- function in IFN-γR (or IL-12/IL-12R, STAT1) produces Mendelian susceptibility to mycobacterial disease (MSMD) with disseminated NTM and BCG-osis.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the immunological defect responsible for disseminated Mycobacterium avium complex (MAC) infection in an HIV-negative child
NBME Trap Fingerprint
MAC is an opportunistic pathogen that only causes disseminated disease in profound immunodeficiency. In an HIV-negative child with MAC osteomyelitis, the specific defect is in the IL-12/IFN-gamma axis. The major pathway for macrophage activation against intracellular organisms.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Absent C5. Neisseria susceptibility, not mycobacteria.
Absent expression of C5 (terminal complement deficiency). Causes susceptibility to encapsulated organisms. Particularly Neisseria meningitidis. MAC is an intracellular pathogen handled by cell-mediated immunity, not complement. Complement deficiencies do not explain mycobacterial susceptibility.
(B)
Bilobed neutrophils with abnormal granules. Chédiak-Higashi/specific granule deficiency; recurrent pyogenic infections, not selective mycobacteria.
Bilobed neutrophil nuclei with abnormal granule morphology. Describes Chediak-Higashi syndrome, caused by LYST gene mutation. Chédiak-Higashi produces giant granules in neutrophils, recurrent bacterial infections, partial albinism, and peripheral neuropathy. It does not specifically produce disseminated MAC, and granule morphology is the distinguishing finding, not IFN-gamma signaling.
(C)
Decreased CD8. Not the mycobacterial defense limb.
Decreased absolute CD8+ T-lymphocyte count would suggest HIV infection or certain combined immunodeficiencies. The question specifically states the HIV test is negative. CD8+ depletion is associated with viral susceptibility, not the specific pattern of disseminated MAC.
(D)
Decreased IgG. Pyogenic encapsulated bacteria; not NTM.
Decreased serum IgG (hypogammaglobulinemia / XLA or CVID). Causes susceptibility to encapsulated bacterial organisms and enteroviral infections. Antibodies are not the primary defense against intracellular mycobacteria.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Dysfunctional IFN-γ receptors. MSMD.
Costume Change
Reappears as: the IFN-gamma/IL-12 axis. Macrophages present antigen → T cells/NK cells produce IFN-gamma → activates macrophages to kill intracellular organisms (Mycobacteria, Salmonella, Listeria). Defects: Mendelian susceptibility to mycobacterial disease (MSMD). Or: HIV-positive patient with MAC → prophylaxis when CD4 < 50 with azithromycin.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had recurrent encapsulated bacterial infections → B cell defect or complement deficiency. If recurrent Neisseria infections → terminal complement deficiency (C5-C9). If the patient had mucocutaneous candidiasis predominantly → T cell defect or STAT3 mutation. If HIV positive with CD4 < 50 + MAC bacteremia → disseminated MAC in HIV (completely different epidemiology).
Tid BitOtherwise-healthy kid with disseminated NTM/BCG → check the IL-12/IFN-γ axis.
Q060 Block 3 Answer: G
PathologyOncologyWound Healing

Keloid scar (myofibroblast-driven dermal fibroproliferation)

Vignette Summary
53F with a recurrent enlarging firm mass at the site of a prior chest excision. Histology shows dense, haphazardly arranged eosinophilic collagen bundles and spindle-shaped cells in the dermis. Lesion extends beyond the original wound margin.
The Discriminator
Recurrent firm mass at a previous surgical site on the upper chest, with dermal type I and III collagen overgrowth extending past the original scar, equals keloid. The cell driving the bulk and the collagen is the myofibroblast.
Why the Answer is Correct
Keloids form when wound healing fails to switch off. TGF-beta and PDGF signaling keep dermal fibroblasts differentiating into myofibroblasts. These cells synthesize abundant disorganized type I and III collagen and continue past the boundary of the original wound. Classic sites are upper chest, shoulders, earlobes, and back. Excision alone recurs in well over half of cases, which is why this patient is back. First-line treatment is intralesional triamcinolone. Refractory keloids can be excised with adjuvant radiation or pressure therapy.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cell type producing a spindle-cell mass at a prior biopsy site. A desmoid tumor / fibromatosis
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Spindle cells at a biopsy site with interlacing fascicles and collagen = myofibroblastic proliferation = desmoid tumor/fibromatosis. Myofibroblasts are the cells responsible for wound contraction and, when dysregulated, for desmoid tumors.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Macrophages. Granulomas/foreign-body reactions; not a recurrent spindle-cell mass.
Macrophages produce granulomas (non-caseating or caseating) or foam cell collections. A macrophage-driven mass would show epithelioid histiocytes, giant cells, and central necrosis. The spindle cell fascicular pattern here is not macrophage morphology.
(B)
Mast cells. Urticaria pigmentosa/mastocytoma; different histology.
Mast cells cause urticaria pigmentosa and systemic mastocytosis. Mast cell tumors contain round cells with metachromatic granules on special staining. They do not produce the spindle cell fascicular pattern with abundant collagen seen here.
(C)
Melanocytic cells. Melanoma; pigmented, atypical melanocytes.
Melanocytic cells produce melanoma and benign nevi. Melanoma can present as a spindle cell variant but would show melanin production, S100 positivity, and typical melanocytic markers. A subcutaneous recurrent mass at a biopsy site without skin involvement is not the typical melanoma presentation.
(D)
Mesothelial cells. Mesothelioma; pleural/peritoneal.
Mesothelial cells line the pleural, pericardial, and peritoneal cavities. Mesothelioma presents as diffuse pleural or peritoneal thickening, not as a discrete subcutaneous chest mass at a biopsy site. Mesothelioma is associated with asbestos exposure.
(E)
Myeloblasts. AML; bone marrow tumor.
Myeloblasts. Are precursor cells in AML. They do not produce subcutaneous mass lesions in isolation and would be found in bone marrow and blood, not as a discrete soft tissue tumor at a biopsy site.
(F)
Myocytes. Leiomyosarcoma/rhabdomyosarcoma; striated/SMA but with different morphology.
Myocytes (skeletal or cardiac muscle cells). Would produce rhabdomyosarcoma (in children) or rhabdomyoma. These cells show cross-striations. The H&E description of interlacing fascicles with fibrous stroma describes a mesenchymal fibroblastic/myofibroblastic tumor, not a rhabdomyosarcoma.
(G)
CORRECT ANSWER
Myofibroblasts. Driver of keloid and DFSP recurrences.
Costume Change
Reappears as: desmoid tumors (fibromatosis). Locally aggressive, non-metastasizing fibroblastic/myofibroblastic tumors. Associated with FAP/Gardner syndrome (APC mutation). Or: scar/keloid vs desmoid. Keloid is exuberant scar formation, remains in skin. Desmoid grows in deep soft tissue and can infiltrate.
Variable Pivot
If mass had S100+ spindle cells in skin → schwannoma or neurofibroma (neural crest). If mass had cross-striations → rhabdomyosarcoma (F). If mass showed foam cells → xanthoma. If the patient had FAP (familial adenomatous polyposis) → desmoid tumors are part of Gardner syndrome.
Tid BitKeloid versus hypertrophic scar: keloid grows past the wound boundary and stays raised for years. Hypertrophic stays inside the wound and tends to soften with time. Both are myofibroblast-driven. Both respond to intralesional steroid before considering excision.
Q061 Block 4 Answer: C
PharmacologyObstetricsTeratology

ACE inhibitor fetopathy

Vignette Summary
36F HTN on lisinopril, amenorrhea 6 mo → previously unrecognized pregnancy at \~24 wk → oligohydramnios, fetal skull deformity, pericardial effusion, small bladder, echogenic bowel → mechanism of harm?
The Discriminator
Lisinopril during the second trimester → ACE inhibitor fetopathy with oligohydramnios.
Why the Answer is Correct
Fetal kidneys depend on angiotensin II to maintain efferent arteriolar tone and glomerular filtration pressure. ACE inhibitors cross the placenta and abolish AT-II-mediated efferent constriction, dropping fetal GFR and urine output. Because amniotic fluid in the 2nd/3rd trimester is fetal urine, output falls → oligohydramnios → Potter sequence features (cranial deformation, limb contractures, pulmonary hypoplasia). Renal tubular dysgenesis and hypotensive ischemia also occur.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the fetal mechanism of ACE inhibitor toxicity in pregnancy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
ACE inhibitors are contraindicated in pregnancy (especially 2nd/3rd trimester) because they reduce fetal renal perfusion → oligohydramnios → fetal compression → Potter sequence features.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Fetal lung/epithelial differentiation. Wrong territory; not the ACEi target.
Fetal lung/epithelial differentiation. Is disrupted by vitamin A analogs (retinoids, isotretinoin) causing cleft palate and ear abnormalities, or by corticosteroids. ACE inhibitors do not disrupt epithelial differentiation.
(B)
Fetal lung/surfactant development. Half-truth; pulmonary hypoplasia here is secondary to oligohydramnios, not a direct surfactant defect.
Surfactant development. Is impaired by corticosteroids (paradoxically. Prenatal corticosteroids ENHANCE surfactant in premature infants, but excessive exposure can impair). ACE inhibitors do not target surfactant synthesis.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Fetal renal hemodynamics. ACEi blocks fetal efferent arteriolar AT-II tone → ↓GFR → oligohydramnios.
(D)
Maternal placental perfusion. Adjacent (cocaine, preeclampsia); lisinopril's harm is fetal-renal, not uteroplacental ischemia.
Maternal placental perfusion. Is affected by NSAIDs (which reduce prostaglandin-mediated vasodilation at the ductus venosus) or by antiphospholipid syndrome. ACE inhibitor toxicity is specifically on FETAL renal function, not maternal placental circulation.
(E)
Maternal prostaglandin synthesis. NSAID mechanism (ductal closure, oligohydramnios from a different route); not ACEi.
Maternal prostaglandin synthesis inhibition by NSAIDs. Causes premature closure of the ductus arteriosus, oligohydramnios, and renal dysplasia. The mechanism is similar to ACE inhibitors but ACE inhibitors work through the renin-angiotensin system on fetal kidney arterioles.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Potter sequence (oligohydramnios → limb contractures, compressed facies, pulmonary hypoplasia) from any cause of reduced fetal urine output. Bilateral renal agenesis, ACE inhibitor, or urinary tract obstruction. Or: ACE inhibitor fetopathy = the full Potter sequence features + skull ossification defects.
Variable Pivot
If the question mentioned warfarin → fetal warfarin syndrome (nasal hypoplasia, stippled epiphyses, CNS abnormalities). If thalidomide → phocomelia (limb shortening). If isotretinoin → cardiac defects, ear abnormalities, CNS defects. If DES (diethylstilbestrol) → clear cell adenocarcinoma of vagina in female offspring.
Tid BitACE inhibitors in pregnancy = no pee, no fluid, no lungs.
Q062 Block 4 Answer: C
PulmonologyCardiologyPathology

Acute pulmonary edema from cardiogenic shock

Vignette Summary
64F sudden respiratory distress, dies in 30 min → autopsy lung micrograph (pink intra-alveolar transudate ± hemosiderin) → underlying cause?
The Discriminator
Acute, frothy, protein-poor intra-alveolar edema with no inflammation = acute cardiogenic pulmonary edema.
Why the Answer is Correct
A massive left-ventricular MI abruptly raises LV end-diastolic pressure, backing pressure up through the pulmonary veins and capillaries. Once hydrostatic pressure exceeds plasma oncotic pressure, capillaries leak ultrafiltrate into alveoli → flash pulmonary edema and rapid hypoxemic death. Histology shows eosinophilic transudate filling alveoli, engorged capillaries, and (in subacute cases) "heart failure" hemosiderin-laden macrophages.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the underlying cause of death from autopsy histology showing specific lung pathology
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The autopsy image shows pink proteinaceous material filling alveolar spaces with congested capillaries. Pulmonary edema from a massive acute MI causing acute left heart failure.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Bilateral bronchopneumonia. Wrong; would show neutrophilic exudate filling alveoli, fever, days-long course.
Bilateral bronchopneumonia. Would show consolidation with alveoli filled with neutrophils and bacteria, not the pink transudate of cardiogenic pulmonary edema. Bronchopneumonia has an infectious inflammatory infiltrate.
(B)
Bronchial asthma. Wrong; mucus plugs, Curschmann spirals, eosinophils, smooth-muscle hypertrophy.
Bronchial asthma. Shows hyperinflated lungs with mucus plugging, Curschmann spirals, Charcot-Leyden crystals, and eosinophilic infiltrate. Not alveolar edema fluid. Asthmatic deaths show different gross and microscopic findings.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Massive myocardial infarction. Acute LV pump failure → pulmonary edema → sudden death.
(D)
Thromboembolus in bronchial artery. Bronchial circulation supplies airways; occlusion is usually clinically silent due to dual supply.
Thromboembolus in bronchial artery. Would cause pulmonary infarction. Wedge-shaped hemorrhagic consolidation peripherally with preserved lung architecture centrally. The bronchial arteries supply lung parenchyma; infarction from bronchial artery occlusion is rare.
(E)
Viral pneumonia. Lymphocytic interstitial infiltrate with hyaline membranes; not a sudden-death picture.
Viral pneumonia. Produces interstitial thickening, lymphocytic infiltrate, and hyaline membrane formation. Not the pink transudate filling the alveoli seen in cardiogenic pulmonary edema.
Costume Change
Reappears as: sudden cardiac death from massive MI → acute pulmonary edema → dyspnea, pink frothy sputum, crepitations. Or: autopsy findings at different time points after MI. First 6 hours (gross: normal), 6-24 hours (coagulative necrosis begins), 1-3 days (neutrophil infiltration), 1-2 weeks (granulation tissue), 2+ months (scar).
Variable Pivot
If autopsy showed organizing pneumonia pattern → cryptogenic organizing pneumonia or subacute pneumonia. If neutrophil-filled alveoli with bacteria → lobar or bronchopneumonia. If hyaline membranes → ARDS or hyaline membrane disease of the newborn. If hemorrhagic wedge infarct → pulmonary embolism.
Tid BitFrothy pink alveolar fluid + sudden death = the heart, not the lung.
Q063 Block 4 Answer: E
PharmacologyDrug Interactions

Rifampin-warfarin interaction

Vignette Summary
78M on warfarin (mechanical valve) starts rifampin + cipro → INR drops, dose doubled but PT/ INR still therapeutic-low.
The Discriminator
Rifampin is a potent inducer of CYP enzymes (2C9 metabolizes warfarin).
Why the Answer is Correct
Rifampin activates the pregnane X receptor, transcriptionally upregulating CYP3A4, CYP2C9, and P-glycoprotein. Increased CYP2C9 clears the more active S-warfarin enantiomer faster, lowering plasma warfarin and INR despite a doubled dose. Effect takes days to develop and persists 1-2 weeks after rifampin is stopped.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism reducing warfarin efficacy in a patient started on rifampin
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Rifampin is the strongest CYP enzyme inducer in clinical medicine. Induction of CYP2C9 accelerates S-warfarin metabolism → decreased warfarin levels → subtherapeutic INR despite doubled dose.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased protein binding. Would transiently raise free warfarin and INR, not lower it.
Decreased protein binding. Would INCREASE free warfarin concentration → increase INR, not decrease it. If warfarin were displaced from albumin, you would see an acute rise in INR (warfarin effect), not the failure seen here.
(B)
Eradication of gut flora. Cipro can ↓ vitamin K-producing flora → ↑INR (opposite direction).
Eradication of gut flora by antibiotics reduces vitamin K synthesis by intestinal bacteria → INCREASES warfarin effect → elevated INR. Ciprofloxacin in this regimen might have this effect, but the dominant interaction here is rifampin induction overwhelming all other effects.
(C)
Increased alcohol intake. Chronic alcohol induces CYP2E1 (not warfarin's); acute use inhibits metabolism → ↑INR.
Increased alcohol intake. Chronic alcohol INDUCES CYP2E1 and can increase warfarin metabolism somewhat, but the dose is only 2 beers/day and hasn't changed. Acute heavy alcohol INHIBITS CYP enzymes and would increase INR. Neither explains the dramatic failure to respond to a doubled warfarin dose.
(D)
Increased vegetable consumption. Vitamin K from greens lowers INR, but the clue is the new drug, not diet.
Increased vegetable consumption. Increases dietary vitamin K → decreases warfarin effect (competes with warfarin's mechanism). This is a real interaction but would cause a mild to moderate INR decrease, not the dramatic resistance seen despite doubling the dose. The dominant effect here is rifampin induction.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Induction of cytochrome enzymes. Rifampin induces CYP2C9 → faster warfarin clearance → low INR.
Costume Change
Reappears as: drugs that induce CYP enzymes → reduce levels of co-administered drugs. Inducers: Rifampin, Carbamazepine, Phenytoin, Phenobarbital, St. John's Wort, Griseofulvin (mnemonic: "Ri Ca PhePhen SJW Gris"). Or: drugs that inhibit CYP → increase warfarin → increased bleeding risk. Inhibitors: Amiodarone, Fluconazole, Metronidazole, Cimetidine, SSRIs.
Variable Pivot
If amiodarone were started → CYP inhibitor → INR would RISE (reduce warfarin dose). If ciprofloxacin alone → gut flora reduction → mild INR rise. If the patient ate a large amount of spinach → vitamin K competition → INR falls. If INR suddenly fell in a patient not on rifampin → consider non-compliance or vitamin K supplementation.
Tid BitRifampin makes the liver chew through warfarin. INR plummets.
Q064 Block 4 Answer: A
OphthalmologyEpidemiology

Age-related macular degeneration

Vignette Summary
80F African American, gradual central vision loss, needs more light for fine work → fundus shows drusen/AMD → biggest risk factor?
The Discriminator
AMD's \#1 modifiable-independent risk factor after smoking is advancing age. Prevalence climbs steeply after 75.
Why the Answer is Correct
AMD is degenerative loss of the retinal pigment epithelium/Bruch membrane/ choriocapillaris complex in the macula, with accumulation of drusen (lipofuscin/complement debris). Oxidative stress and impaired RPE handling of photoreceptor outer-segment turnover accumulate over decades, making age the dominant risk factor.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the strongest risk factor for age-related macular degeneration from a clinical history
NBME Trap Fingerprint
AMD's strongest modifiable risk factor is smoking. Its strongest non-modifiable risk factor is age. Among ALL factors listed, age is the single greatest risk factor for AMD.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Age. Strongest non-modifiable risk factor; AMD is uncommon before 60 and prevalence rises sharply with each decade.
(B)
Alcohol consumption. Heavy use is a weak risk; one glass thrice weekly is not clinically meaningful.
Alcohol consumption at moderate levels (one glass with dinner 3x/week) is not a significant risk factor for AMD. Heavy alcohol use has some association with AMD in epidemiological studies but the magnitude of risk is far less than age.
(C)
Ethnicity. AMD is actually more common in non-Hispanic white patients; her ethnicity lowers risk relative to whites.
Ethnicity. AMD is more common in white/European ancestry populations than in African Americans. This patient is African American. Her ethnicity would actually be PROTECTIVE relative to white populations. Ethnicity is a risk modifier but not the dominant risk factor here.
(D)
Family history. Real risk factor (complement factor H) but smaller than age and not given here.
Family history of glaucoma. Is a risk factor for GLAUCOMA, not for AMD. AMD and glaucoma are distinct conditions with separate risk profiles. The family history of glaucoma increases her risk of open-angle glaucoma, not AMD.
(E)
Hypertension. Weak association; primarily a risk for hypertensive retinopathy, not AMD.
Hypertension. Has a modest association with AMD (cardiovascular risk factors overlap with AMD risk), but the relative risk from hypertension is far smaller than that from age. An 80-year-old has exponentially higher risk from aging than from blood pressure.
Costume Change
Reappears as: AMD prevention. Smoking cessation is the most important modifiable intervention. AREDS vitamins (vitamins C, E, beta-carotene, zinc) reduce progression from intermediate to advanced AMD. Or: AMD types. Dry (drusen, geographic atrophy) vs wet (choroidal neovascularization → anti-VEGF treatment with ranibizumab, bevacizumab).
Variable Pivot
If the patient were a smoker → smoking is the most important MODIFIABLE risk factor. If the question asked about glaucoma risk → family history + elevated IOP + African American ancestry. If the patient had new wavy distortion of straight lines → Amsler grid change → neovascular/wet AMD → urgent anti-VEGF treatment.
Tid BitDrusen + central scotoma + 80-year-old = AMD; age is the engine.
Q065 Block 4 Answer: E
HematologyOncologyImmunology

Chemo-induced neutropenia

Vignette Summary
4yo with ALL on induction (asparaginase, dexamethasone, doxorubicin, vincristine) → fever, gram-negative rod bacteremia at week 3 → mechanism?
The Discriminator
Cytotoxic induction chemotherapy ablates the marrow → absolute neutrophil count falls 7-14 days after dosing, the classic nadir window.
Why the Answer is Correct
Doxorubicin and other induction agents kill rapidly dividing marrow precursors, producing profound neutropenia (ANC \<500). Loss of the neutrophil barrier permits translocation of gut gram-negative rods (E. Coli, Klebsiella, Pseudomonas) into blood, especially in mucositis (note the gum ulcers). Pallor + alopecia confirm marrow suppression.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism of infection susceptibility in a leukemia patient on induction chemotherapy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Chemotherapy causes neutropenia. A febrile neutropenic patient growing gram-negative rods is the classic leukemia-related immunocompromise scenario. The mechanism is neutropenia from myelosuppression.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Deficiency of terminal complement. Causes recurrent Neisseria, not gut GNRs.
Deficiency of terminal complement (C5-C9). Causes susceptibility to Neisseria species (meningococcus, gonorrhea) specifically. Chemotherapy does not specifically deplete complement, and this patient grew gram-negative rods, not Neisseria specifically.
(B)
Hypogammaglobulinemia. Predisposes to encapsulated organisms (Strep pneumo, H. Flu); not the dominant defect here.
Hypogammaglobulinemia. Causes susceptibility to encapsulated bacterial organisms (Strep pneumoniae, H. Influenzae, N. Meningitidis) after the loss of opsonizing antibodies. Chemotherapy-induced immunosuppression primarily affects rapidly dividing cells. Neutrophils are the primary effectors depleted.
(C)
Impaired T-lymphocyte function. Dexamethasone contributes, but classic post-induction febrile illness is driven by neutropenia.
Impaired T-lymphocyte function. Causes susceptibility to intracellular organisms (viruses, Pneumocystis, fungi, mycobacteria) and is seen in HIV, congenital T cell defects, or anti-T-cell therapies. Induction chemotherapy for ALL causes primarily NEUTROPENIA, not selective T cell depletion.
(D)
Inhibition of TNF-α function. Mechanism of infliximab/etanercept; predisposes to TB reactivation.
Inhibition of TNF-alpha function. Occurs with biologics like infliximab and etanercept, causing susceptibility to granulomatous organisms (TB, histoplasma, coccidioides). These drugs are not part of ALL induction regimens.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Neutropenia. Chemo-induced marrow aplasia → loss of phagocyte defense → GNR bacteremia.
Costume Change
Reappears as: febrile neutropenia protocol. ANC < 500 + fever → urgent blood cultures → empiric broad-spectrum antibiotics (antipseudomonal beta-lactam). Do NOT wait for culture results. Or: prophylaxis in neutropenia → G-CSF (filgrastim) to shorten neutropenic period, fluconazole for fungal prophylaxis, TMP-SMX for PCP prophylaxis.
Variable Pivot
If the immunocompromised patient had PCP pneumonia → T cell defect (AIDS, post-transplant). If Aspergillus infection → prolonged neutropenia OR steroid use OR transplant. If recurrent Neisseria infections → complement deficiency (A). If hypogammaglobulinemia + recurrent sinopulmonary infections → XLA or CVID (B).
Tid BitFever + chemo + nadir = neutropenic fever until proven otherwise.
Q066 Block 4 Answer: A
BiostatisticsClinical Epidemiology

Absolute risk reduction

Vignette Summary
RCT of two cesarean closures; infection 12/1000 (A) vs 18/1000 (B) → calculate ARR.
The Discriminator
ARR = control risk − treatment risk (simple subtraction of incidence proportions).
Why the Answer is Correct
Absolute risk reduction = (event rate in comparator) − (event rate in intervention) = 18/1000 − 12/1000 = 6/1000 (0.6%). Enrollment fraction (85%) and relative risk reduction are different measures and do not enter the ARR formula.
Cognitive Demand
Calculate absolute risk reduction correctly from given infection rates
NBME Trap Fingerprint
ARR = Risk in control group - Risk in treatment group. Simple subtraction of the event rates. Do not multiply by enrollment rate (85%). That is a participation rate, not a probability modifier for the outcome.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
(18/1000) − (12/1000). Definition of ARR.
(B)
0.85 × (12/1000). Mis-applies the enrollment proportion; not an ARR.
0.85 × (12/1000) multiplies the Procedure A infection rate by the study enrollment percentage. This confuses the participation rate with the treatment effect. The 85% enrollment rate tells you how many patients were recruited. It does not change the per-patient risk of wound infection in those who received the procedure.
(C)
0.85 × (18/1000). Same error.
0.85 × (18/1000) makes the same error, applying the enrollment fraction to the control event rate. The absolute risk reduction formula uses the event rates directly, not weighted by participation.
(D)
\[0.85 × (18/1000 − 12/1000)\]. Adds an irrelevant 0.85 weighting.
0.85 × (18/1000 - 12/1000) applies the enrollment fraction to the difference in rates. Again, the enrollment percentage is irrelevant to the ARR calculation. ARR is purely the difference in risk between the two groups.
(E)
\[(1.2/100) − (1.8/100)\]/(1.8/100). That formula (with a sign flip) is relative risk reduction, not ARR.
[(1.2/100) - (1.8/100)] / (1.8/100) calculates RELATIVE RISK REDUCTION (RRR), not absolute risk reduction. RRR = ARR / control risk. This formula was asked in some previous NBME forms; recognize the denominator as the identifier that this is RRR.
Costume Change
Reappears as: ARR, RRR, and NNT calculations. ARR = control rate - treatment rate. RRR = ARR / control rate. NNT = 1 / ARR. Example here: ARR = 18/1000 - 12/1000 = 6/1000 = 0.006. NNT = 1/0.006 ≈ 167 patients need to undergo Procedure A instead of B to prevent 1 additional wound infection.
Variable Pivot
If asked for RRR → (18-12)/18 = 33%. If asked for NNT → 1/ARR = 1/0.006 ≈ 167. If the rates were given as percentages → same formula but convert. If asked for odds ratio → (12/988) / (18/982) ≈ 0.66.
Tid BitARR is just plain subtraction; RRR is the fraction.
Q067 Block 4 Answer: B
HematologyOncology

Acute myelogenous leukemia (Auer rods)

Vignette Summary
67M smoker, fatigue/bruising 3 wk, now fever + gum bleeding + pneumonia; WBC 24,500 with "monocytes" 50%, plt 18k, Hgb 10; smear shows blasts with Auer rods.
The Discriminator
Auer rods (azurophilic, needle-shaped MPO crystals) on a blood smear in an older adult = AML.
Why the Answer is Correct
AML is clonal expansion of myeloid blasts that fail to differentiate. Marrow replacement causes pancytopenia clinically: anemia (fatigue), thrombocytopenia (gum bleeds, petechiae, ecchymoses), and functional neutropenia (pneumonia despite high WBC because the cells are nonfunctional blasts). The "monocyte" count is really a flag for misclassified blasts. Auer rods are pathognomonic.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the leukemia type from peripheral smear + CBC differential in an elderly smoker
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The smear shows large monocytoid cells. High monocyte count (50%) + high lymphocytes (40%) with blasts in context of age, smoking, and acute presentation = AML M5 (acute monocytic leukemia). The photomicrograph shows myeloid blasts with monocytic differentiation.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Acute lymphocytic leukemia. Pediatric disease; TdT+ lymphoblasts, no Auer rods.
Acute lymphocytic leukemia (ALL). Is predominantly a disease of children (peak age 2-5) and presents with lymphoblasts on smear. Small cells with scant cytoplasm and fine chromatin. The 67-year-old age, heavy smoking history, and monocyte-predominant differential make ALL very unlikely.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Acute myelogenous leukemia. Auer rods + pancytopenia + elderly.
(C)
Chronic lymphocytic leukemia. Smudge cells, mature B-lymphocyte lymphocytosis, indolent course; no Auer rods.
Chronic lymphocytic leukemia (CLL). Presents with extremely elevated WBC (often > 50,000-100,000), predominantly small, mature-appearing lymphocytes, and smudge cells on smear. It has an indolent course. Patients typically don't present acutely ill with fever and DIC-like picture. The platelet count of 18,000 and acute presentation argue against CLL.
(D)
Chronic myelogenous leukemia. Massive leukocytosis with full myeloid spectrum and basophilia, low LAP; no Auer rods, less pancytopenia.
Chronic myelogenous leukemia (CML). Presents with an extremely elevated WBC with the full spectrum of maturing myeloid cells (myelocytes, metamyelocytes, bands, mature neutrophils). A left shift across all stages. CML is caused by BCR-ABL (Philadelphia chromosome t[9;22]). This patient's monocyte-dominant differential with blasts is not the CML pattern.
Costume Change
Reappears as: AML subtypes. M0/M1/M2 (myeloid), M3 (promyelocytic, t[15;17], AUER RODS + DIC), M4 (myelomonocytic), M5 (monocytic, gum infiltration), M6 (erythroid), M7 (megakaryocytic). Or: DIC in AML → particularly M3 (promyelocytic) from granule release. Or: Auer rods = pathognomonic for AML.
Variable Pivot
If smear showed Auer rods → AML, specifically M3 if DIC present. If smear showed smudge cells → CLL. If smear showed ALL blast (child) → ALL → treat with multiagent regimen including vincristine, prednisone, L-asparaginase. If t(9;22) → CML → imatinib (BCR-ABL inhibitor).
Tid BitAuer rods = AML, period.
Q068 Block 4 Answer: D
LipidologyCardiologyGenetics

Familial hypercholesterolemia

Vignette Summary
45M with early MI; multiple first-degree relatives dead of MI \<50; tendinous xanthomas, Achilles thickening; LDL-laden lipid panel (TC 410, normal TG).
The Discriminator
Tendon xanthomas + extreme LDL + autosomal dominant family pattern = heterozygous familial hypercholesterolemia.
Why the Answer is Correct
FH (type IIa) is autosomal dominant loss of functional LDL receptors on hepatocytes. Without receptor-mediated clearance, plasma LDL accumulates (heterozygotes \~2× normal LDL; total chol 300-500). Macrophages take up oxidized LDL via scavenger receptors → tendon xanthomas, xanthelasma, and premature atherosclerosis/MI before age 50.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the specific molecular defect in familial hypercholesterolemia causing premature MI with tendinous xanthomas
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Young patient + very high LDL + tendinous xanthomas + family history of premature MI = familial hypercholesterolemia = LDL receptor deficiency (autosomal dominant).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Apo B48. Chylomicron synthesis; loss = abetalipoproteinemia (fat malabsorption, acanthocytes), not hypercholesterolemia.
Apo B48 deficiency. Affects chylomicron assembly in intestinal cells. Chylomicrons carry dietary fat. Deficiency causes fat malabsorption and low chylomicrons. It does not cause the marked LDL elevation seen in familial hypercholesterolemia.
(B)
Apo C. Apo C-II is the LPL cofactor; deficiency → ↑chylomicrons/TG, not LDL.
Apo C deficiency. Would affect LDL-C differently. Apo CII is required to activate lipoprotein lipase. ApoCII deficiency causes severe hypertriglyceridemia (chylomicronemia syndrome) with eruptive xanthomas and pancreatitis. Not the tendinous xanthomas and extreme LDL elevation seen here.
(C)
HMG-CoA reductase activity. Deficiency would lower cholesterol; this is the statin target, not an FH cause.
HMG-CoA reductase activity deficiency. Would actually LOWER cholesterol production. The opposite of what is occurring. HMG-CoA reductase is the rate-limiting enzyme for endogenous cholesterol synthesis. Inhibiting it (statins) lowers LDL. Loss of activity would cause decreased cholesterol, not the extreme hypercholesterolemia here.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
LDL receptor. Heterozygous FH; impaired hepatic LDL clearance.
(E)
Lipoprotein lipase activity. Type I hyperlipidemia → high TG/ chylomicrons, eruptive xanthomas, pancreatitis; TG is normal here.
Lipoprotein lipase activity deficiency. Causes chylomicronemia. Severe hypertriglyceridemia, eruptive xanthomas on buttocks and extensor surfaces, pancreatitis, and milky plasma. The cholesterol is predominantly triglycerides, not LDL. This patient has extreme LDL elevation, not hypertriglyceridemia.
Costume Change
Reappears as: FH presentation. Corneal arcus + tendinous xanthomas (Achilles, extensor tendons of hands) + premature cardiovascular disease in family. Or: which statin works in FH? → High-intensity statins + ezetimibe + PCSK9 inhibitors (evolocumab, alirocumab). Or: FH heterozygote (1/500 people) vs homozygote (1/250,000, extremely severe).
Variable Pivot
If hypertriglyceridemia + pancreatitis + eruptive xanthomas → LPL deficiency or ApoC2 deficiency. If high LDL + increased VLDL → familial combined hyperlipidemia. If high LDL alone → FH (LDL receptor) or familial defective ApoB100.
Tid BitTendon xanthomas + teenage cholesterol = LDL receptor is broken.
Q069 Block 4 Answer: C
PediatricsUrology

Primary nocturnal enuresis

Vignette Summary
Otherwise-well 5yo boy with persistent nighttime wetting (never dry overnight); normal exam, normal UA, normal growth.
The Discriminator
Primary monosymptomatic nocturnal enuresis in a young child with normal workup → developmental.
Why the Answer is Correct
Primary nocturnal enuresis reflects a maturational lag in the cortical/brainstem control of detrusor contraction and external urethral sphincter coordination during sleep, often combined with reduced nocturnal ADH secretion and a small functional bladder capacity. Most children outgrow it by school age (\~15% per year resolve spontaneously).
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most likely cause of primary nocturnal enuresis in a healthy 5-year-old boy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Key word: NEVER achieved full bladder control at night, but achieved daytime continence. This is primary enuresis = developmental delay in nocturnal detrusor/sphincter control. Normal exam + normal UA + no structural findings = maturational delay.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Abnormal terminal ureter insertion. Ectopic ureter causes continuous dribbling/daytime wetness, not isolated nighttime enuresis.
Abnormal insertion of the terminal ureter (ectopic ureter) in girls causes continuous dribbling of urine despite normal voiding. Because the ureter inserts below the urethral sphincter. This would cause CONTINUOUS dampness, not episodic nocturnal wetting with normal daytime control. Also not seen in boys (ectopic ureter in boys inserts above the sphincter into seminal vesicles).
(B)
Bladder colonization with recurrent UTI. UA is normal; no dysuria, urgency, or fever.
Bladder colonization causing recurrent UTI. Would cause dysuria, frequency, urgency, and possibly fever. Not asymptomatic nocturnal enuresis. The dipstick UA is completely normal here.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Delayed maturation of detrusor and sphincter control. Classic primary nocturnal enuresis.
(D)
Occult neurologic lesion. Would expect leg findings, sacral dimple, abnormal reflexes, or bowel involvement.
Occult neurologic lesion causing neurogenic bladder. Would produce abnormal findings on examination (sacral dimple, tuft of hair over spine, asymmetric reflexes, or gait abnormality). This child has a completely normal neurological and physical examination.
(E)
Vesicoureteral reflux. Presents with febrile UTIs and pyelonephritis, not isolated enuresis.
Vesicoureteral reflux (VUR). Causes urine to flow from the bladder back into the ureter during micturition, predisposing to pyelonephritis. VUR would cause recurrent febrile UTIs, not simple nocturnal enuresis. The UA is normal.
Costume Change
Reappears as: primary nocturnal enuresis management. First try behavioral interventions (fluid restriction before bed, scheduled voiding). If persistent → moisture alarm (most effective long-term). Desmopressin (ADH analog) for short-term use (overnight stays, camps). Or: enuresis alarm mechanism. Conditions child to wake with bladder fullness sensation.
Variable Pivot
If the child had daytime AND nighttime incontinence → consider structural/neurological cause. If constant urinary dribbling in a girl → ectopic ureter. If UTI symptoms → urine culture + renal ultrasound to rule out VUR. If spinal findings → neurogenic bladder from spinal dysraphism.
Tid BitDry by day, wet by night, normal exam = wait. The brain just needs to catch up.
Q070 Block 4 Answer: A
PediatricsSurgeryGI

Rectal prolapse from chronic constipation

Vignette Summary
5yo, pebble-like stools every 5 days, painful BMs, no recent BM today, exam shows 1 cm of bright-red rectal mucosa protruding through anus.
The Discriminator
Repeated straining against hard stool in a healthy child → mucosal rectal prolapse.
Why the Answer is Correct
Chronic constipation forces prolonged Valsalva against a poorly supported pelvic floor in young children.
