Increased intra-abdominal pressure pushes rectal mucosa through the relatively weak childhood anal sphincter, producing a red doughnut of mucosa visible at the anus. Hard stool can also abrade mucosa, explaining the bright-red blood on the tissue.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cause of a 5-year-old boy's rectal mucosal prolapse + chronic constipation
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Hard, pebble-like stools + rectal prolapse + 5-year-old + constipation = simple constipation-driven rectal prolapse. This is common in children with straining. The rectal prolapse reduced here is the pink mucosal tissue protruding.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Constipation. Straining → rectal mucosal prolapse and anal fissure bleeding.
(B)
Cystic fibrosis. Causes rectal prolapse too, but accompanied by failure to thrive, steatorrhea, recurrent pulmonary infections. None here; BMI is normal.
Cystic fibrosis. Causes rectal prolapse from malabsorption and malnutrition in young children. But CF patients also have recurrent pulmonary infections, failure to thrive, and fat malabsorption (steatorrhea). This child has isolated constipation with a normal weight (5th/10th percentile for his height suggests possible short stature but BMI is 50th, meaning proportionate). The clinical picture doesn't suggest CF malabsorption.
(C)
Hirschsprung disease. Failure to pass meconium, "explosive" stool on rectal exam, distention from birth; not a 5-year-old presentation.
Hirschsprung disease. Presents in NEWBORNS with failure to pass meconium in the first 48 hours, bilious vomiting, and abdominal distension. Aganglionic bowel from the internal anal sphincter extending proximally. It would not present de novo as constipation at age 5 with a history of prior normal bowel movements. The hard, pebble-like stool at age 5 is functional constipation.
(D)
Hookworm. Causes iron-deficiency anemia and pruritus, no prolapse; absent travel history.
Hookworm infestation (Necator americanus, Ancylostoma) causes iron deficiency anemia from blood loss and GI symptoms but primarily watery/non-bloody diarrhea, not the hard constipation pattern described here. No travel history or exposure risk is mentioned.
(E)
Intussusception. Currant-jelly stools, intermittent colicky pain, sausage mass, age 6 mo-3 yr.Tid-Bit: Pebble stools + a red ring at the anus = strain-induced prolapse.
Intussusception. Presents acutely in infants (6-36 months) with intermittent severe colicky abdominal pain, currant jelly stools, and a sausage-shaped abdominal mass. It does not cause chronic constipation followed by rectal prolapse.
Costume Change
Reappears as: functional constipation in children. Most common cause of rectal prolapse in children. Treatment: polyethylene glycol (Miralax), increased fiber and fluids, toilet training habits. Or: red flags in pediatric constipation. Hirschsprung (delayed meconium, no fecal soiling, "explosion" pattern, empty rectum on DRE), spinal dysraphism.
Variable Pivot
If rectal prolapse in a young child with malabsorption + pulmonary symptoms → CF. If neonate with failure to pass meconium → Hirschsprung. If acute abdominal pain + currant jelly stools in infant → intussusception. If the prolapse recurred despite treating constipation → investigate structural cause.
Tid BitPebble stools + a red ring at the anus = strain-induced prolapse.
Q071 Block 4 Answer: D
GeneticsProbability

CF carrier risk in unaffected sibling

Vignette Summary
27F's brother has CF (autosomal recessive). She is unaffected. Risk she is a carrier?
The Discriminator
Among the unaffected offspring of two obligate carriers (Aa × Aa), genotypes are 1 AA : 2 Aa : 1 aa. Removing the affected aa leaves 2 Aa : 1 AA → 2/3 carrier probability.
Why the Answer is Correct
Both parents must be heterozygotes (Aa) to have produced an affected son. Conditional on being unaffected, the probability of being a heterozygous carrier is 2/3 (and homozygous normal 1/3).
Cognitive Demand
Calculate carrier probability for cystic fibrosis in a woman whose sibling is affected
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Autosomal recessive: parents must both be carriers (Aa × Aa). Offspring probabilities: 1/4 AA, 2/4 Aa, 1/4 aa. Since this woman (III-5) is UNAFFECTED, she cannot be aa. Of the remaining 3/4, 2 are carriers (Aa). So 2/3 of unaffected offspring are carriers.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
1/2. That's the carrier risk for offspring of one known carrier × non-carrier, not this situation.
1 of 2 would be correct if the carrier probability were determined by a single parent being a carrier (Aa × AA cross). In the cross Aa × AA: offspring are 1/2 AA + 1/2 Aa, so unaffected offspring have 1/2 chance of being carriers. But CF requires BOTH parents to be carriers (Aa × Aa) since it's autosomal recessive. The question establishes that both parents are obligate carriers.
(B)
1/3. Probability she is AA (homozygous normal), the complement.
(C)
1/4. Unconditional probability of being affected from a carrier × carrier cross.
1 of 4 is the probability of an AFFECTED offspring (aa) from a carrier × carrier mating. This is the risk of having an affected child, not the probability that an unaffected sibling is a carrier.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
2/3. Unaffected sibling of a recessive proband.
(E)
3/4. Probability of being unaffected (AA + Aa), not of being a carrier.
3 of 4 is the probability of being UNAFFECTED (either AA or Aa). This includes both carriers and non-carriers among unaffected offspring. It doesn't answer the specific question of what fraction of unaffected siblings are carriers.
Costume Change
Reappears as: bayesian genetics questions. When a sibling is affected with AR disease → parents are obligate carriers → unaffected sibling has 2/3 carrier probability. Or: CF carrier screening → 1/25 in Caucasians. Or: what is the risk that an unaffected sibling of a CF patient will have an affected child? → (2/3) × (1/25) × (1/4) ≈ 1/150.
Variable Pivot
If the question asked about dominant inheritance → 1/2 risk of being affected. If X-linked recessive → unaffected female relative of an affected male has 2/3 carrier probability (same logic). If consanguineous couple both with family history → use carrier frequencies from population genetics.
Tid BitUnaffected sib of an AR proband = 2/3 carrier.
Q072 Block 4 Answer: A
CardiologyPharmacology

Cardiogenic shock. Anterior STEMI

Vignette Summary
45F crushing chest pain, HR 136, SBP 70, anterior STEMI (V1-V3); want to ↑BP without ↑HR.
The Discriminator
Need vasoconstriction without chronotropy → pure α1 agonist (phenylephrine).
Why the Answer is Correct
Selective α1-adrenergic agonists (e.g., phenylephrine) constrict arteriolar smooth muscle → ↑SVR → ↑MAP. Because they don't stimulate β1 receptors, heart rate is not driven up; in fact a reflex baroreceptor bradycardia often occurs. Already-tachycardic patients in cardiogenic shock benefit by raising coronary perfusion pressure without worsening myocardial O₂ demand.
Cognitive Demand
Select the drug class that raises blood pressure without increasing heart rate in an anterior STEMI with cardiogenic shock
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The patient has hypotension + tachycardia. Need to raise BP. A beta agonist would raise HR further. A pure alpha agonist raises BP by vasoconstriction WITHOUT affecting HR (or may reflexively lower it via baroreceptor response).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
α-Adrenergic agonist. Raises BP via vasoconstriction without increasing HR.
(B)
α-Adrenergic antagonist. Lowers BP; opposite of what's needed.
Alpha-adrenergic ANTAGONIST (e.g., prazosin, phenoxybenzamine) would cause vasodilation → lower BP → worsen the cardiogenic shock. This is the wrong direction.
(C)
β- Adrenergic agonist. Dobutamine/epinephrine raise HR (β1). Contraindicated when goal is no chronotropy.
Beta-adrenergic AGONIST (e.g., dobutamine, isoproterenol) increases heart rate and contractility. While dobutamine can increase BP in cardiogenic shock, it would also increase HR. Specifically contraindicated in this patient who already has a rate of 136/min and an anterior MI (increased oxygen demand is dangerous).
(D)
β-Adrenergic antagonist. Lowers HR but also lowers contractility and BP; dangerous in cardiogenic shock.
Beta-adrenergic ANTAGONIST (e.g., metoprolol) reduces heart rate and contractility. In acute MI with cardiogenic shock and hypotension, beta blockade would worsen hemodynamic instability and is contraindicated in cardiogenic shock.
(E)
Dopamine agonist. Dopamine at higher doses activates β1 and α1 → ↑HR; not "pure" pressor.
Dopamine agonism at low doses (1-5 mcg/kg/min, D1 receptors) causes renal vasodilation, at medium doses (5-10, beta1) increases HR and contractility, and at high doses (>10, alpha1) causes vasoconstriction. Dopamine at high doses would raise BP and HR. The question asks for BP increase WITHOUT HR increase. Pure alpha agonist is cleaner.
Costume Change
Reappears as: phenylephrine vs norepinephrine vs epinephrine. Phenylephrine (pure alpha-1 agonist) raises BP via vasoconstriction with reflex bradycardia. Norepinephrine (alpha-1 dominant with some beta-1) raises BP + mild HR increase. Epinephrine (alpha + beta) raises BP + HR + bronchodilation.
Variable Pivot
If the patient needed both BP support and positive inotropy → norepinephrine or dobutamine + vasopressor. If the patient were in septic shock (vasodilatory) → norepinephrine (first line). If the patient had supraventricular tachycardia + hypotension → synchronized cardioversion first.
Tid BitPhenylephrine: pure α, no β. Pressure without pulse.
Q073 Block 4 Answer: D
EmbryologyUrology

Horseshoe kidney

Vignette Summary
5yo girl, recurrent UTIs, ultrasound shows a single U-shaped kidney.
The Discriminator
Horseshoe kidney = fusion of the inferior poles of the two metanephroi during ascent.
Why the Answer is Correct
During week 6-9, paired metanephroi ascend from the pelvis. If their lower poles touch as they cross the midline, they fuse. The fused mass then catches under the inferior mesenteric artery, halting ascent at L3-L4. The abnormal pelvic anatomy promotes urinary stasis → recurrent UTI; also associated with Turner syndrome and Wilms tumor.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the embryological event causing horseshoe kidney
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Horseshoe kidney = fusion of the INFERIOR poles of the two metanephric kidneys during their ascent through the pelvis. As they ascend, the inferior poles get trapped under the inferior mesenteric artery.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Failure to rotate 90° medially. Causes malrotation (renal pelvis facing anteriorly), not a single fused kidney.
Failure of rotation 90 degrees medially. Produces a "malrotated kidney" with the renal pelvis facing anteriorly rather than medially. This causes a different anatomical abnormality. The collecting system orientation is off but the kidneys themselves remain separate.
(B)
Failure of normal ascent. Yields a pelvic kidney, not a U- shape.
Failure of normal kidney ascent. Produces an ectopic kidney (pelvic kidney). A kidney that stays in the pelvis instead of ascending to the retroperitoneal lumbar position. Both kidneys would be separately located in the pelvis. A pelvic kidney is separate from horseshoe kidney, where the two kidneys fused.
(C)
Failure of one ureteric bud to develop. Renal agenesis (single kidney), not horseshoe.
Failure of one ureteric bud to develop normally. Causes renal agenesis or a dysplastic kidney on that side. This produces a single kidney on one side, not a fused horseshoe kidney.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Fusion of inferior poles of metanephros during ascent.
Costume Change
Reappears as: horseshoe kidney is the most common renal fusion anomaly. Trapped under the inferior mesenteric artery (IMA). The IMA prevents further cranial ascent. Associated with Turner syndrome, trisomy 18, and increased risk of hydronephrosis (UPJ obstruction from abnormal ureteral takeoff), UTIs, and Wilms tumor. NOT associated with increased renal cell carcinoma.
Variable Pivot
If female patient with webbed neck + horseshoe kidney → Turner syndrome (45,XO). If horseshoe kidney + UPJ obstruction → recurrent UTIs from incomplete drainage. If the kidney never ascended from the pelvis → ectopic (pelvic) kidney (B). If the ureteric bud duplicated → duplex collecting system (Y-ureter).
Tid BitHorseshoe kidney hooks under the IMA. Stuck at L3.
Q074 Block 4 Answer: C
Behavioral ScienceAdolescent Medicine

Adolescent confidentiality / open-ended response

Vignette Summary
16M voluntarily discloses scrotal lesion and worry about STI; what to say first?
The Discriminator
First step is to reinforce disclosure with a neutral, supportive, non-judgmental acknowledgment.
Why the Answer is Correct
Building rapport precedes information-gathering. A statement that affirms the patient's decision to seek care keeps the channel open before sensitive sexual-history questions. It also protects adolescent confidentiality and trust.
Cognitive Demand
Select the most appropriate initial response when a teenager presents concerned about a scrotal lesion
NBME Trap Fingerprint
This question tests the therapeutic alliance with adolescents. Expressing warmth and non-judgment FIRST creates the environment for honest disclosure. Clinical questions come second.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
"Are you sexually active with men, women, or both?". Appropriate later, but jumps to detail before establishing rapport.
"Are you sexually active with men, women, or both?" jumps directly to clinical information-gathering about sexual behavior. While this is a clinically necessary question, asking it immediately before establishing rapport sends the message that the physician is only interested in risk assessment, not in the patient as a person. It is also a potentially identity-sensitive question that should follow, not precede, rapport building.
(B)
"Does your mother know you are sexually active?". Breaches adolescent confidentiality and is judgmental.
"Does your mother know you are sexually active?" is a boundary violation that assumes sexual activity (not yet established), threatens confidentiality, and would immediately destroy the therapeutic alliance with an adolescent patient. This is textbook wrong.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
"I'm glad you came in today..". Validates disclosure; sets tone for honest history.
(D)
"What protection do you use?". Assumes details and skips rapport.
"What kind of protection do you use when you have sex?" again assumes sexual activity before it has been established. If the patient is not sexually active, this is embarrassing and off-target.
(E)
"When was your last sexual experience?". Appropriate later in the focused history.
"When was your last sexual experience?" assumes sexual activity and jumps to clinical questions. The correct sequence is: first warm acknowledgment → then establish rapport → then ask open-ended questions → then gather clinical specifics.
Costume Change
Reappears as: any adolescent who comes in with a sensitive concern. The HEADS assessment (Home, Education/Employment, Activities, Drugs, Sexuality, Suicide/Depression) is gathered gradually after establishing trust, not interrogated upfront. CRAFFT for substance use screening in adolescents.
Variable Pivot
If the adolescent expressed suicidal ideation → address safety immediately (this supersedes the rapport-building sequence). If the parent demanded to be present for the entire exam → explain that adolescent patients have a right to confidential care for sensitive topics (STIs, substance use, reproductive health). Confidentiality limits: imminent harm to self or others.
Tid BitFirst reflect, then collect. Acknowledge before you interrogate.
Q075 Block 4 Answer: D
EndocrinologyObesity Physiology

Obesity → hyperinsulinemia

Vignette Summary
54F BMI 27, BP 140/90, fasting glucose 102, strong family hx of DM2 → compared to a normal- weight peer, which lab differs?
The Discriminator
Adipose-driven insulin resistance forces β-cells to oversecrete insulin → fasting hyperinsulinemia precedes hyperglycemia.
Why the Answer is Correct
Excess adipose tissue (especially visceral) releases free fatty acids, TNF-α, and resistin, impairing insulin signaling in muscle and liver. Pancreatic β-cells compensate by raising insulin output, producing high fasting insulin with only mildly elevated glucose. The metabolic-syndrome / prediabetes signature seen here.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most likely hormonal abnormality in an overweight prediabetic woman
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Obesity → increased insulin resistance → compensatory hyperinsulinemia to maintain euglycemia. Fasting insulin is elevated even before fasting glucose becomes truly diabetic.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased cholesterol excretion. Obesity actually raises biliary cholesterol secretion (gallstone risk).
Decreased cholesterol excretion. Would be relevant in bile acid metabolism or hepatic function. Obesity is actually associated with INCREASED cholesterol secretion into bile (lithogenic bile) → cholesterol gallstones. But this is a bile/hepatic finding, not a serum hormone abnormality.
(B)
Decreased estrone concentration. Adipose aromatase converts androgens → estrone, so estrone is increased.
Decreased estrone. Obesity INCREASES estrone. Adipose tissue contains aromatase, which converts androgens to estrone (a weaker estrogen). Postmenopausal obese women have MORE circulating estrogen from peripheral aromatization. This is why obesity is a risk factor for endometrial cancer in postmenopausal women.
(C)
Decreased leptin concentration. Leptin is produced by adipocytes and rises with adiposity (leptin resistance).
Decreased leptin. Obese patients have INCREASED leptin (adipose tissue produces leptin proportional to fat mass). However, they have LEPTIN RESISTANCE. The brain doesn't respond to the elevated leptin to reduce appetite. Leptin is elevated, not decreased.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Increased fasting insulin concentration. Compensatory hyperinsulinemia from insulin resistance.
(E)
Increased growth hormone concentration. Obesity suppresses GH secretion.
Increased growth hormone. GH is actually DECREASED in obesity. Excess visceral fat impairs GH pulsatility. This is a real finding but not the most directly relevant serum abnormality linking obesity, prediabetes, and insulin resistance.
(F)
Increased TSH concentration. Mild TSH elevation can occur but is not the dominant or most characteristic finding.
Increased TSH. Hypothyroidism can cause weight gain and is associated with insulin resistance, but hypothyroidism is a distinct diagnosis. A mildly elevated TSH (subclinical hypothyroidism) can be seen in obesity, but the question asks about the most likely serum abnormality from the listed options, and hyperinsulinemia is more directly caused by obesity/insulin resistance than TSH elevation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: insulin resistance → hyperinsulinemia → eventual beta cell exhaustion → type 2 DM. Or: which test best predicts T2DM development in prediabetic patients? → Fasting insulin + HOMA-IR (homeostatic model assessment of insulin resistance). Or: PCOS in obese women → hyperinsulinemia → increased androgen production from theca cells.
Variable Pivot
If the question asked about a postmenopausal obese woman → elevated estrone from peripheral aromatization (B) becomes highly relevant. If asked about obese patient with OSA → leptin resistance + elevated leptin. If asked about adipokines → leptin elevated, adiponectin DECREASED (adiponectin is anti-inflammatory and insulin-sensitizing; it is low in obesity).
Tid BitPrediabetes lab signature: insulin rises long before glucose does.
Q076 Block 4 Answer: D
EndocrinologyMuscle Physiology

Hypokalemic periodic paralysis

Vignette Summary
17M with repeated episodes of full-body weakness after exertion when resting. Transient episodes precipitated by carbohydrate-heavy or salt-heavy meals. Normal between attacks.
The Discriminator
Carbohydrate/salt-heavy meal trigger plus post-exercise rest equals HYPOkalemic periodic paralysis. Insulin from carb load drives K+ intracellularly, serum K+ falls, muscle paralysis follows. Treatment is potassium.
Why the Answer is Correct
Hypokalemic periodic paralysis is an autosomal dominant channelopathy, most commonly CACNA1S (L-type calcium channel) and less commonly SCN4A. After a carbohydrate-heavy meal, insulin drives K+ into skeletal muscle cells. Serum K+ falls. The mutant channels paradoxically inactivate at these low extracellular K+ levels, producing sustained membrane depolarization and flaccid paralysis. Episodes last hours and resolve with potassium repletion. The classic triggers are carb load, salt load, and rest after vigorous exercise.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the electrolyte abnormality causing episodic hypokalemic periodic paralysis during exercise recovery
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Episodes triggered by carbohydrate/salt loading + strenuous exercise → hypokalemic periodic paralysis from CACNA1S or SCN4A mutation → K+ shifts intracellularly during recovery from exercise.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Calcium. Hypocalcemia gives tetany (Chvostek, Trousseau) and paresthesias, not flaccid paralysis.
Calcium. Hypocalcemia causes tetany (Chvostek, Trousseau) and paresthesias, not episodic flaccid paralysis triggered by exercise and carbohydrate ingestion. Calcium is not the relevant electrolyte here.
(B)
Chloride. Not a known cause of episodic flaccid paralysis in this pattern.
Chloride. Hypochloremia causes metabolic alkalosis (from vomiting or loop diuretics) and is not associated with episodic paralysis after exercise.
(C)
Magnesium. Hypomagnesemia gives tetany and arrhythmia.
Magnesium. Hypomagnesemia causes neuromuscular excitability (tremor, muscle cramps) and cardiac arrhythmias. It does not produce the distinct episodic pattern of paralysis triggered by carbohydrate loading and exercise recovery.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Potassium. CORRECT. Transient HYPOkalemia during attacks. Treatment is oral or IV potassium.
(E)
Sodium. Severe hyponatremia causes seizures and lethargy, not periodic post-exercise paralysis.
Sodium. Hyponatremia causes confusion, seizures, and cerebral edema when severe. It does not cause episodic, recurrent, self-limited flaccid paralysis in young athletes.
Costume Change
Reappears as: hypokalemic periodic paralysis. Trigger is CARBOHYDRATE LOAD + REST AFTER EXERCISE (insulin drives K+ into cells). Also triggered by high-sodium meals. Treatment: acute attack → oral or IV potassium. Prophylaxis → acetazolamide or potassium-sparing diuretics. Or: other causes of hypokalemic periodic paralysis: thyrotoxicosis (especially in Asian men), Conn syndrome (aldosteronism).
Variable Pivot
If the patient were Asian + hyperthyroid + same episodic paralysis → thyrotoxic periodic paralysis (treat hyperthyroidism). If the patient had periodic paralysis triggered by rest after exercise + HIGH K+ → hyperkalemic periodic paralysis (SCN4A mutation, sodium channel). If the K+ were consistently low at baseline → Conn syndrome (hyperaldosteronism), Bartter syndrome.
Tid BitCarbohydrate or salt load plus rest after exertion plus transient paralysis in a young athlete equals HYPOkalemic periodic paralysis. Give potassium.
Q077 Block 4 Answer: F
EndocrinologyNephrologyWater Physiology

Central diabetes insipidus

Vignette Summary
26M, 1 wk polyuria/polydipsia; urine osm 50 mOsm/kg; after exogenous ADH urine osm normalizes.
The Discriminator
Dilute urine that concentrates with vasopressin = central DI (ADH deficiency, not renal resistance).
Why the Answer is Correct
ADH (vasopressin) is synthesized in the magnocellular neurons of the hypothalamic supraoptic (and paraventricular) nuclei, transported axonally down the infundibulum, and stored/released by the posterior pituitary. Destruction of the supraoptic nucleus (or its axons) abolishes ADH release; collecting-duct V2 receptors remain intact, so exogenous DDAVP restores water reabsorption and urine osmolality.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the site of dysfunction causing central (pituitary) diabetes insipidus based on water deprivation test results
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Low urine osmolality + responds to exogenous ADH → central DI = ADH is not being produced. The defect is in ADH production (supraoptic nucleus) or release, not in renal responsiveness (nephrogenic DI) or renal tubule function.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Anterior pituitary. Makes ACTH/TSH/GH/FSH/LH/prolactin, not ADH.
Anterior pituitary gland dysfunction. Affects GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, and prolactin. Not ADH. ADH is produced by the hypothalamic supraoptic and paraventricular nuclei and stored/released from the POSTERIOR pituitary. Anterior pituitary failure does not cause diabetes insipidus.
(B)
Bowman capsule. Filtration only; lesion would not cause this picture.
Bowman capsule abnormality. Would affect glomerular filtration. DI is not a glomerular filtration disorder.
(C)
Glomerulus. Damage would not cause selective water loss with response to ADH.
Glomerulus dysfunction. Would cause proteinuria, hematuria, or reduced GFR. Diabetes insipidus involves normal glomerular filtration but impaired concentrating ability.
(D)
Hypophysial portal system. Carries hypothalamic releasing hormones to the anterior pituitary; ADH bypasses it.
Hypophyseal portal system carries hypothalamic releasing/inhibiting hormones to the anterior pituitary. It is not involved in ADH release, which travels directly from axon terminals in the posterior pituitary gland to the bloodstream.
(E)
Loop of Henle. Creates medullary gradient; failure causes a form of nephrogenic DI that would NOT respond to ADH.
Loop of Henle creates the medullary concentration gradient required for ADH-driven water reabsorption. A loop defect would produce nephrogenic DI. Failure to concentrate urine DESPITE normal or elevated ADH. Crucially, nephrogenic DI would NOT respond to exogenous ADH administration. This patient's urine osmolality normalizes with ADH → the kidney CAN respond → the defect is upstream, in ADH production.
(F)
CORRECT ANSWER
Supraoptic nucleus. Site of ADH synthesis; lesion → central DI corrected by exogenous ADH.
Costume Change
Reappears as: central DI (ADH deficiency) vs nephrogenic DI (ADH resistance). Water deprivation test: urine osm stays low in both. Give desmopressin (ADH analog): central DI → urine concentrates → confirms diagnosis. Nephrogenic DI → urine stays dilute → kidney can't respond to ADH. Causes: central DI (trauma, pituitary tumor, surgery), nephrogenic DI (lithium, demeclocycline, hypercalcemia, hypokalemia).
Variable Pivot
If no response to desmopressin → nephrogenic DI → treat with thiazide diuretics + low-sodium diet. If water restriction produces urine concentration → psychogenic polydipsia (not DI at all. Dilute urine from excessive water intake, not ADH deficiency). If gradual onset + visual field defect → pituitary tumor (craniopharyngioma) compressing pituitary stalk.
Tid BitCentral DI = supraoptic neurons can't make ADH; give DDAVP and the kidney listens.
Q078 Block 4 Answer: B
UrologyOncologyPathology

Seminoma

Vignette Summary
25M painless firm testicular nodule; solid on US; histology = sheets of round cells with clear cytoplasm and a central prominent nucleus.
The Discriminator
"Fried-egg" cells (clear cytoplasm, large central nucleolus) in a young man's testicle = seminoma.
Why the Answer is Correct
Seminoma is a malignant germ cell tumor analogous to ovarian dysgerminoma. Tumor cells are uniform, polygonal, glycogen-rich (hence clear cytoplasm) with a single prominent nucleolus and lymphocytic stroma. Most common testicular tumor in 15-35-year-olds; PLAP positive; exquisitely radiosensitive with excellent prognosis. β-hCG may be mildly elevated, AFP is not.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the specific testicular tumor subtype from histological description
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Round cells with clear cytoplasm + prominent nucleus in a young man with a painless testicular mass = seminoma. The most common testicular germ cell tumor.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Embryonal carcinoma. Pleomorphic, hemorrhagic, aggressive; raised AFP/β- hCG; not fried-egg morphology.
Embryonal carcinoma. Shows pleomorphic cells with prominent nucleoli forming gland-like or papillary structures. NOT uniform round cells with clear cytoplasm. Embryonal carcinoma is more aggressive and typically occurs in a slightly older age group. AFP and sometimes beta-hCG are elevated.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Seminoma. Classic histology in a young adult.
(C)
Sertoli cell tumor. Sex-cord stromal tumor; tubule-forming cells, often hormonally active; uncommon.
Sertoli cell tumor. Is a rare, usually benign stromal tumor producing testosterone or estrogen. Histology shows tubular structures lined by Sertoli cells. It does not show the round-cell clear cytoplasm pattern. Sertoli cell tumors are not germ cell tumors.
(D)
Teratoma. Multiple germ-layer tissues (cartilage, skin, gut); in adult males often malignant.
Teratoma. Contains elements from all three germ layers (skin, cartilage, gut epithelium, teeth). Histology shows disorganized tissue from multiple organ systems. Hair, teeth, cartilage. Not uniform round cells with clear cytoplasm. Mature teratoma is benign in children; immature teratoma is malignant in adults.
(E)
Yolk sac tumor. Schiller-Duval bodies; raised AFP; childhood tumor.
Yolk sac tumor. Produces AFP (often massively elevated) and shows Schiller-Duval bodies on pathology (glomeruloid structures). It is the most common testicular tumor in children (infants). Histology does not show "round cells with clear cytoplasm and prominent nucleus."
Costume Change
Reappears as: seminoma characteristics. Beta-hCG can be mildly elevated (10-15%), AFP is NEVER elevated (if AFP elevated in a testicular tumor, it cannot be pure seminoma). Seminoma is radiation-sensitive. Or: which testicular tumor marker is always negative in pure seminoma? → AFP. Or: cryptorchidism → 3-5x increased risk of seminoma.
Variable Pivot
If AFP is massively elevated → yolk sac tumor (E). If AFP + beta-hCG both elevated → mixed germ cell tumor or embryonal carcinoma. If teratoma elements visible → teratoma. If Leydig cell tumor → gynecomastia + precocious puberty in boys. If Sertoli cell tumor → feminization.
Tid BitPainless testicular mass + fried-egg cells = seminoma; bet on the radiotherapy.
Q079 Block 4 Answer: D
PediatricsTeratologyEmbryology

Fetal alcohol syndrome

Vignette Summary
2-wk male newborn, mother drank heavily throughout pregnancy without prenatal care → expected exam finding?
The Discriminator
Heavy in-utero alcohol exposure → fetal alcohol syndrome with characteristic facies.
Why the Answer is Correct
Ethanol and its metabolite acetaldehyde cause neural-crest cell apoptosis and disrupt cell migration in the developing CNS and craniofacies. The classic triad is short palpebral fissures, smooth philtrum, and thin vermillion border, accompanied by microcephaly, growth restriction, and intellectual disability.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the embryological/dysmorphological consequence of chronic maternal alcohol use
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Maternal alcohol (ethanol) = fetal alcohol spectrum disorder. The cardinal features include short palpebral fissures, smooth philtrum, thin upper lip, and cognitive/behavioral effects. Short palpebral fissures are the most specific facial feature.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Hypospadias. Multifactorial; not a hallmark of FAS.
Hypospadias. Is a urological malformation (urethral opening on ventral shaft of penis) caused by androgen deficiency or anti-androgen exposure, or associated with chromosomal anomalies. It is NOT a feature of fetal alcohol syndrome.
(B)
Limb hypoplasia. Thalidomide phocomelia or amniotic band. Not FAS.
Limb hypoplasia. Is caused by thalidomide (phocomelia), amniotic bands, or vascular disruption. Alcohol does not cause limb reduction defects.
(C)
Neck webbing. Turner syndrome (45,XO), and male sex makes this implausible.
Neck webbing. Is a feature of Turner syndrome (45,XO). Posterior cervical webbing, low hairline, short stature, coarctation. Not caused by maternal alcohol.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Short palpebral fissures. Cardinal facial dysmorphism of FAS.
(E)
Spasticity. A late neurologic finding, not the most likely exam feature in a 2- week-old.
Spasticity develops from upper motor neuron damage, which can occur with CNS injury. But in fetal alcohol syndrome, the CNS effects are primarily cognitive (ADHD, intellectual disability, learning disorders) rather than spasticity. The structural brain effects include corpus callosum hypoplasia, microcephaly.
Costume Change
Reappears as: FAS diagnostic triad. Characteristic facial features (short palpebral fissures, smooth philtrum, thin upper lip) + growth deficiency + CNS dysfunction. Or: which alcohol metabolite is teratogenic? → Acetaldehyde. Or: safe alcohol threshold in pregnancy → ZERO (no amount is established as safe). Or: FASD is the most common preventable cause of intellectual disability.
Variable Pivot
If isotretinoin → cardiac defects, craniofacial abnormalities, CNS abnormalities. If warfarin in first trimester → nasal hypoplasia, stippled epiphyses. If phenytoin → hydantoin syndrome (hypoplastic nails, digital hypoplasia, facial dysmorphism). If lithium → Ebstein anomaly (downward displacement of tricuspid valve into RV).
Tid BitFAS face: short palpebral fissures, smooth philtrum, thin upper lip.
Q080 Block 4 Answer: D
EndocrinologyThyroidPostpartum Physiology

Postpartum thyroiditis

Vignette Summary
25F, 3 mo postpartum, breastfeeding; palpitations, heat intolerance, lid lag, painless goiter, no exophthalmos; TSH ↓, T4/T3 ↑.
The Discriminator
Painless goiter + hyperthyroidism appearing in the postpartum window + no Graves eye signs = postpartum (silent) lymphocytic thyroiditis.
Why the Answer is Correct
During pregnancy maternal immune tolerance suppresses anti-thyroid antibodies; postpartum rebound allows anti-TPO-driven lymphocytic infiltration of the gland. Follicular destruction releases preformed colloid hormones into circulation → transient thyrotoxicosis, often followed by a hypothyroid phase before recovery. No TSH-receptor stimulation → no exophthalmos or pretibial myxedema.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the specific mechanism causing postpartum thyroiditis in a breastfeeding woman
NBME Trap Fingerprint
New mother + hyperthyroid symptoms + enlarged nontender thyroid + undetectable TSH + NO exophthalmos + low radioactive iodine uptake → postpartum thyroiditis (lymphocytic thyroiditis). Mechanism = lymphocytic infiltration of the thyroid from rebound immune activity after pregnancy-induced immune suppression.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Activating TSH-receptor mutations. Cause familial/sporadic non- autoimmune hyperthyroidism (toxic adenoma); not postpartum-onset.
Activating mutations of TSH receptors cause Graves-like autonomous thyroid stimulation. This is the mechanism of McCune-Albright syndrome affecting the thyroid, or toxic nodules. In Graves disease, TSH receptor AUTOANTIBODIES (not mutations) activate the receptor. Postpartum thyroiditis is destructive, not stimulatory.
(B)
Increased serum thyroglobulin. Thyroglobulin is a marker, not a mechanism, of follicular destruction.
Increased serum thyroglobulin. Occurs when thyroid follicles are disrupted and thyroglobulin leaks into the circulation. This is CONSISTENT with destructive thyroiditis (supporting the correct answer of lymphocytic infiltration), but thyroglobulin elevation is a consequence of the infiltration, not the mechanism.
(C)
Ischemic injury to the hypothalamus. Sheehan affects the anterior pituitary, not hypothalamus, and causes hypopituitarism (low TSH and low T4).
Ischemic injury to the hypothalamus. Would suppress TRH → suppress TSH → secondary hypothyroidism, not hyperthyroidism. Postpartum hypopituitarism (Sheehan syndrome from peripartum hemorrhage) would cause secondary hypothyroidism. Low T4 + low TSH.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Lymphocytic infiltration of the thyroid. Mechanism of postpartum thyroiditis.
(E)
TSH receptor autoantibodies. Graves disease; would expect exophthalmos and pretibial myxedema.
TSH receptor autoantibodies. Are the mechanism of GRAVES DISEASE. The classic autoimmune hyperthyroidism. Graves disease is distinguished from postpartum thyroiditis by exophthalmos, pretibial myxedema, positive TSI/TRAb, and HIGH radioactive iodine uptake. This patient has no exophthalmos and has a nontender (not firm, not irregular) thyroid. Postpartum thyroiditis has LOW RAI uptake.
Costume Change
Reappears as: postpartum thyroiditis course. Hyperthyroid phase (1-4 months postpartum, low RAI uptake, from preformed T4 release) → hypothyroid phase (4-8 months, may require temporary levothyroxine) → recovery (most women return to normal). Breastfeeding contraindication: methimazole is safer than PTU for breastfeeding; RAI is absolutely contraindicated.
Variable Pivot
If exophthalmos + high RAI uptake + TSI positive → Graves disease. If painful thyroid + viral prodrome + high ESR → de Quervain (subacute granulomatous) thyroiditis. If thyroid enlarged + firm + slow onset → Hashimoto thyroiditis (antithyroid peroxidase antibodies). If postpartum + low TSH + fatigue + no thyroid enlargement → Sheehan syndrome (hypopituitarism from peripartum hemorrhage).
Tid BitPostpartum hyperthyroidism without eye signs = silent (lymphocytic) thyroiditis, not Graves. NBME Free 120 (2026). Block 5 Answer Explanations
Q081 Block 5 Answer: D
BiostatisticsDiagnostic Testing

Screening test false positives

Vignette Summary
2x2 validation data → prevalence 80/100,000 → screen 100,000 → expected FP count.
The Discriminator
Specificity = 900/1000 = 90%, so FPR = 10%. Non-diseased population ≈ 100,000 − 80 = 99,920. FP ≈ 0.10 × 99,920 ≈ 9992.
Why the Answer is Correct
False positives = (1 − specificity) × number without disease. From validation: 100/1000 healthy women tested positive, so FPR = 10%. With prevalence 80/100,000, \~99,920 women are disease-free. 10% × 99,920 ≈ 9992.
Cognitive Demand
Calculate false positives from specificity in a screening scenario
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Specificity = TN/(TN+FP). With 1000 non-diseased people and 90% specificity → 100 false positives. The question asks about non-diseased people only.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
20. Number of expected true positives (\~25% sensitivity × 80), not FP.
100 is the correct answer. 1000 non-diseased × (1-0.90) = 100 FP. Other choices reflect common calculation errors.
(B)
80. Total diseased, not FP.
200 reflects using 80% specificity or mixing up sensitivity and specificity calculations.
(C)
8993. Arithmetic error / off by one order.
900 is the number of TRUE NEGATIVES among non-diseased people, not false positives.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
9992. FPR(0.10) × non-diseased(99,920).
(E)
10,012. Counts true positives mistakenly as FP.
10 would reflect a 99% specificity rate, not 90%.
Costume Change
Reappears as: 2×2 table calculations. Sensitivity = TP/(TP+FN). Specificity = TN/(TN+FP). PPV = TP/(TP+FP). NPV = TN/(TN+FN). High specificity → few false positives → good for confirmatory tests. High sensitivity → few false negatives → good for screening tests.
Variable Pivot
If specificity were 99% → 10 FP. If asked about false negatives → use sensitivity and diseased population. If prevalence were higher → PPV would increase for same sensitivity and specificity.
Tid BitFP = (1 − specificity) × non-diseased; never use the whole population denominator.
Q082 Block 5 Answer: E
NeurologyCortical Localization

Right parietal lesion with extinction

Vignette Summary
73M bumps left-sided objects → left lower quadrantanopia → tactile extinction on left with simultaneous stimulation → no motor/language deficits.
The Discriminator
Hemineglect (extinction to double simultaneous stimulation) localizes to the non- dominant (right) parietal lobe; left lower quadrantanopia = right upper optic radiation (parietal Baum loop).
Why the Answer is Correct
The right inferior parietal lobule mediates spatial attention to both hemifields; lesions cause contralateral hemineglect. Optic radiations carrying the contralateral superior visual field pass through the parietal lobe, producing left lower quadrantanopia (“pie on the floor”).
Cognitive Demand
Identify deficit pattern from right parietal lobe lesion
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Right parietal lobe → left visual field defect (lower quadrantanopia from Meyer's loop involvement) + left hemisensory neglect (extinction on double simultaneous stimulation). But NO motor deficit, NO language deficit.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Left frontal. Would give right-sided weakness/aphasia.
Right homonymous hemianopia. Would require a left occipital or optic tract lesion. Damage to the right visual cortex or right optic tract would cause LEFT visual field loss, not right. Right parietal involvement affects Meyer's loop → contralateral lower quadrant.
(B)
Left parietal. Gerstmann (agraphia, acalculia), right-sided neglect, not left.
Aphasia (language deficit) localizes to the LEFT hemisphere. Broca's area (inferior frontal) and Wernicke's area (superior temporal). A right parietal lesion in a right-handed person does not cause aphasia.
(C)
Left temporal. Wernicke aphasia / right upper quadrantanopia.
Right hemiparesis localizes to the LEFT motor cortex or internal capsule, not the right parietal cortex. Parietal lesions cause sensory neglect and visuospatial deficits, not hemiparesis.
(D)
Right frontal. Motor neglect possible but no quadrantanopia or extinction pattern.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Right parietal. Contralateral neglect + lower quadrantanopia.
Prosopagnosia (inability to recognize faces) localizes to the right fusiform gyrus. Bilateral occipitotemporal lesions more classically. It is not the primary deficit of parietal lesion.
(F)
Right temporal. Would give left upper quadrantanopia (“pie in the sky”).
Costume Change
Reappears as: right parietal = left neglect + constructional apraxia + dressing apraxia + left lower quadrantanopia. Left parietal = Gerstmann syndrome (acalculia + agraphia + finger agnosia + left-right confusion) + right lower quadrantanopia. Or: which lobe? Frontal = motor + executive. Parietal = sensory integration + neglect. Temporal = memory + language (left). Occipital = vision.
Variable Pivot
If left parietal lesion → Gerstmann syndrome. If bilateral parietal lesion → Balint syndrome (simultanagnosia, optic ataxia, ocular apraxia). If right temporal lesion → left visual field defect + prosopagnosia. If left Broca area → expressive aphasia.
Tid BitParietal radiations → lower quadrantanopia; temporal Meyer loop → upper quadrantanopia.
Q083 Block 5 Answer: E
ImmunologyPediatricsGenetics

DiGeorge syndrome

Vignette Summary
Newborn with hypertelorism, short philtrum, micrognathia → interrupted aortic arch → hypocalcemic tetany at day 3.
The Discriminator
Conotruncal cardiac defect + hypocalcemia + facial dysmorphism = 22q11.2 deletion.
Why the Answer is Correct
22q11.2 microdeletion disrupts development of the 3rd and 4th pharyngeal pouches, causing thymic and parathyroid aplasia (hence hypocalcemia and T-cell deficiency) and abnormal neural crest migration into the conotruncus (interrupted aortic arch, truncus arteriosus, tetralogy of Fallot).
Cognitive Demand
Identify the genetic and clinical syndrome in a neonate with cardiac defects + hypocalcemia + T cell deficiency
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Cardiac + hypocalcemia (from absent parathyroids) + T cell deficiency (from absent thymus) = DiGeorge syndrome = 22q11.2 deletion.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
13. Patau: holoprosencephaly, polydactyly, cleft.
Cri-du-chat (5p deletion). Causes a cat-like cry in infants, intellectual disability, microcephaly, and hypertelorism. NOT cardiac + thymic + parathyroid triad.
(B)
14. Uniparental disomy syndromes; not this phenotype.
Down syndrome (trisomy 21). Causes intellectual disability, flat facies, single palmar crease, Brushfield spots, AV canal defects, and duodenal atresia. While cardiac defects occur, hypocalcemia from hypoparathyroidism and T cell deficiency are NOT features of Down syndrome.
(C)
18. Edwards: clenched fists, rocker-bottom feet, microcephaly.
Klinefelter (47,XXY) causes tall stature, hypogonadism, gynecomastia, and infertility. No cardiac/parathyroid/thymic abnormalities.
(D)
21. Down: AV canal, simian crease, hypotonia.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
22. 22q11.2 deletion (DiGeorge/ velocardiofacial).
Turner syndrome (45,XO) causes short stature, webbed neck, streak gonads, coarctation of the aorta, and horseshoe kidney. Not the same triad.
Costume Change
Reappears as: DiGeorge. "CATCH-22" mnemonic: Cardiac defects, Abnormal facies, Thymic hypoplasia, Cleft palate, Hypocalcemia, 22q11 deletion. Or: which T cell subset is low in DiGeorge? → CD3+ T cells (all T cells, because the thymus is absent for maturation). Or: recurrent infections despite normal B cells = T cell immunodeficiency.
Variable Pivot
If recurrent sinopulmonary infections + low immunoglobulins + normal T cells → XLA (Bruton). If all cells low + recurrent severe infections from birth → SCID. If normal T cells + low B cells + hypogammaglobulinemia → CVID. If absent thymus + low T cells + cardiac → DiGeorge.
Tid BitCATCH-22. Cardiac, Abnormal facies, Thymic aplasia, Cleft palate, Hypocalcemia.
Q084 Block 5 Answer: E
CardiologyElectrophysiology

Typical atrial flutter (cavotricuspid isthmus reentry)

Vignette Summary
76F with chronic alcohol use, recent episodic palpitations, dyspnea, light-headedness. ECG shows an irregular ventricular rhythm with sawtooth baseline activity most visible in inferior leads. Narrow QRS. Variable AV conduction.
The Discriminator
Sawtooth flutter waves in the inferior leads with an irregular ventricular response equals typical atrial flutter, not atrial fibrillation. The reentrant circuit is in the RIGHT atrium, around the tricuspid annulus, dependent on the cavotricuspid isthmus between the IVC and the tricuspid valve.
Why the Answer is Correct
Typical atrial flutter is a macroreentrant tachycardia. The wavefront travels counterclockwise around the tricuspid valve, passes through a narrow corridor between the IVC and the tricuspid annulus (the cavotricuspid isthmus), and reenters the right atrial wall. The atrial rate is roughly 250 to 350 per minute. The AV node imposes a variable block, producing the irregular ventricular response. Alcohol is the precipitant here (holiday heart can present as AFib or flutter). Stroke risk parallels AFib, so anticoagulation is required. Definitive treatment for recurrent typical flutter is radiofrequency ablation of the cavotricuspid isthmus, which cures the circuit.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cardiac chamber responsible for the ECG abnormality in a holiday drinker
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Irregular rhythm with no P waves + narrow QRS + binge alcohol → atrial fibrillation from holiday heart syndrome = right atrium (AF originates in atrial tissue, primarily around pulmonary veins entering left atrium, but the question may frame it as right atrial in the context of the conducting tissue).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
AV node. The AV node is the gatekeeper that filters the atrial rate down to a tolerable ventricular rate. It is not the engine of the arrhythmia. AVNRT is a different problem with regular narrow QRS and absent or buried P waves.
Left ventricle dysfunction. Produces ST changes, bundle branch blocks, and ventricular arrhythmias (VT, VF). Not irregular atrial fibrillation without P waves.
(B)
Bundle of His. Junctional rhythms or accessory pathways live here. Wide complex tachycardias or short PR with delta wave point this way. Not flutter.
Left atrium. AF actually originates predominantly from pulmonary vein foci entering the LEFT atrium, but Holiday Heart classically shows typical AF and the question likely frames the conducting tissue question around the sinoatrial node region (right atrium). In pure anatomy, AF = left atrial foci but appears clinically as right atrial conduction issue.
(C)
Left ventricle. Wide QRS ventricular tachycardia origin. Narrow QRS flutter rules this out.
Right ventricle dysfunction. Causes right bundle branch block patterns, right axis deviation, or right heart failure signs. The narrow QRS here indicates no ventricular conduction delay.
(D)
Purkinje fibers. Idiopathic ventricular ectopy and PVCs. Again, wide complex.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Right atrium. CORRECT. The flutter circuit is around the tricuspid annulus, dependent on the cavotricuspid isthmus.
Sinoatrial node. Is IN the right atrium (at junction of SVC and RA). If SA node dysfunction caused this, you'd see sinus pause or sick sinus syndrome. The SA node is not firing normally in AF; instead, multiple ectopic atrial foci fire chaotically.
(F)
Sinoatrial node. The SA node is in the right atrium too, but its tachycardias (sinus tach, inappropriate sinus tach) have a regular rhythm with a P wave preceding every QRS. Not flutter.
Costume Change
Reappears as: holiday heart syndrome. AF after binge alcohol consumption, especially during holiday periods. Usually self-limiting. Risk of stroke from AF-induced atrial thrombus (left atrial appendage). Treatment: rate control, anticoagulation per CHA2DS2-VASc score.
Variable Pivot
If the patient had ventricular fibrillation → defibrillate immediately. If stable AF < 48 hours duration → rate control or cardioversion (low clot risk). If AF > 48 hours → anticoagulate for 3+ weeks before cardioversion OR TEE to rule out LAA thrombus. If hypotensive → synchronized cardioversion immediately.
Tid BitSawtooth in II, III, aVF equals typical atrial flutter. Circuit lives in the right atrium around the tricuspid annulus. Anticoagulate the same as AFib. Cure with cavotricuspid isthmus ablation.
Q085 Block 5 Answer: D
GIPharmacologyGastric Physiology

NSAID-induced gastric ulcer

Vignette Summary
52F on chronic ibuprofen → hematemesis, hypotension, tachycardia → epigastric tenderness, heme-positive stool.
The Discriminator
Oral NSAID-related upper GI bleed → systemic COX-1 inhibition decreasing mucosal prostaglandins.
Why the Answer is Correct
Ibuprofen nonselectively inhibits COX-1, which normally produces PGE2 and PGI2 in gastric mucosa. These prostaglandins stimulate mucus and bicarbonate secretion and maintain mucosal blood flow. Loss of this cytoprotection allows acid/pepsin to erode the epithelium → ulcer and bleeding.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism of NSAID-induced GI injury
NBME Trap Fingerprint
NSAIDs inhibit COX-1 → decreased PGE2 and PGI2 → loss of gastric mucosal protection (decreased mucus, decreased bicarbonate, decreased blood flow) → ulceration and GI bleed.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Nitric oxide generation. NO is protective, not injurious.
Direct cytotoxic effect. NSAIDs DO have some direct mucosal toxicity when in contact with the gastric epithelium (aspirin is mildly acidic). However, this is NOT the primary mechanism. The primary mechanism is prostaglandin depletion via COX-1 inhibition, which impairs the mucosal defense mechanisms even without direct contact.
(B)
Increased prostaglandin production. Reverse mechanism; NSAIDs decrease PGs.
Stimulation of acid secretion. NSAIDs do NOT stimulate acid secretion. If anything, the loss of protective prostaglandins means the mucosal barrier is weaker, but acid secretion itself is unchanged. The problem is not MORE acid. It's LESS protection from normal acid.
(C)
Pro-inflammatory cytokine induction. Describes H. Pylori, not NSAIDs.
Inhibition of bile acid secretion. NSAIDs have no effect on bile acid secretion. Bile acid injury is a separate phenomenon (bile reflux gastritis). This is not the mechanism of NSAID injury.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Inhibition of COX-1. Loses gastric cytoprotection.
(E)
Pre-absorptive topical injury. Minor contributor; systemic COX-1 inhibition dominates and is the canonical answer.
Vasodilation of mesenteric vessels. NSAIDs actually cause VASOCONSTRICTION of renal arterioles (reducing GFR, relevant for NSAID-induced AKI). In the GI mucosa, prostaglandins maintain mucosal blood flow. Their inhibition causes mucosal ISCHEMIA (vasoconstriction), not vasodilation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: misoprostol (PGE1 analog) protects gastric mucosa from NSAID injury. It is the rationale for combining misoprostol with NSAIDs in high-risk patients. Or: which NSAID has the lowest GI risk? → COX-2 selective inhibitors (celecoxib). Spare COX-1 in gastric mucosa while still inhibiting COX-2 for anti-inflammatory effect.
Variable Pivot
If the patient were on NSAIDs + had GI bleed → stop NSAIDs, start PPI, test for H. Pylori. If using NSAIDs for arthritis pain → consider celecoxib + PPI if high GI risk. If the question asked about renal toxicity of NSAIDs → COX-1 inhibition → reduced prostaglandin-dependent afferent arteriolar dilation → decreased GFR.
Tid BitNSAID ulcers = systemic COX-1 blockade, not local irritation.
Q086 Block 5 Answer: A
TeratologyEmbryology

Clubfoot from oligohydramnios

Vignette Summary
Term newborn, oligohydramnios from uteroplacental insufficiency → bilateral inwardly turned, downward-pointing feet (talipes equinovarus).
The Discriminator
Normal embryologic development with extrinsic mechanical compression in utero = deformation.
Why the Answer is Correct
Deformation = mechanical force distorts an otherwise normally formed structure. Oligohydramnios reduces cushioning fluid, compressing fetal limbs against the uterine wall and producing positional clubfoot (plus Potter-sequence features when severe).
Cognitive Demand
Classify a clubfoot developing from oligohydramnios as a deformation vs malformation
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Oligohydramnios → mechanical compression of fetus → clubfoot from external force on a normally formed limb = DEFORMATION. The intrinsic limb anatomy is normal; the external environment compressed it.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Deformation. Extrinsic compression on normal tissue.
Agenesis. Complete absence of a structure from failure of formation. Clubfoot is NOT absence of a foot. The foot exists and is structurally complete but positioned abnormally.
(B)
Disruption. Destruction of normally formed tissue (e.g., amniotic bands).
Disruption. Destruction of a previously normal structure (e.g., amniotic band syndrome). Disruptions cause amputations, ring constrictions, or tissue loss. Not a positional deformity from compression.
(C)
Dysplasia. Abnormal cellular organization within a tissue.
Dysplasia. Abnormal cellular organization within a tissue (e.g., skeletal dysplasias). The clubfoot from oligohydramnios has normal cellular histology. It is a positional/mechanical problem, not a cellular organization problem.
(D)
Malformation. Intrinsic primary defect during morphogenesis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Potter sequence = bilateral renal agenesis → oligohydramnios → constraint deformities (limb contractures, flat face, pulmonary hypoplasia). All Potter features are DEFORMATIONS or disruptions secondary to fluid loss, not intrinsic malformations. Or: clubfoot prognosis depends on etiology. Deformation (oligohydramnios) is more amenable to casting (Ponseti method) than intrinsic malformation.
Variable Pivot
If bilateral limb amputations + ring constrictions → amniotic band syndrome (disruption). If abnormal cellular architecture on histology → dysplasia. If absent limb entirely → agenesis. If intrinsic genetic cause of abnormal limb formation → malformation.
Tid BitOligohydramnios → Potter facies, pulmonary hypoplasia, limb deformations.
Q087 Block 5 Answer: B
PulmonologyEmergency Medicine

Tension pneumothorax

Vignette Summary
19M with right-sided knife wound → tachypnea, low-normal BP, tracheal shift to the left.
The Discriminator
Tracheal deviation away from the injured side = tension pneumothorax on the right.
Why the Answer is Correct
Air enters the pleural space with each breath but cannot escape, collapsing the right lung and pushing mediastinum leftward. Air between chest wall and lung dampens transmitted vibrations (decreased fremitus), gives a hyperresonant percussion note, and reduces breath sounds.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the physical exam findings of tension pneumothorax
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Tension pneumothorax = air accumulating under pressure in pleural space → ipsilateral lung collapse + mediastinal shift → decreased breath sounds + hyperresonance IPSILATERALLY + tracheal deviation AWAY from affected side + hemodynamic collapse.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Dec fremitus / dull / dec BS. Describes pleural effusion or hemothorax.
Bilateral decreased breath sounds. Suggests bilateral pneumothorax or massive effusion. Unlikely in this scenario. Tension pneumothorax is typically unilateral.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Dec fremitus / hyperresonant / dec BS. Pneumothorax triad.
(C)
Dec fremitus / hyperresonant / dull BS. Internally inconsistent.
Dull percussion + decreased breath sounds suggests pleural effusion (fluid, not air). Pneumothorax is hyperresonant; effusion is dull. This answer describes hemothorax.
(D)
Inc fremitus / dull / bronchial. Lobar consolidation.
Normal breath sounds bilaterally rules out both pneumothorax and significant effusion.
(E)
Inc fremitus / dull / dec BS. Atypical; not pneumothorax.
Stridor + decreased breath sounds suggests upper airway obstruction (foreign body, epiglottitis) combined with a lower cause. Not the pure tension pneumothorax pattern.
Costume Change
Reappears as: tension pneumothorax triad. Absent breath sounds + hyperresonance + tracheal deviation away from lesion + hypotension. Treatment: immediate needle decompression (2nd intercostal space, midclavicular line). Do NOT wait for CXR. Then chest tube placement.
Variable Pivot
If dull to percussion + decreased breath sounds → hemothorax (chest tube). If decreased breath sounds + resonant + hypotension after trauma → tension pneumothorax (needle first). If patient on mechanical ventilation with sudden deterioration + high peak pressures → tension pneumothorax from barotrauma.
Tid BitHyperresonant + tracheal shift away = tension pneumothorax, needle decompress.
Q088 Block 5 Answer: D
HematologyReticulocytes

Reticulocytosis after acute blood loss

Vignette Summary
22M trauma, 20% volume loss, bleeding controlled → smear 10 days later.
The Discriminator
Acute blood loss → EPO surge → reticulocytosis by day 3-7, peaking around 10 days.
Why the Answer is Correct
Reticulocytes are immature RBCs containing residual ribosomal RNA. On Wright stain this RNA imparts a bluish-grey tint to the cytoplasm (polychromasia). Following acute hemorrhage, bone marrow ramps up erythropoiesis and releases polychromatophilic cells into the circulation.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the peripheral smear finding of reticulocytosis 10 days after acute blood loss
NBME Trap Fingerprint
10 days after blood loss → bone marrow has responded with reticulocyte release → polychromasia on Wright-Giemsa stain (reticulocytes appear blue-purple because residual RNA stains basophilically).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Heinz bodies. Oxidative damage (G6PD deficiency).
Acanthocytes (spur cells) appear in liver disease, McLeod syndrome, and abetalipoproteinemia. Not in response to acute blood loss.
(B)
Hypochromia. Iron deficiency; develops over weeks, not 10 days after one bleed.
Elliptocytes (ovalocytes). Are seen in hereditary elliptocytosis and iron deficiency anemia. They do not appear in response to acute hemorrhage.
(C)
Microspherocytosis. Hereditary spherocytosis or immune hemolysis.
Schistocytes (fragmented red cells) appear in microangiopathic hemolytic anemia. TTP, HUS, DIC, mechanical heart valves. They are not a response to blood loss.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Polychromasia. Reticulocyte response to blood loss.
(E)
Siderotic granules. Sideroblastic anemia or post-splenectomy.
Spherocytes appear in hereditary spherocytosis, autoimmune hemolytic anemia, or transfusion reactions. Not a feature of blood loss response.
Costume Change
Reappears as: timeline of anemia findings after acute blood loss. Immediate: normal CBC (cells and plasma lost proportionally). 24-48h: plasma volume restored → dilutional anemia. 5-7 days: EPO-stimulated reticulocyte production. 10+ days: polychromasia = reticulocytosis visible on smear. Or: reticulocyte count on automated CBC = indirect measure of bone marrow erythropoietic activity.
Variable Pivot
If immediate smear after acute hemorrhage → normocytic normochromic anemia. If polychromasia + spherocytes → hemolytic anemia. If schistocytes → microangiopathic process. If hypersegmented neutrophils + macrocytosis → B12 or folate deficiency.
Tid BitBluish RBCs on smear = young reticulocytes = marrow compensating.
Q089 Block 5 Answer: B
NutritionGastroenterology

Steatorrhea → vitamin A deficiency

Vignette Summary
53M, 1-year dry scaly rash + 15-kg weight loss + bulky floating stools → fat malabsorption.
The Discriminator
Steatorrhea preferentially depletes fat-soluble vitamins (A, D, E, K). Dry scaly skin (follicular hyperkeratosis) and rash point to vitamin A.
Why the Answer is Correct
Vitamin A (retinol) maintains epithelial differentiation via retinoic acid-nuclear receptor signaling. With fat malabsorption (chronic pancreatitis, celiac, etc.), micelle formation fails and retinol absorption drops, producing xerosis, follicular hyperkeratosis, and night blindness.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the fat-soluble vitamin deficiency causing skin findings in a patient with fat malabsorption
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Floating oily stools + dry scaly skin + fat malabsorption setting = fat-soluble vitamin deficiency. Among fat-soluble vitamins, vitamin A deficiency specifically causes follicular hyperkeratosis and dry scaly skin (not just night blindness).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Magnesium. Alcohol/diuretic loss; gives tetany, arrhythmias.
Vitamin B1 (thiamine) deficiency. Causes Wernicke encephalopathy (confusion, ataxia, ophthalmoplegia) and beriberi (dry = peripheral neuropathy, wet = high-output cardiac failure). Thiamine is water-soluble and not affected by fat malabsorption. Does not cause the dry scaly skin described.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Vitamin A. Fat-soluble; epithelial maintenance.
(C)
Vitamin B12. Water-soluble; causes macrocytic anemia and neuropathy, not scaly skin.
Vitamin B6 (pyridoxine) deficiency. Causes peripheral neuropathy, glossitis, and seborrheic dermatitis. B6 is water-soluble. The skin manifestation of B6 deficiency is seborrheic-like, not dry and scaly from fat malabsorption.
(D)
Vitamin C. Scurvy: perifollicular hemorrhage, bleeding gums.
Vitamin C (ascorbic acid) deficiency. Causes scurvy. Perifollicular hemorrhages, gingival bleeding, corkscrew hairs, poor wound healing. Vitamin C is water-soluble and not affected by fat malabsorption.
(E)
Zinc. Acrodermatitis with perioral/perianal rash, alopecia, diarrhea. Possible but less classic with steatorrhea picture.
Vitamin K deficiency. Causes coagulopathy (prolonged PT/INR) from impaired gamma-carboxylation of clotting factors II, VII, IX, X. It does not cause the skin findings described.
Costume Change
Reappears as: fat-soluble vitamins ADEK and their deficiency syndromes. A = night blindness + skin (follicular hyperkeratosis). D = rickets (children) / osteomalacia (adults). E = hemolytic anemia + peripheral neuropathy. K = coagulopathy. All become deficient in fat malabsorption (Crohn's, chronic pancreatitis, cystic fibrosis, cholestasis).
Variable Pivot
If night blindness only → early vitamin A deficiency. If rickets (bowing legs, craniotabes) + low calcium → vitamin D deficiency. If prolonged PT + bleeding → vitamin K deficiency. If hemolytic anemia in a premature neonate → vitamin E deficiency.
Tid BitFat-soluble A-D-E-K. Steatorrhea takes them all.
Q090 Block 5 Answer: C
NephrologySickle CellRenal Medulla Physiology

Sickle cell papillary necrosis

Vignette Summary
22F with sickle cell disease → painless hematuria → papillary necrosis on CT.
The Discriminator
Renal medulla is hypoxic and hyperosmolar, the perfect environment for HbS polymerization and microvascular occlusion.
Why the Answer is Correct
The vasa recta countercurrent system makes the medulla relatively hypoxic (low O2 tension) and hyperosmolar (high osmolality). Both conditions promote red cell dehydration and deoxy-HbS polymerization, causing sickling, vaso-occlusion of vasa recta, and ischemic necrosis of the renal papillae.
Cognitive Demand
Explain why the renal medulla is uniquely susceptible to sickle cell-related papillary necrosis
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The renal medulla is naturally hypoxic AND hyperosmolar AND acidic. These three conditions are exactly what promote HbS polymerization and sickling. Papillary necrosis from sickling is therefore unique to the medulla.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
↑O2 / ↑osm. Incorrect physiology; medulla is hypoxic.
The renal cortex has high blood flow, normal oxygen tension, and normal osmolality. It is relatively protected from sickling. Cortical blood flow is robust through the peritubular capillaries.
(B)
↑O2 / ↓osm. Describes cortex, low risk for sickling.
The glomerulus has high blood flow and operates at near-systemic oxygen tensions. Glomerular disease from sickle cell occurs (FSGS pattern) but through different mechanisms than the medullary hypoxia driving papillary necrosis.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
↓O2 / ↑osm. Promotes HbS polymerization.
(D)
↓O2 / ↓osm. Partial truth; high osmolality is essential to drive sickling.
The ureter is not a vascular territory susceptible to sickling. Ureteral obstruction could result from shed papillae causing ureteral colic.
Costume Change
Reappears as: sickle cell renal manifestations. Hyposthenuria (inability to concentrate urine from medullary damage) + papillary necrosis (hematuria, passage of tissue) + renal tubular acidosis type IV + increased susceptibility to encapsulated organisms (functional asplenia). Or: papillary necrosis from other causes: NSAIDs, analgesic nephropathy, diabetes, obstruction (SADDO mnemonic).
Variable Pivot
If gross hematuria + flank pain + history of sickle cell → papillary necrosis (shed papillae). If sickle cell patient cannot concentrate urine even with desmopressin → hyposthenuria from medullary damage. If proteinuria + nephrotic syndrome in sickle cell → FSGS pattern from glomerular hyperfiltration. If renal tubular acidosis type IV + hyperkalemia → sickle cell nephropathy affecting the collecting duct.
Tid BitSickle-cell trait/disease → painless hematuria from medullary papillary necrosis.
Q091 Block 5 Answer: C
ImmunologyBiochemistryInflammation

Acute appendicitis. Neutrophilic infiltrate

Vignette Summary
34F with 6-hour periumbilical/RLQ pain, fever 40°C, rebound tenderness → appendectomy with transmural neutrophilic infiltrate.
The Discriminator
The histologic hallmark is dense neutrophil infiltration; the chief chemotactic mediator is leukotriene B4.
Why the Answer is Correct
Obstruction of the appendiceal lumen elevates intraluminal pressure, triggers bacterial overgrowth, and activates mucosal macrophages. These release LTB4, a 5-lipoxygenase product that is one of the most potent neutrophil chemoattractants, recruiting the transmural acute inflammatory infiltrate seen microscopically.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the lipid mediator responsible for neutrophil chemotaxis in acute appendicitis
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Neutrophil chemotaxis in acute inflammation is primarily driven by leukotriene B4 (LTB4), complement fragment C5a, and IL-8. Of these, LTB4 is the major lipid chemotactic agent.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Bradykinin. Pain and vasodilation, not neutrophil recruitment.
C5a (complement fragment). Is a potent neutrophil chemotactic agent AND anaphylatoxin. However, C5a is a protein/peptide, not a lipid mediator. The question asks about the lipid mediator specifically.
(B)
Histamine. Vasodilation and increased permeability in early/immediate inflammation; not chemotactic.
Hageman factor (factor XII) initiates the intrinsic coagulation cascade and kinin system. It is a coagulation protein, not a lipid mediator of neutrophil chemotaxis.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Leukotriene (LTB4). Major neutrophil chemoattractant.
(D)
Lipoxin. Anti- inflammatory; inhibits neutrophil recruitment.
Prostaglandin E2 (PGE2). Is a lipid mediator from COX pathway (arachidonic acid → cyclooxygenase → PGH2 → PGE2). PGE2 causes fever, vasodilation, and sensitization of pain receptors. It does NOT function as a neutrophil chemotactic agent.
(E)
Thromboxane. Platelet aggregation and vasoconstriction.
Thromboxane A2 (TXA2). Is a lipid mediator produced by platelets. It causes platelet aggregation and vasoconstriction. It is not a neutrophil chemotactic agent.
Costume Change
Reappears as: arachidonic acid metabolism. COX pathway → PGE2 (fever, pain sensitization, vasodilation), TXA2 (platelet aggregation, vasoconstriction), PGI2 (prostacyclin. Vasodilation, inhibits platelet aggregation). LOX pathway → LTB4 (neutrophil chemotaxis), LTC4/D4/E4 (bronchoconstriction, mucus secretion. The slow-reacting substances of anaphylaxis). Aspirin/NSAIDs → COX inhibition.
Variable Pivot
If question asked about bronchoconstriction in asthma → LTC4/D4/E4 (leukotrienes). If question asked about fever → PGE2 (COX pathway). If question asked about platelet aggregation → TXA2. If question asked about vasodilation + inhibiting platelets → PGI2 (prostacyclin). If C5a → complement, not a lipid.
Tid BitLTB4 = neutrophil “come hither”; lipoxins = “go home.”
Q092 Block 5 Answer: B
PsychiatryDiagnostic Classification

Brief psychotic disorder

Vignette Summary
15M, previously well, sudden agitation, command auditory hallucinations and persecutory delusions after hearing about a school shooting one week earlier.
The Discriminator
Acute psychotic symptoms lasting \<1 month following a marked stressor, with prior baseline functioning intact.
Why the Answer is Correct
Brief psychotic disorder is defined by one or more positive psychotic symptoms (delusions, hallucinations, disorganized speech) lasting at least one day but less than one month, often precipitated by a stressor, with full return to premorbid functioning expected.
Cognitive Demand
Classify acute psychotic symptoms appearing immediately after a traumatic event
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Psychosis onset within hours to days after a major stressor + duration < 1 month = brief psychotic disorder. The school shooting is the precipitating stressor.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Adjustment disorder with anxiety. No psychotic features.
Acute stress disorder. ASD involves dissociative symptoms, intrusive memories, avoidance, and hyperarousal. The stress response symptoms. For 3 days to 1 month after trauma. ASD does not include hallucinations or delusions. This patient has frank psychotic symptoms, not the PTSD-spectrum response.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Brief psychotic disorder. \<1 month, post-stressor psychosis.
(C)
PTSD. Requires direct trauma exposure + re-experiencing/avoidance; psychosis is not core.
Adjustment disorder. Involves emotional or behavioral symptoms in response to an identifiable stressor, but the disturbance does not meet criteria for another specific mental disorder and does not include psychotic features. Hallucinations and delusions are not part of adjustment disorder.
(D)
Schizophrenia. Requires ≥6 months of symptoms.
Schizophrenia. Requires ≥ 6 months total duration of symptoms, including a prodrome. Acute onset hours after a traumatic event with no prior psychiatric history does not meet schizophrenia criteria.
(E)
Schizotypal personality disorder. Chronic odd beliefs without frank psychosis.
Schizophreniform disorder. Requires 1-6 months of schizophrenia-like symptoms (not < 1 month). This patient's symptoms appeared after a stressor and would be classified as brief psychotic disorder if they resolve within a month.
Costume Change
Reappears as: duration rules in psychotic disorders. < 1 day: insufficient. 1 day to 1 month: brief psychotic disorder. 1-6 months: schizophreniform. > 6 months: schizophrenia. All timeframes assume schizophrenia-level symptom severity. Schizoaffective = psychosis + prominent mood episode.
Variable Pivot
If symptoms lasted 2 months → schizophreniform disorder (E). If symptoms lasted > 6 months → schizophrenia (D). If no psychotic features, only anxiety/dissociation/hyperarousal after trauma → ASD < 1 month, PTSD > 1 month (A). If prominent mood episode with psychosis → Bipolar I or schizoaffective depending on relative duration.
Tid BitBrief \<1 month, Schizophreniform 1-6 months, Schizophrenia ≥6 months.
Q093 Block 5 Answer: B
Infectious DiseaseNeurologyMeningitis

Viral (aseptic) meningitis

Vignette Summary
10M, summer camp outbreak, fever/headache/photophobia → mild nuchal rigidity → CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis, normal glucose, mildly elevated protein.
The Discriminator
Summer + camp outbreak + lymphocyte-predominant CSF with normal glucose = enterovirus (coxsackie, echovirus).
Why the Answer is Correct
Enteroviruses are non-enveloped RNA picornaviruses transmitted fecal-orally; they peak in late summer/early fall and cause most cases of viral meningitis. They invade the CNS via viremia, eliciting a lymphocytic CSF response without consuming glucose.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most common viral cause of aseptic meningitis in a summer camp cluster
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Summer + children + aseptic meningitis cluster = enteroviral meningitis. Enteroviruses (echovirus, coxsackievirus) peak in summer and fall, spread fecal-oral, and cause the vast majority of viral meningitis cases.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Adenovirus. Respiratory/conjunctival illness; not classic for meningitis outbreaks.
Adenovirus. Causes respiratory illness (pharyngoconjunctival fever), gastrointestinal illness, and can cause meningitis but is not the MOST COMMON cause. Adenovirus lacks the seasonal clustering seen here.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Enterovirus. Summer aseptic meningitis.
(C)
HSV. Encephalitis with RBCs, temporal lobe focality.
HIV. Can cause aseptic meningitis during acute seroconversion (acute retroviral syndrome), but this affects adults (sexually active or IV drug users), not a child summer camp cluster, and would not present as a cluster outbreak.
(D)
N. Meningitidis. Neutrophilic pleocytosis, low glucose, high protein.
Mumps virus. Causes parotitis, orchitis, and can cause viral meningitis and encephalitis. Mumps meningitis outbreaks have occurred in unvaccinated populations. However, enteroviruses are overwhelmingly more common as causes of viral meningitis overall, and the summer/camp setting fits enterovirus better.
(E)
S. Pneumoniae. Same bacterial pattern; not lymphocytic.
Varicella-zoster. Causes chickenpox (primary) and shingles (reactivation). VZV can cause encephalitis and meningitis but more commonly with dermatomal rash. VZV meningitis would show vesicular rash, and is not the most common cause of aseptic meningitis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: CSF profile in viral meningitis. Lymphocytic pleocytosis (100-500 WBC, lymphocyte predominant), normal glucose (> 60% of serum), normal to mildly elevated protein. This contrasts with bacterial meningitis (PMN pleocytosis, low glucose, high protein) and TB meningitis (low glucose, very high protein, lymphocytes). Treatment: supportive (no antivirals for enteroviral meningitis).
Variable Pivot
If HIV exposure history in an adult + same CSF profile → acute HIV meningitis. If HSV encephalitis (temporal lobe involvement, fever, behavioral change, focal deficits) → acyclovir immediately. If mumps without vaccine → parotitis + meningitis in unvaccinated person. If bacterial cause suspected → empiric antibiotics immediately without waiting for LP.
Tid BitBacterial = PMNs + low glucose; viral = lymphocytes + normal glucose.
Q094 Block 5 Answer: B
PulmonologyNeuromuscular Physiology

Neuromuscular respiratory weakness

Vignette Summary
85F, BMI 15, progressive dyspnea → diaphragmatic excursion 1.5 cm → FEV1/FVC = 1.0 with TLC 54% (restriction), but DLCO 100%.
The Discriminator
Restrictive pattern with preserved DLCO and reduced diaphragmatic excursion indicates extrapulmonary (neuromuscular/chest wall) restriction, not parenchymal disease.
Why the Answer is Correct
When the lung parenchyma is normal but inspiratory muscles are weak, the patient cannot generate normal inspiratory pressures, lowering TLC and FVC proportionally. Gas exchange surface area is intact, so DLCO remains normal. The flow-volume loop shows reduced volumes with preserved shape.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cause of restrictive pattern with hypercapnia and normal DLCO in a cachectic elderly woman
NBME Trap Fingerprint
FEV1/FVC = 1.0 (preserved) + reduced TLC + normal DLCO + hypercapnia → pure extrapulmonary restrictive disease from NEUROMUSCULAR WEAKNESS. The alveoli are structurally intact (normal DLCO) but the respiratory muscles can't expand the chest adequately.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased lung elasticity. Emphysema; would raise TLC and obstruct flow.
Emphysema. Is an obstructive disease with airspace destruction → decreased FEV1/FVC (< 0.70), hyperinflation (increased TLC and FRC), and dramatically decreased DLCO (alveolar surface area lost). None of these match the preserved FEV1/FVC, reduced TLC, and normal DLCO described.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Decreased respiratory muscle strength. Extrapulmonary restriction with normal DLCO.
(C)
Decreased surfactant. Neonatal RDS, not elderly.
Idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis. Causes restrictive pattern with DECREASED DLCO (because fibrosis thickens alveolar walls and impairs gas exchange). IPF would also show bilateral lower lobe reticular opacities on imaging. Normal DLCO rules out significant parenchymal fibrosis.
(D)
Increased alveolar surface tension. Same as C, also lowers DLCO.
Pulmonary arterial hypertension. Causes decreased exercise tolerance, right heart failure signs, and eventually decreased DLCO (as pulmonary vasculature is destroyed). It is not primarily a ventilatory restriction. TLC and spirometry are normal or near-normal until late disease.
(E)
Increased bronchial mucous secretions. Obstructive (chronic bronchitis).
Sarcoidosis. Causes granulomatous interstitial lung disease. Restrictive pattern with decreased DLCO (granuloma impairs diffusion) + bilateral hilar adenopathy. Normal DLCO rules against significant sarcoid lung involvement.
(F)
Increased lung compliance. Emphysema pattern.
Costume Change
Reappears as: hypercapnia with NORMAL spirometry or normal DLCO → think neuromuscular disease (ALS, myasthenia, GBS, phrenic nerve paralysis). Or: maximum inspiratory pressure (MIP) and maximum expiratory pressure (MEP) are the tests of respiratory muscle strength. Or: nocturnal BiPAP as respiratory support for neuromuscular restrictive disease.
Variable Pivot
If low DLCO + restrictive → IPF or sarcoidosis or other interstitial lung disease. If obstructive + low DLCO → emphysema. If restrictive + normal DLCO → neuromuscular (B) or obesity (chest wall restriction). If restrictive + severe hypercapnia in a young person → think ALS or advanced myasthenia.
Tid BitRestrictive PFTs + normal DLCO = extrapulmonary cause (NM disease, chest wall).
Q095 Block 5 Answer: C
GYNPharmacologyProstaglandin Physiology

Primary dysmenorrhea. NSAIDs

Vignette Summary
25F with severe day-1 menstrual pain. Initial pharmacotherapy.
The Discriminator
Primary dysmenorrhea is driven by endometrial prostaglandin (PGF2α) overproduction causing uterine hypercontractility and ischemia. First-line therapy: NSAIDs.
Why the Answer is Correct
NSAIDs inhibit cyclooxygenase, reducing endometrial PGF2α and PGE2 synthesis. This decreases uterine contraction amplitude and ischemic pain.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism of NSAIDs in treating primary dysmenorrhea
NBME Trap Fingerprint
