Primary dysmenorrhea = uterine contractions from prostaglandin (PGF2α and PGE2) excess → painful menses. NSAIDs inhibit COX → decrease prostaglandin synthesis → reduce uterine contractions. This is the correct mechanism.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Inhibition of estrogen synthesis. Aromatase inhibitors; used in endometriosis, not first-line dysmenorrhea.
Inhibition of leukotriene synthesis. Is the mechanism of drugs like zileuton (5-LOX inhibitor) and montelukast (leukotriene receptor antagonist) used in asthma. Leukotrienes do not primarily drive uterine contractions in primary dysmenorrhea.
(B)
Inhibition of 11β-hydroxylase. Metyrapone; cortisol/ Cushing testing.
Alpha-adrenergic receptor blockade (e.g., tamsulosin, doxazosin) relaxes smooth muscle in the prostate/bladder neck and some blood vessels. Alpha-1 receptors in the uterus are present but alpha blockade is not the mechanism of NSAID action in dysmenorrhea.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Inhibition of prostaglandin synthesis. NSAIDs are first-line.
(D)
Stimulation of FSH. Clomiphene; for ovulation induction.
Beta-adrenergic receptor stimulation. Causes uterine relaxation (tocolysis) through beta-2 receptors. Terbutaline and ritodrine work this way to stop preterm labor. NSAIDs do not stimulate beta receptors.
(E)
Stimulation of LH. Not therapeutic for dysmenorrhea.
Direct myometrial relaxation via calcium channel blockade. Nifedipine is used for tocolysis through calcium channel blockade. NSAIDs do not directly block calcium channels in uterine smooth muscle.
Costume Change
Reappears as: primary dysmenorrhea treatment. NSAIDs (first line, most effective → start 1-2 days before menses), OCPs (reduce prostaglandin production by suppressing ovulation and endometrial proliferation). Or: secondary dysmenorrhea (endometriosis, adenomyosis, fibroids). Must rule out secondary causes if NSAIDs fail.
Variable Pivot
If NSAIDs fail → try OCPs (suppress ovulation, thin endometrium, reduce PG). If secondary dysmenorrhea suspected → pelvic ultrasound + laparoscopy for endometriosis. If the question asked about mechanism of oxytocin → binds GPCR → IP3/DAG → intracellular Ca2+ → uterine contraction.
Tid BitPrimary dysmenorrhea → NSAIDs first; OCPs if refractory.
Q096 Block 5 Answer: D
NephrologyADH Physiology

Exercise/heat-induced volume depletion

Vignette Summary
60F mid-marathon collapse in 90°F heat → hypotension, tachycardia, dry mucous membranes, decreased turgor.
The Discriminator
Hypovolemia + hyperosmolar plasma → maximal ADH (vasopressin) signaling at the collecting duct.
Why the Answer is Correct
Volume loss and elevated plasma osmolality stimulate hypothalamic osmoreceptors and baroreceptors, releasing ADH from the posterior pituitary. ADH binds V2 receptors on principal cells, activating cAMP-PKA, which inserts aquaporin-2 channels into the apical membrane. This raises water reabsorption and urine osmolality.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cellular mechanism responsible for water reabsorption after exercise-induced dehydration
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Dehydration → increased plasma osmolality → ADH release → ADH binds V2 receptors in collecting duct → cAMP → PKA → phosphorylation and insertion of AQUAPORIN-2 channels into apical membrane → water reabsorption.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased ADH receptors. Opposite; ADH signaling is up.
Decreased expression of Na+/K+ ATPase would impair the sodium gradient driving renal concentration. It would cause salt wasting and isosthenuria, not enhanced water retention. The Na+/K+ ATPase is at the basolateral membrane of tubular cells and maintains the electrochemical gradient.
(B)
Decreased renin. RAAS is activated, not suppressed.
Increased activity of Na+/K+/2Cl- cotransporter (NKCC2) in the thick ascending limb is the mechanism of creating the medullary concentration gradient. Loop diuretics block NKCC2. While NKCC2 creates the gradient necessary for water reabsorption, the specific response to ADH is aquaporin insertion, not NKCC2 upregulation.
(C)
Decreased urine osmolality. ADH concentrates urine.
Increased activity of the Na+/H+ exchanger occurs in response to acidosis and in proximal tubular reabsorption. It is not the mechanism of ADH-mediated water reabsorption.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Increased aquaporin expression. ADH inserts AQP2.
(E)
Increased GFR. Hypovolemia drops GFR.
(F)
Increased albumin reabsorption. Not a regulated response to dehydration.
Costume Change
Reappears as: ADH mechanisms. V1 receptor (vascular smooth muscle) → vasoconstriction. V2 receptor (collecting duct) → cAMP → aquaporin-2 insertion → water reabsorption. Or: diabetes insipidus central (no ADH) vs nephrogenic (no V2 receptor response or aquaporin insertion). Or: which diuretic causes nephrogenic DI? → Lithium (blocks cAMP pathway for aquaporin insertion).
Variable Pivot
If V1 receptor activated → vasoconstriction (relevant in septic shock: vasopressin analog). If nephrogenic DI → ADH present but aquaporin-2 doesn't insert (V2 receptor or downstream signaling blocked. Lithium). If SIADH → too much ADH → too many aquaporin channels → water retention → hyponatremia.
Tid BitADH = AQP2 to the apical membrane of the collecting duct.
Q097 Block 5 Answer: D
Infectious DiseaseMicrobiologyVirology

Mumps parotitis

Vignette Summary
19M college freshman, 4 days of tender swollen parotid glands + fever, malaise.
The Discriminator
Bilateral parotitis in an unvaccinated/under-vaccinated young adult in a dorm setting = mumps virus.
Why the Answer is Correct
Mumps virus is a single-stranded RNA Paramyxovirus that infects salivary epithelium via the HN and F glycoproteins, producing painful parotid inflammation. Complications include orchitis, aseptic meningitis, and pancreatitis. College outbreaks recur when herd immunity wanes.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the virus causing parotid gland swelling in a college student without vaccination history
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Parotid gland swelling (parotitis) + college student + unvaccinated = mumps virus. Mumps is the classic cause of viral parotitis.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
EBV. Mononucleosis with cervical/posterior lymphadenopathy, splenomegaly, not parotitis.
Coxsackievirus A. Causes hand-foot-and-mouth disease (oral vesicles, palmar and plantar lesions) and herpangina (posterior pharyngeal vesicles). It does not cause parotid swelling.
(B)
HBV. Hepatitis, not salivary gland.
Cytomegalovirus (CMV). Causes mononucleosis-like illness in immunocompetent hosts (heterophile-negative mono) and disseminated disease in immunocompromised patients. CMV does not specifically cause parotitis.
(C)
Measles. Koplik spots, maculopapular rash.
Herpes simplex virus. Causes oral ulcers (HSV-1), genital ulcers (HSV-2), encephalitis, and keratitis. HSV does not cause parotid swelling.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Mumps. Parotitis.
(E)
Rubella. Postauricular lymphadenopathy and rash.
Rubella virus. Causes German measles. Maculopapular rash that spreads craniocaudally + postauricular/occipital lymphadenopathy + arthralgias in adults. Rubella does NOT cause parotid gland swelling.
Costume Change
Reappears as: mumps complications. Orchitis (most common complication in post-pubertal males, can cause infertility), aseptic meningitis, pancreatitis (elevated amylase/lipase from parotid AND pancreatic involvement), sensorineural hearing loss. Or: MMR vaccine → prevents measles, mumps, rubella. Mumps resurgent in vaccinated populations due to waning immunity.
Variable Pivot
If parotid swelling + immunocompromised patient → consider HIV parotitis or salivary gland lymphoma. If unilateral parotid swelling + fever + fluctuance → bacterial parotitis (Staph aureus, in dehydrated elderly patients). If bilateral parotid enlargement chronically → Sjögren syndrome, sarcoidosis, alcoholic liver disease.
Tid BitMumps = swollen parotids + orchitis + meningitis in an unvaccinated young adult.
Q098 Block 5 Answer: D
OncologyImmunologyCheckpoint Inhibitors

CTLA-4 checkpoint blockade in melanoma

Vignette Summary
64M with metastatic, unresectable melanoma → choose antibody target for immunotherapy.
The Discriminator
Anti-CTLA-4 (ipilimumab) is an established checkpoint inhibitor for advanced melanoma.
Why the Answer is Correct
CTLA-4 on activated T cells binds B7 on APCs with higher affinity than CD28, transmitting an inhibitory signal that down-regulates T-cell activation. Blocking CTLA-4 with ipilimumab keeps CD28-B7 co-stimulation dominant, sustaining anti-tumor T-cell responses.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the immune checkpoint mechanism of ipilimumab for metastatic melanoma
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Ipilimumab is the prototypical anti-CTLA-4 antibody. CTLA-4 on T cells normally competes with CD28 for B7 ligand on APCs. CTLA-4 sends an inhibitory signal. Blocking CTLA-4 allows CD28 to activate T cells unopposed.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
B7. Blocking B7 would abolish CD28 co-stimulation and suppress immunity.
BCR-ABL kinase. Imatinib (Gleevec) inhibits the BCR-ABL fusion kinase in CML. This is a targeted therapy for a specific molecular target in leukemia, not an immune checkpoint for melanoma.
(B)
CD28. Same problem; blocking it disables T-cell activation.
CD20. Rituximab targets CD20 on B cells, used in NHL and autoimmune conditions. Melanoma is not a B cell tumor and CD20 is not expressed on melanocytes.
(C)
CD40. Important for B-cell/APC activation; not a tumor checkpoint target.
PD-1. Pembrolizumab and nivolumab block the PD-1/PD-L1 axis. This is a DIFFERENT checkpoint mechanism than CTLA-4. PD-1 inhibitors prevent tumor cells from deactivating T cells at the tumor site. CTLA-4 inhibitors (ipilimumab) work earlier, at the priming stage in lymph nodes.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
CTLA-4. Ipilimumab for melanoma.
(E)
FcγR. Receptor on innate cells; not a checkpoint.
VEGF. Bevacizumab inhibits VEGF to prevent tumor angiogenesis. This is an anti-angiogenic strategy, not an immune checkpoint mechanism.
Costume Change
Reappears as: immune checkpoint inhibitors. Anti-CTLA-4 (ipilimumab), anti-PD-1 (pembrolizumab, nivolumab), anti-PD-L1 (atezolizumab). Combination therapy (ipilimumab + nivolumab) has higher response rate in melanoma but more autoimmune side effects. Immune-related adverse events: colitis, pneumonitis, hepatitis, endocrinopathies (hypothyroidism, adrenal insufficiency).
Variable Pivot
If the checkpoint inhibitor caused diarrhea/colitis → ipilimumab > PD-1 inhibitors. If the question described PD-L1 expression on tumor cells → relevant for anti-PD-L1 therapy response prediction. If melanoma with BRAF V600E mutation → vemurafenib (BRAF inhibitor) is targeted therapy option.
Tid BitCTLA-4 = early checkpoint at lymph node; PD-1 = late checkpoint at tumor.
Q099 Block 5 Answer: D
Behavioral ScienceMedical EthicsCommunication

Professional medical interpreter

Vignette Summary
Mandarin-speaking patient with new pancreatic cancer diagnosis → husband (bilingual) insists on interpreting → hospital interpreter unavailable until morning.
The Discriminator
Family members should not serve as medical interpreters because of confidentiality, accuracy, and emotional bias issues. A telephone interpreter is available and meets the standard.
Why the Answer is Correct
Best practice and legal/ethical standards require use of a qualified medical interpreter. Phone interpreter services are 24/7 alternatives when in-person interpreters are unavailable. They preserve patient autonomy and confidentiality without burdening or biasing a family member.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the appropriate interpreter to use when discussing serious diagnosis with a non-English speaking patient
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The patient's husband should NOT be the interpreter for serious medical conversations. Family members have conflicts of interest, may filter information, and remove the patient's autonomy. A professional medical interpreter (in person or via phone) is required.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Husband interprets. Risks inaccuracy, emotional filtering, breaches autonomy.
Using a live telephone interpreter (professional medical interpreter service) when no in-person interpreter is available IS appropriate and is better than using a family member. This may be close to the correct answer depending on the exact options. A professional telephone interpreter is the right fallback when in-person is unavailable.
(B)
Ethics committee. Not needed; clear standard exists.
Explaining through gestures and written notes. Is a last resort when NO interpreter is available at all. It is inferior to a professional interpreter and not appropriate for discussing a serious diagnosis like cancer.
(C)
Delay until morning. Denies timely informed consent; unnecessary when phone services exist.
Asking the husband to interpret. Family members should generally NOT be used as interpreters for serious medical discussions because they may filter information (not share bad news to protect the patient), may have their own interests, and the patient may not speak freely about symptoms, abuse, or concerns. The husband's role as a primary support could compromise honest communication.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Telephone interpreter. Qualified, available, preserves autonomy.
Costume Change
Reappears as: medical interpreter ethics. Professional interpreters are required for medical discussions in patients with limited English proficiency (Title VI of Civil Rights Act in US). Children should NEVER interpret for parents. Or: informed consent requires that the patient genuinely understands. This cannot happen without proper interpretation.
Variable Pivot
If the patient were an emergency and needed immediate consent for life-saving procedure → use the best available interpreter (phone) and document. If the patient explicitly requested that their husband interpret and was capacitated → respect the patient's autonomy (though document the discussion). If the patient were a minor being interpreted by a sibling → refuse and find professional interpreter.
Tid BitFamily ≠ interpreter; always use a certified medical interpreter.
Q100 Block 5 Answer: C
Infectious DiseasePharmacology

Pertussis. Macrolide therapy

Vignette Summary
5-month-old, unvaccinated, 10 days of paroxysmal coughing spells with post-tussive emesis and inspiratory “gasp” (whoop).
The Discriminator
Classic Bordetella pertussis in an unvaccinated infant → treat with a macrolide (azithromycin first-line).
Why the Answer is Correct
B. Pertussis produces pertussis toxin (ADP-ribosylates Gi → unopposed cAMP) and tracheal cytotoxin damaging ciliated epithelium. Macrolides (azithromycin, erythromycin) inhibit the bacterial 50S ribosome, eradicating nasopharyngeal carriage and limiting transmission. Treatment is most effective in the catarrhal stage but is still indicated in the paroxysmal stage to reduce spread.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the antibiotic class for pertussis treatment and prophylaxis
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Bordetella pertussis = whooping cough = macrolide antibiotic (azithromycin is first line, erythromycin second line). Treatment reduces contagiousness but may not shorten illness duration if started in the catarrhal phase.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Cephalosporin. Poor activity against B. Pertussis.
Aminoglycosides (gentamicin, tobramycin) treat gram-negative rod infections, including Pseudomonas. Not indicated for Bordetella pertussis.
(B)
Fluoroquinolone. Not first-line; avoided in infants/children.
Cephalosporins. Have variable gram-positive and gram-negative coverage but are not the first-line agent for pertussis. Bordetella responds specifically to macrolides.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Macrolide. Azithromycin for pertussis.
(D)
Penicillin. Ineffective against B. Pertussis.
Penicillins cover gram-positive organisms primarily. Bordetella pertussis is gram-negative and not reliably covered by standard penicillins.
(E)
Sulfonamide. TMP-SMX is second-line if macrolide intolerant; not first choice.
Tetracyclines (doxycycline). Have broad-spectrum activity and are alternatives for pertussis in adults > 8 years old when macrolides cannot be used. However, they are NOT first-line and are contraindicated in young children (dental staining, impaired bone growth) and infants.
Costume Change
Reappears as: pertussis clinical stages. Catarrhal (1-2 weeks, most contagious, cold-like), paroxysmal (1-6 weeks, characteristic whooping cough, post-tussive vomiting), convalescent (resolution). Or: pertussis in infants → apnea rather than whoop (airways too small). Prevention: Tdap vaccination, prophylaxis of household contacts with azithromycin.
Variable Pivot
If the patient were a pregnant woman exposed to pertussis → give azithromycin prophylaxis. If the question asked about treatment of atypical pneumonia (Mycoplasma, Chlamydophila, Legionella) → macrolides or fluoroquinolones. If whooping cough in adult → antibiotics reduce transmission even if disease is established.
Tid BitWhooping cough = unvaccinated infant + paroxysms + post-tussive vomiting → azithromycin. NBME Free 120 (2026). Block 6 Answer Explanations
Q101 Block 6 Answer: F
NeurosciencePharmacologySympathomimetics

Amphetamine sympathomimetic mechanism

Vignette Summary
25M amphetamine user → tremor, palpitations, HTN, tachycardia → mechanism of sympathomimetic effect.
The Discriminator
Amphetamines are indirect sympathomimetics that act primarily at the presynaptic terminal.
Why the Answer is Correct
Amphetamine enters the presynaptic neuron via DAT/NET, then enters synaptic vesicles via VMAT and displaces stored monoamines (NE, DA, 5-HT) into the cytoplasm. It also reverses the direction of DAT/NET so cytoplasmic catecholamines spill into the synapse. Net effect: massive non- exocytotic release of biogenic amines → adrenergic surge → HTN, tachycardia, tremor, anxiety.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism of amphetamine at the presynaptic noradrenergic/dopaminergic terminal
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Amphetamines are NOT simple reuptake inhibitors (like cocaine). They actively cause RELEASE of stored catecholamines from the terminal, specifically by reversing the monoamine transporter and displacing vesicular stores.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Decreased intracellular metabolism of biogenic amines. That is the MAOI mechanism (eg, phenelzine), not amphetamine.
Reuptake inhibition. Is the mechanism of cocaine (blocks DAT, NET, SERT) and some antidepressants (TCAs, SNRIs, SSRIs). Amphetamines ALSO inhibit reuptake to some degree, but their PRIMARY mechanism is forcing monoamine release. If this answer said "reuptake inhibition" it would be partially correct for cocaine, not the full mechanism for amphetamines.
(B)
Decreased MAO activity. Same as A; wrong drug class.
Increased monoamine oxidase activity. Would DEGRADE catecholamines → reduce synaptic DA/NE. This is the opposite of the amphetamine effect.
(C)
Decreased presynaptic receptor activation. α2 autoreceptor blockade would increase release, but amphetamine doesn't act here.
Blocking postsynaptic receptors. Would reduce catecholamine signaling. This is the antipsychotic/alpha-blocker/beta-blocker mechanism. Amphetamines are sympathomimetics, not sympatholytics.
(D)
Increased intracellular metabolism of biogenic amines. Would lower, not raise, synaptic catecholamines.
Increased vesicular packaging (VMAT2 activity increase). Would store more monoamines in vesicles. Amphetamines actually do the OPPOSITE: they displace monoamines from vesicles into the cytoplasm for release via reversed transporter.
(E)
Increased presynaptic receptor activation. α2 stimulation (clonidine) decreases NE release; reverse mechanism.
Stimulating autoreceptors. Would inhibit further neurotransmitter release (autoreceptors are inhibitory). Amphetamines actually BYPASS autoreceptor regulation by forcing nonvesicular release.
(F)
CORRECT ANSWER
Increased release of biogenic amines. VMAT reversal + DAT/NET reversal dump cytoplasmic NE/ DA into the synapse.
Costume Change
Reappears as: amphetamine vs cocaine. Both cause euphoria and are addictive. Cocaine: blocks reuptake transporters (DAT, NET, SERT). Amphetamine: reverses transporters → forces release. Both increase synaptic catecholamines. Or: MDMA (ecstasy) uses same mechanism but primarily for SEROTONIN release + some DA/NE.
Variable Pivot
If the question were about cocaine mechanism → blocks DAT (reuptake inhibition). If about TCA mechanism → blocks NE and 5-HT reuptake (antidepressant) + anticholinergic + Na channel block (arrhythmia). If about MAO inhibitors → block MAO → more catecholamine stored and available for release.
Tid BitCocaine blocks reuptake; amphetamine forces release. Both end in a catecholamine storm.
Q102 Block 6 Answer: E
CardiologyGeneticsConnective Tissue

Marfan with acute aortic dissection and AR

Vignette Summary
19M, 6'5" (marfanoid) → sharp interscapular back pain → collapse, hypotension, tachycardia → diastolic murmur at LLSB.
The Discriminator
Tall young man + tearing back pain + new diastolic murmur = type A aortic dissection producing acute aortic regurgitation.
Why the Answer is Correct
Marfan syndrome (fibrillin-1 defect) weakens the aortic media → cystic medial degeneration → ascending aortic dissection. As the dissection propagates proximally, it dilates the aortic root and stretches the aortic anulus, pulling the cusps apart so they cannot coapt. Result: acute severe AR with a diastolic murmur, falling diastolic pressure, and cardiogenic shock.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the structural cardiac complication of Marfan syndrome causing aortic regurgitation and dissection
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Tall young man + aortic regurgitation + back pain + hypotension = aortic root dissection from cystic medial necrosis of the aorta in Marfan syndrome. The aortic valve leaflets are anatomically normal. The regurgitation is from stretched aortic annulus/root.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Atrial septal defect. Fixed split S2 with systolic flow murmur; not diastolic, not acute collapse.
Bicuspid aortic valve. Causes AS (most commonly) or AR through a dysmorphic valve mechanism. Bicuspid AV is associated with aortopathy but is not the specific finding in Marfan. Marfan produces aortic ROOT dilation with normal (trileaflet) valve anatomy. The regurgitation from Marfan is functional (dilated root), not structural (abnormal leaflet).
(B)
Mitral stenosis. Diastolic rumble at apex, opening snap, chronic course; doesn't appear acutely.
Dilated cardiomyopathy. Involves ventricular dilation and systolic dysfunction. This would produce biventricular heart failure, not isolated aortic regurgitation. DCM is not the mechanism here.
(C)
Papillary muscle rupture. Produces acute mitral regurgitation (systolic), usually post-MI.
Hypertrophic obstructive cardiomyopathy. Causes outflow obstruction with dynamic LVOT gradient, systolic anterior motion of mitral valve, and atypical chest pain with exertion. Not acute aortic regurgitation and dissection.
(D)
Perforated tricuspid valve. Endocarditis territory (IV drug use); produces TR (systolic), with elevated JVP. Patient has none.
Mitral valve prolapse. Is actually the most common cardiac manifestation of Marfan syndrome, but it causes MITRAL REGURGITATION (systolic murmur), not AORTIC REGURGITATION (diastolic decrescendo murmur). The question specifically describes severe aortic regurgitation + dissection → aortic root.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Stretched aortic anulus. Dissection dilates the root → leaflets fail to coapt → acute AR.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Marfan syndrome (FBN1 mutation, fibrillin-1) → cystic medial necrosis → aortic root dilation → dissection risk. Prophylactic beta-blockers (reduce aortic wall stress). Surgical repair when aortic root > 5 cm. Or: aortic dissection presentation. Tearing chest pain radiating to the back + BP differential between arms.
Variable Pivot
If systolic murmur + midsystolic click in Marfan → mitral valve prolapse → mitral regurgitation (D). If question asked about most common cardiac complication of Marfan → MVP. If acute type A dissection (ascending) → emergency surgery. If type B dissection (descending) → medical management (beta-blockers).
Tid BitMarfan + sudden interscapular pain + diastolic murmur = type A dissection until proven otherwise.
Q103 Block 6 Answer: E
NeurologyNeuromuscularParaneoplastic

Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome

Vignette Summary
78F with bronchogenic (small cell) carcinoma → prolonged paralysis after pancuronium → biopsy: low EPP, normal MEPP, normal response to applied ACh.
The Discriminator
MEPP (quantal size) normal but EPP (number of quanta released) decreased → presynaptic ACh release defect, not postsynaptic.
Why the Answer is Correct
Lambert-Eaton syndrome is a paraneoplastic disorder (most commonly small cell lung cancer) caused by IgG antibodies against presynaptic voltage-gated P/Q-type Ca²⁺ channels at the motor nerve terminal. Less Ca²⁺ entry → fewer vesicles released per action potential → low EPP amplitude. The postsynaptic membrane is normal, so individual quanta (MEPP) and exogenous ACh responses are unchanged. Non-depolarizing blockers like pancuronium are profoundly potentiated.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the neuromuscular junction defect site based on electrophysiology in a patient with small cell lung cancer
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Small cell lung cancer + proximal weakness that IMPROVES with repetitive stimulation + low amplitude compound motor action potential (CMAP) that INCREASES with high-frequency stimulation = Lambert-Eaton myasthenic syndrome (LEMS) = presynaptic calcium channel antibodies (VGCC-Ab).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Pancuronium adverse effect. Pancuronium would decrease ACh response, but applied ACh response is normal.
Acetylcholine deficiency. Myasthenia gravis and LEMS both involve insufficient ACh release or receptor blockade, but the mechanism differs. LEMS has REDUCED calcium influx at the presynaptic terminal → reduced vesicle fusion → reduced ACh release. The deficiency is secondary to impaired calcium channel function, not a primary ACh synthesis problem.
(B)
Sevoflurane adverse effect. Sevoflurane potentiates blockade but is short-lived and would also blunt postsynaptic response.
Acetylcholinesterase. Acetylcholinesterase inhibitors (pyridostigmine) actually improve MG symptoms by preventing ACh degradation. Inhibiting AChE is TREATMENT, not the pathophysiology.
(C)
Cholinergic crisis. Excess ACh → depolarization block; would not give low EPP with normal MEPP.
Postsynaptic acetylcholine receptor antibodies. This is the mechanism of MYASTHENIA GRAVIS (anti-AChR antibodies or anti-MuSK antibodies). MG is characterized by fatigable weakness that WORSENS with repetitive stimulation (decremental response on EMG). LEMS shows INCREMENTAL response (improves with repetition). This is the classic differentiator.
(D)
Myasthenia gravis. Postsynaptic AChR antibodies → low MEPP and low ACh response; opposite pattern.
Nerve action potential generation (voltage-gated sodium channels). Impaired sodium channels would prevent action potential propagation entirely, producing weakness without the specific presynaptic calcium channel dysfunction of LEMS.
(E)
CORRECT ANSWER
Lambert-Eaton. Presynaptic VGCC antibodies → reduced quantal release → low EPP, preserved MEPP.
Costume Change
Reappears as: LEMS vs MG distinction. MG: decremental response on EMG, anti-AChR Ab, thymic abnormality (thymoma or hyperplasia), ocular/bulbar > proximal limbs. LEMS: incremental response on EMG, anti-VGCC Ab, associated with small cell lung cancer, proximal limb weakness, autonomic dysfunction (dry mouth, ED). Or: Eaton-Lambert treatment. 3,4-diaminopyridine (DAP) increases ACh release by prolonging presynaptic depolarization.
Variable Pivot
If ocular drooping + diplopia + fatigable weakness + decremental EMG + AChR Ab → myasthenia gravis. If proximal weakness + autonomic features + incremental EMG + SCLC → LEMS. If bulbar paralysis + descending flaccid paralysis + toxin exposure → botulism (also presynaptic, blocks SNARE proteins for ACh vesicle fusion).
Tid BitMG = postsynaptic (worsens with use); LEMS = presynaptic (improves with use, paraneoplastic SCLC).
Q104 Block 6 Answer: C
EndocrinologyCalciumGenetics

Familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia

Vignette Summary
28M, asymptomatic, family hypercalcemia → Ca 11.3, PTH 112, urine Ca 42 mg/24h, FE-Ca \<1%.
The Discriminator
Hypercalcemia + inappropriately normal/high PTH + low urinary calcium (FE \<1%) = FHH, not primary hyperparathyroidism.
Why the Answer is Correct
FHH is an autosomal dominant inactivating mutation of the calcium-sensing receptor (CaSR) on parathyroid chief cells and the renal thick ascending limb. The set point for calcium sensing is reset upward: the parathyroid does not suppress PTH at normally hypercalcemic levels, and the kidney avidly reabsorbs calcium despite the high serum level. Result: mild lifelong hypercalcemia with paradoxically low urinary calcium and no end-organ damage. Parathyroidectomy is unnecessary and ineffective.
Cognitive Demand
Explain why calcium-sensing receptor mutation produces familial hypocalciuric hypercalcemia (FHH)
NBME Trap Fingerprint
CaSR normally senses Ca2+ and when activated, tells the parathyroid to STOP making PTH and tells the kidney to EXCRETE Ca2+. Inactivating CaSR mutation → parathyroid "thinks" Ca2+ is low → continues making PTH → elevated Ca2+. Kidneys also "think" Ca2+ is low → reabsorb Ca2+ instead of excreting → hypocalciuria.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CGRP receptor. Vasodilator/pain pathway; not in calcium homeostasis.
CGRP receptor. Calcitonin gene-related peptide is a potent vasodilator and pain pathway neuromodulator. CGRP receptor antagonists (erenumab, fremanezumab, galcanezumab) are used to prevent migraine. CGRP has no role in calcium homeostasis. A CGRP receptor mutation would not produce hypercalcemia of any kind.
(B)
Calcitonin receptor. Calcitonin plays a minor role in humans; defect doesn't cause this picture.
Calcitonin receptor. Calcitonin is secreted by parafollicular C-cells of the thyroid in response to hypercalcemia and modestly inhibits osteoclast activity. In humans calcitonin plays only a minor role in calcium regulation. Loss of the calcitonin receptor does not produce significant hypercalcemia. Patients post-thyroidectomy lose calcitonin entirely without developing FHH.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Calcium-sensing receptor. Inactivating CaSR mutation → reset upward, low urinary Ca. \
(D)
PTH receptor. Inactivating mutation = pseudohypoparathyroidism (hypocalcemia, high PTH).
PTH receptor (PTH1R). Inactivating mutations cause pseudohypoparathyroidism type Ia (Albright hereditary osteodystrophy). The target organ cannot respond to PTH, so calcium stays LOW while PTH rises to compensate. This is the opposite pattern of FHH. FHH has high calcium with high PTH, not low calcium with high PTH.
(E)
RANK. Osteoclast differentiation; mutations cause osteopetrosis-spectrum, not hypercalcemia with low urinary Ca.
RANK receptor. Receptor activator of nuclear factor kappa-B sits on osteoclast precursors. RANKL binding promotes osteoclast differentiation and bone resorption. Inactivating mutations cause osteopetrosis (deficient osteoclast activity, dense brittle bone). Activating mutations cause familial expansile osteolysis. Neither produces the FHH pattern of hypercalcemia with low urine calcium.
(F)
Vitamin D receptor. Defect → hereditary vitamin D-resistant rickets (hypocalcemia).
Vitamin D receptor (VDR). Inactivating mutations cause hereditary vitamin D-resistant rickets type II. Despite high calcitriol levels, target organs cannot respond. Patients have hypocalcemia, hypophosphatemia, secondary hyperparathyroidism, alopecia, and rickets. Calcium is LOW, not high.
Costume Change
Reappears as: FHH vs primary hyperparathyroidism distinction. Both cause elevated Ca2+ + elevated PTH. KEY difference: urine calcium excretion. FHH → VERY LOW urine Ca (fractional excretion of Ca < 1%). Primary hyperparathyroidism → HIGH urine Ca. FHH requires NO treatment (benign lifelong condition). Parathyroidectomy does NOT cure FHH (remove parathyroids, Ca stays elevated because the kidney still reabsorbs it maximally).
Variable Pivot
If elevated Ca + elevated PTH + HIGH urine calcium → primary hyperparathyroidism (parathyroid adenoma in 80%). If elevated Ca + suppressed PTH → malignancy (humoral or local). If FHH pattern (elevated Ca + normal PTH + low urine Ca) → CaSR mutation → reassure, no surgery. If patient had MEN1 → parathyroid hyperplasia + pituitary + pancreatic tumors.
Tid BitFHH = high Ca + low urine Ca. If FE-Ca \<1%, do NOT operate.
Q105 Block 6 Answer: C
Palliative CareMedical Ethics

Goals-of-care conversation

Vignette Summary
32F metastatic breast cancer, recurrent malignant pleural effusions, not curable, says she is "done" → best physician response.
The Discriminator
Patient is expressing a values-driven shift; the physician should explore goals of care, not redirect, dismiss, or pathologize.
Why the Answer is Correct
Open-ended elicitation of goals respects autonomy, gathers data needed to make a value-aligned plan (eg, hospice, comfort-directed thoracentesis vs PleurX vs no procedure), and validates her experience without abandoning her. It is the standard NBME-correct response when a patient with advanced illness signals shifting priorities.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the most appropriate physician response when a patient declining further thoracenteses in the context of malignancy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Patient declining thoracenteses → physician should explore what she wants from the remaining time (goals of care conversation). Not override, not push, not abandon.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
"Sorry you're giving up". Judgmental and minimizing; labels her decision pejoratively.
Referring to oncology for a different treatment plan. While multidisciplinary care is important, the immediate issue is the patient's decision about thoracentesis and what matters to her. Immediately referencing other doctors avoids the physician's responsibility to have the goals-of-care conversation.
(B)
"Endure for your wife and children". Coercive, violates autonomy.
Explaining the benefits of thoracentesis again. The patient has had 4 thoracenteses already. She understands the procedure. Re-explaining benefits after she's declined is applying the righting reflex. Trying to change her mind rather than understanding her perspective. It is paternalistic.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
"Tell me about your goals". Patient-centered, opens shared decision-making.
(D)
"See colleague for depression". Premature; no evidence of major depression, and her decision is reasonable given prognosis.
Telling the patient she. Will get more short of breath without thoracentesis: this is an implicit threat or guilt tactic. Using fear to change the patient's mind. While accurate as medical information, delivering it in this context is coercive rather than supportive.
(E)
"Common to hate thoracentesis". Deflects from her stated wish; doesn't address goals.
Documenting that the patient is not compliant. Labeling a patient as "noncompliant" for making an autonomous decision about her own care is both ethically wrong and clinically inappropriate. Patients have the right to decline interventions. Non-compliance implies an obligation to comply. There is no such obligation.
Costume Change
Reappears as: goals of care conversations with seriously ill patients. The correct response is always open-ended, patient-centered: "Help me understand what's most important to you right now" or "What are you hoping for?" or "What are you most worried about?" The physician's role is to provide information and support patient decision-making, not to make decisions for patients.
Variable Pivot
If the patient lacked decision-making capacity → determine surrogate, apply previously stated wishes if known. If the patient were suicidal (requesting refusal of all treatment to die faster) → psychiatric evaluation for treatable depression first. If the patient wanted all aggressive measures → honor her autonomy for full code status.
Tid BitWhen a dying patient says "I'm done," explore goals before fixing logistics.
Q106 Block 6 Answer: C
EndocrinologyGeneticsEmbryology

46,XX virilized newborn

Vignette Summary
Term newborn, ambiguous genitalia (vagina present + phallus with urethra at the base), 46,XX karyotype.
The Discriminator
46,XX with virilization but normal müllerian structures → fetal androgen excess. The classic intrinsic cause is 21-hydroxylase deficiency; the question lists maternal androgen exposure as the only valid androgen-source option.
Why the Answer is Correct
A 46,XX fetus lacks SRY → ovaries form and müllerian ducts develop normally (uterus/vagina present). External genitalia masculinize only if androgens act on the urogenital sinus/ tubercle during weeks 8-12. Maternal sources of androgens (luteoma of pregnancy, androgen-secreting tumor, exogenous androgens/anabolic steroids) cross the placenta and virilize the female fetus, producing clitoromegaly and labial fusion as seen here.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the karyotype of a virilized infant with ambiguous genitalia and normal female internal anatomy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Phallic structure + urethral opening at base + vagina present = virilized genetic female from excess androgen exposure during development. Normal female internal anatomy (mullerian structures present) = Y chromosome absent = 46,XX.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
17α-hydroxyprogesterone deficiency. Non-standard; 17α-hydroxylase deficiency causes 46,XX under-virilization with HTN and hypokalemia.
45,X (Turner syndrome) → streak gonads, no sex hormone production, phenotypically female, no virilization. Turner would present with sexual infantilism at puberty, not virilization.
(B)
Increased MIS. Would regress müllerian structures; this patient has a vagina, arguing against it.
46,XY with normal female phenotype → androgen insensitivity syndrome. These patients have testes (intraabdominal), high testosterone, no uterus (AMH produced by testes inhibits mullerian development), blind vagina. They do NOT present with virilization. They present as normal-appearing females.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Maternal androgen exposure. Transplacental androgens virilize external genitalia in 46,XX.
(D)
SRY gene present. Would produce 46,XX testicular DSD with male internal anatomy (no vagina/uterus).
47,XXY (Klinefelter) → phenotypically male, hypogonadism, gynecomastia, infertility. Cannot explain virilization of a genetic female with female internal anatomy.
(E)
5α- reductase deficiency. Affects 46,XY fetuses (undervirilized males); irrelevant in XX.
47,XYY → tall males, no significant hormonal abnormality, phenotypically normal males. Cannot explain ambiguous genitalia or female internal anatomy.
Costume Change
Reappears as: CAH (21-hydroxylase deficiency) in a 46,XX infant = classic cause of virilized female. Or: exogenous androgen exposure (mother on anabolic steroids, maternal androgen-secreting tumor) = same phenotype without enzyme defect. Or: key distinction → 46,XX with male external genitalia has FEMALE internal anatomy (uterus, tubes, ovaries) + is FERTILE after reconstruction.
Variable Pivot
If 46,XY with female phenotype + no uterus + intraabdominal testes + high testosterone → complete AIS (androgen insensitivity). If 46,XX + virilized + elevated 17-OHP → CAH (21-hydroxylase deficiency). If 46,XY + ambiguous + low testosterone → 5-alpha reductase deficiency (converts testosterone → DHT; absence impairs virilization of external genitalia despite normal testosterone).
Tid Bit46,XX virilization → think CAH (21-OH) first; if listed answer is exogenous, maternal androgens.
Q107 Block 6 Answer: D
DermatologyPharmacologyAntifungals

Tinea pedis treatment

Vignette Summary
37M dishwasher, chronically wet feet → itchy plantar/interdigital eruption → tinea pedis → drug target.
The Discriminator
Dermatophyte infection of the feet → first-line oral/topical agent for moccasin/ extensive tinea is terbinafine.
Why the Answer is Correct
Terbinafine inhibits fungal squalene monooxygenase (squalene epoxidase), blocking conversion of squalene to lanosterol. Two consequences: ergosterol depletion (membrane synthesis failure) and toxic intracellular squalene accumulation, which is fungicidal against dermatophytes.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the enzyme target of terbinafine in treating tinea pedis (athlete's foot)
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Terbinafine inhibits squalene monooxygenase (squalene epoxidase) → squalene accumulates (toxic) → ergosterol depleted → fungal cell membrane disruption. This is distinct from azoles, which inhibit a LATER step (lanosterol 14-alpha demethylase).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Chitin synthase. Target of echinocandins? No. Echinocandins hit glucan. Nikkomycins hit chitin; not used.
14-alpha-demethylase (CYP51, lanosterol demethylase): this is the target of AZOLE antifungals (fluconazole, itraconazole, ketoconazole, voriconazole). Azoles inhibit a step downstream from terbinafine in the ergosterol synthesis pathway. NBME often asks students to distinguish terbinafine from azoles.
(B)
1,3-β-D-glucan synthase. Echinocandins (caspofungin); used for invasive Candida/Aspergillus, not dermatophytes.
Glucan synthase (beta-1,3-glucan synthase). This is the target of ECHINOCANDINS (caspofungin, micafungin, anidulafungin). Echinocandins disrupt the fungal cell wall (not membrane). Used for invasive Candida and Aspergillus.
(C)
Phospholipase D. Not a clinical antifungal target.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Squalene monooxygenase. Terbinafine's target; drug of choice for tinea pedis.
Protein synthesis (fungal ribosomes). Some antifungals work at the ribosome level, but terbinafine is not one of them. This is more relevant to bacterial antibiotics (aminoglycosides, macrolides, tetracyclines).
(E)
Thymidylate synthase. Flucytosine target (for Cryptococcus/Candida), not dermatophytes.
DNA synthesis/topoisomerase. Flucytosine (5-FC) is incorporated into fungal RNA and inhibits DNA synthesis. Not the mechanism of terbinafine.
Costume Change
Reappears as: antifungal target map. Polyenes (amphotericin B, nystatin) bind ergosterol directly → membrane disruption. Azoles inhibit 14-alpha-demethylase → block ergosterol synthesis. Allylamines (terbinafine) inhibit squalene epoxidase → earlier block + squalene accumulation. Echinocandins inhibit glucan synthase → cell wall disruption.
Variable Pivot
If the question describes a systemic antifungal for invasive aspergillosis → voriconazole (azole). If topical for nail fungus → terbinafine (oral) or efinaconazole (topical azole). If the question asks about drug causing squalene accumulation specifically → terbinafine (squalene accumulation is toxic to fungi but not human cells, which use a different pathway).
Tid BitTerbinafine = "TERBINate the squalene epoxidase."
Q108 Block 6 Answer: B
NeurologyBrainstemEye Movements

Internuclear ophthalmoplegia

Vignette Summary
27F, 2 weeks diplopia → on rightward gaze, left eye fails to adduct past midline; leftward gaze normal.
The Discriminator
Adduction failure on contralateral gaze with intact convergence = INO; localize by the side of the adducting eye.
Why the Answer is Correct
On rightward gaze, the right abducens nucleus fires, abducting the right eye and sending a signal via the left MLF to the left oculomotor (medial rectus) subnucleus to adduct the left eye. A lesion in the left MLF disrupts that signal → left eye fails to adduct. In a young woman, multiple sclerosis is the canonical cause.
Cognitive Demand
Localize the lesion causing internuclear ophthalmoplegia (INO). Adduction failure on rightward gaze
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Left eye fails to adduct on rightward gaze = left internuclear ophthalmoplegia = LEFT medial longitudinal fasciculus (MLF) lesion. The MLF connects CN VI nucleus on one side to CN III nucleus on the other side to coordinate conjugate gaze.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Left abducens nerve. Would impair left eye abduction (leftward gaze), not adduction.
Left abducens (CN VI) nucleus. CN VI nucleus damage causes ipsilateral abduction failure AND contralateral gaze palsy (because CN VI nucleus contains interneurons that cross the midline via the MLF to activate the contralateral CN III for adduction). CN VI nucleus lesion = conjugate gaze palsy, not isolated adduction failure.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Left MLF. Disconnects left medial rectus from right abducens nucleus.
(C)
Left abducens nucleus. Would cause a left horizontal gaze palsy (neither eye moves left).
Right abducens nucleus. The right CN VI nucleus would affect rightward gaze initiation. A right CN VI palsy would cause failure of the right eye to abduct on right gaze (different from the left eye's failure to adduct).
(D)
Right abducens nerve. Would impair right eye abduction on rightward gaze.
Right medial longitudinal fasciculus. A right MLF lesion would cause failure of the RIGHT eye to adduct on LEFTWARD gaze (not rightward). The MLF lesion causes adduction failure ipsilateral to the lesion, affecting gaze in the CONTRALATERAL direction.
(E)
Right MLF. Would cause right eye adduction failure on leftward gaze (opposite picture).
Right oculomotor (CN III) nucleus. CN III nucleus damage in the midbrain would cause ipsilateral ptosis, dilated pupil, and down-out eye deviation. A full third nerve palsy, not isolated adduction failure on conjugate gaze testing.
(F)
Right abducens nucleus. Right horizontal gaze palsy; both eyes fail to move right.
Costume Change
Reappears as: INO is the most common brainstem gaze finding in MS (bilateral INO = bilateral MLF plaques = pathognomonic for MS in young adults). Or: one-and-a-half syndrome = MLF lesion + ipsilateral CN VI nucleus lesion → only contralateral abduction is preserved. Or: the WEBINO syndrome (wall-eyed bilateral INO).
Variable Pivot
If young woman + bilateral INO → MS. If elderly patient + vascular risk factors + unilateral INO → brainstem lacunar infarct. If the eye was deviated down-and-out + ptosis + blown pupil → CN III palsy (posterior communicating artery aneurysm until proven otherwise). If Parinaud syndrome (loss of upward gaze) → dorsal midbrain lesion.
Tid BitINO is named for the adducting side: left adduction fails → left MLF.
Q109 Block 6 Answer: A
OphthalmologyCardiologyHypertension

Hypertensive retinopathy

Vignette Summary
50M with longstanding HTN, BP 180/105, headache and DOE → fundoscopic finding.
The Discriminator
Chronic hypertension → arteriolar wall thickening that indents crossing venules.
Why the Answer is Correct
Sustained hypertension causes hyaline arteriolosclerosis: thickening of the arteriolar wall narrows the lumen and stiffens the vessel. Where an arteriole crosses a venule (sharing an adventitial sheath), the rigid artery compresses the underlying vein, producing the classic AV nicking seen on fundoscopy. Cotton-wool spots, flame hemorrhages, and papilledema are seen with more severe/malignant HTN.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the chronic hypertensive retinal finding on fundoscopy
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Long-standing hypertension → arteriolar wall thickening → at AV crossings, the arteriole compresses the underlying venule → AV nicking (AV nipping).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
AV nicking. Hallmark of chronic hypertensive retinopathy.
(B)
Uveal melanocytes. Found in melanoma/nevi/ocular melanocytosis, not HTN.
Cotton wool spots. Small white fluffy patches on the retina from focal nerve fiber layer infarcts. They occur in acute or poorly controlled hypertension, diabetes, HIV retinopathy, and other microvascular diseases. They represent acute ischemic injury, not the chronic thickening of AV nicking.
(C)
Optic neuritis. MS; pain with eye movement, central scotoma.
Drusen. Small yellow deposits beneath the retinal pigment epithelium. Classic finding in age-related macular degeneration (AMD). Not related to hypertension.
(D)
Posterior subcapsular cataracts. Chronic steroids/ diabetes; not a hypertensive finding.
Flame hemorrhages. Splinter-shaped retinal hemorrhages in the nerve fiber layer. They appear in hypertensive retinopathy, papilledema, and central retinal vein occlusion. They are acute hemorrhagic findings, not the chronic structural change of AV nicking.
(E)
Tractional retinal detachment. Proliferative diabetic retinopathy.
Neovascularization. New vessel growth in response to retinal ischemia. This is the hallmark of proliferative diabetic retinopathy. Not hypertensive retinopathy (hypertension causes arteriolar narrowing and wall changes, not ischemia-driven neovascularization in the retina).
Costume Change
Reappears as: hypertensive retinopathy grades. Grade 1: arteriolar narrowing. Grade 2: AV nicking. Grade 3: flame hemorrhages + cotton wool spots. Grade 4: papilledema (malignant hypertension). Or: which fundoscopic finding is pathognomonic for accelerated hypertension? → papilledema.
Variable Pivot
If blood pressure extremely elevated (>180/120) + papilledema + encephalopathy → hypertensive emergency, treat with IV labetalol or nicardipine. If cotton wool spots in a diabetic → diabetic retinopathy + ? hypertension contributing. If drusen + central visual blurring in elderly → AMD.
Tid BitHTN retinopathy ladder: AV nicking → copper/silver wiring → flame hemorrhages → cotton-wool spots → papilledema.
Q110 Block 6 Answer: D
GINeonatologyBile Physiology

Neonatal conjugated hyperbilirubinemia

Vignette Summary
16-day-old male, jaundice + dark urine, weight loss → total bili 20, direct 14 (70% direct), elevated ALP/GGT/AST/ALT.
The Discriminator
Conjugated hyperbilirubinemia in a 2-week-old with elevated GGT and ALP = biliary obstruction → biliary atresia.
Why the Answer is Correct
Biliary atresia is a progressive fibro-obliterative cholangiopathy of extrahepatic ducts presenting in the first weeks of life with conjugated jaundice, acholic stools, dark urine, and hepatomegaly. Blocked bile flow elevates conjugated bilirubin and the canalicular enzymes GGT and ALP. Urgent Kasai portoenterostomy before 8 weeks improves outcome; untreated → cirrhosis.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the pathophysiology of biliary atresia in a neonate with conjugated hyperbilirubinemia
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Conjugated (direct) hyperbilirubinemia in a neonate = bile excretion problem (not hemolysis, which causes unconjugated). Biliary atresia → bile cannot flow from liver into intestine → backs up → conjugated bilirubin in blood → jaundice + acholic stools.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
UDP-glucuronosyltransferase deficiency. Crigler-Najjar / Gilbert → unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia; GGT normal.
Increased bilirubin production (from hemolysis or massive bruising). Produces UNCONJUGATED hyperbilirubinemia. The liver conjugates bilirubin normally; the problem is too much unconjugated bilirubin arriving at the liver. Biliary atresia involves CONJUGATED bilirubin because the liver has conjugated it but cannot excrete it.
(B)
Hemolysis. Unconjugated bilirubin rise + anemia; CBC is normal here.
Impaired bilirubin uptake by hepatocytes. Causes unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia (Gilbert syndrome, Crigler-Najjar). The bilirubin hasn't even been processed yet.
(C)
Low canalicular MRP2 (Dubin-Johnson). Conjugated hyperbilirubinemia but normal GGT/ALP and asymptomatic; not this acute presentation.
Impaired bilirubin conjugation (Crigler-Najjar type I. Absent UGT1A1, severe; type II. Reduced UGT1A1, mild) produces unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia. The problem is failure to add glucuronide to bilirubin. Biliary atresia involves CONJUGATED bilirubin backing up.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Obstruction of bile flow. Biliary atresia → high direct bilirubin with elevated GGT/ALP.
Costume Change
Reappears as: conjugated vs unconjugated hyperbilirubinemia diagnostic algorithm. Conjugated (direct) = problem with excretion or obstruction. Unconjugated (indirect) = problem with production (hemolysis), uptake (Gilbert), or conjugation (Crigler-Najjar). Or: biliary atresia diagnosis → HIDA scan (no excretion into bowel), liver biopsy (bile duct proliferation + portal fibrosis), Kasai procedure (surgical hepatoportoenterostomy) before 8 weeks for best outcome.
Variable Pivot
If jaundice + hemolytic anemia + spherocytes in neonate → AIHA or hereditary spherocytosis → unconjugated. If jaundice + acholic stools + dark urine in neonate → biliary atresia → conjugated. If jaundice in 2-3 day old with high bilirubin + no acholic stools → physiological or ABO incompatibility → unconjugated.
Tid BitConjugated jaundice + high GGT in a neonate = biliary atresia until proven otherwise. Kasai by 8 weeks.
Q111 Block 6 Answer: B
NephrologyRadiologyHypertension

Renovascular HTN in a young woman

Vignette Summary
21F, BP 220/100, abdominal bruit, LVH on ECG, normal labs → cause.
The Discriminator
Young woman + abdominal bruit + severe secondary HTN = fibromuscular dysplasia of the renal arteries.
Why the Answer is Correct
FMD is a non-atherosclerotic, non-inflammatory segmental disease of medium-sized arteries (renal, carotid), predominantly in women 15-50. Medial fibroplasia produces alternating stenoses and aneurysms ("string of beads" on angiography). Renal artery stenosis → activation of RAAS → renin/ angiotensin II-mediated hypertension with an audible flank/abdominal bruit. Treat with angioplasty.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cause of hypertension in a young woman with renal artery stenosis on imaging
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Young woman + severe hypertension + abdominal bruit + "string of beads" appearance on renal artery imaging = fibromuscular dysplasia of the renal artery. The most common cause of renovascular hypertension in young women.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Extrinsic arterial compression. Rare; would suggest mass effect, not present.
Atherosclerotic disease. Is the most common cause of renal artery stenosis OVERALL (in elderly patients with risk factors). But atherosclerosis would be extremely unusual in a 28-year-old woman without traditional cardiovascular risk factors. Atherosclerotic stenosis involves the proximal renal artery, while FMD involves the mid/distal artery.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Fibromuscular dysplasia. Young woman + bruit + secondary HTN.
(C)
Mid-aortic syndrome. Rare pediatric coarctation-like narrowing; usually with leg claudication / BP differential.
Polyarteritis nodosa (PAN). Causes segmental inflammation of medium-sized arteries. Renal arteries included. But PAN produces aneurysms and infarctions rather than the characteristic beaded stenotic pattern. PAN is associated with hepatitis B and presents with systemic symptoms (fever, myalgia, livedo reticularis, mononeuritis multiplex).
(D)
Premature atherosclerosis. Possible but would expect dyslipidemia/family history; FMD is far more common at this age.
Takayasu arteritis. Is a large vessel vasculitis affecting the aorta and its primary branches. It predominantly affects young Asian women. Takayasu can cause renal artery involvement, but the characteristic imaging shows smooth, long-segment stenosis of the aorta/main renal artery. Not the mid-vessel beading of FMD. Takayasu also has systemic inflammatory symptoms (fever, elevated ESR, extremity claudication from subclavian involvement).
(E)
Spontaneous renal artery dissection. Sudden severe flank pain; not this indolent presentation.
Thromboembolism to the renal artery. Would cause acute renal infarction. Sudden severe flank pain, hematuria, elevated LDH, and acute kidney injury. It would not cause chronic severe hypertension with a bruit and a classic imaging pattern over time.
Costume Change
Reappears as: FMD diagnosis → MRA or CTA showing "string of beads" (alternating stenoses and aneurysms) in the medial fibromuscular type. Treatment: percutaneous transluminal angioplasty (PTA). Excellent results in FMD unlike atherosclerotic RAS. Or: renovascular hypertension mechanism → renal artery stenosis → decreased renal perfusion → renin-angiotensin activation → aldosterone → sodium retention → hypertension.
Variable Pivot
If elderly man + atherosclerosis risk factors + RAS → atherosclerotic RAS → ACE inhibitor or ARB (but watch creatinine). If systemic inflammatory symptoms + aortic involvement + young Asian woman → Takayasu arteritis. If young woman with no systemic symptoms + beaded mid-artery → FMD.
Tid BitFMD = "string of beads" in a young woman with renovascular HTN.
Q112 Block 6 Answer: D
BiostatisticsNormal Distribution

Normal distribution. 50th percentile

Vignette Summary
Mean leukocyte count 7500/mm3, SD 1000/mm3, gaussian distribution → which range contains approximately 50% of individuals?
The Discriminator
In a normal distribution, the mean equals the median. By definition, exactly 50% of all values fall below the mean. No range arithmetic needed. Half the population is below 7500.
Why the Answer is Correct
A gaussian distribution is perfectly symmetric around its mean. The median (the value that splits the population exactly in half) equals the mean. Therefore exactly 50% of individuals have a leukocyte count less than 7500/mm3. This is not an approximation. It is the definition of the median in a symmetric distribution.
Cognitive Demand
Calculate the percentage of values below the mean in a normal distribution
NBME Trap Fingerprint
In a perfect normal (Gaussian) distribution, the MEAN equals the MEDIAN equals the MODE. Therefore, exactly 50% of values fall below the mean. By definition.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
5500-9500. This is mean plus or minus 2 SD, capturing approximately 95% of the population. Far too wide for 50%.
34% represents the percentage of values between the mean and +1 standard deviation (or between -1 SD and the mean). Not the percentage below the mean.
(B)
Less than 6500 or greater than 8500. These are the tails outside plus or minus 1 SD, containing approximately 32% of the population. Not 50%.
68% represents the percentage of values within ±1 standard deviation of the mean (the famous 68-95-99.7 rule). Not the percentage below the mean.
(C)
6500-8500. This is mean plus or minus 1 SD, capturing approximately 68% of the population. Common trap. 68 is not 50.
This answer (6500-8500 = ±1 SD = 68%) confuses the question being asked. The question asks what percentage of values fall BELOW the MEAN (7500), not within 1 SD. The mean = median in a normal distribution, so exactly 50% fall below.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Less than 7500. CORRECT. Exactly 50% of a symmetric distribution falls below the mean. The mean IS the median.
(E)
Greater than 9500. Only approximately 2.5% of the population lies here (beyond +2 SD).
95% is the percentage within ±2 SDs. Not the percentage below the mean.
Costume Change
Reappears as: properties of the normal distribution. Mean = median = mode. 68-95-99.7 rule for ±1, ±2, ±3 SDs. Skewed distributions: if positively skewed → mean > median > mode. If negatively skewed → mode > median > mean. The median is always more robust to outliers than the mean.
Variable Pivot
If asked what percentage falls between 6500-8500 (mean ±1 SD) → 68%. If asked what percentage falls above the mean → 50%. If the distribution were positively skewed → mean would be pulled higher than the median, so > 50% would fall below the mean. If asked about the 95th percentile → approximately mean + 1.65 SD.
Tid BitMean = median in a normal distribution. Half the population is always below the mean. Do not confuse 68% (plus or minus 1 SD) with 50%.
Q113 Block 6 Answer: C
HematologyCoagulation

Glanzmann thrombasthenia

Vignette Summary
10M with mucocutaneous bleeding, affected brother, normal platelet count and morphology, normal PT/aPTT, normal ristocetin, absent aggregation to thrombin/ADP.
The Discriminator
Normal ristocetin (vWF-GpIb axis intact) but failed aggregation to physiologic agonists = GpIIb/IIIa defect.
Why the Answer is Correct
Glanzmann thrombasthenia is an autosomal recessive deficiency of platelet glycoprotein IIb/IIIa (integrin αIIbβ3), the fibrinogen receptor that mediates platelet-platelet cross-bridging during aggregation. Adhesion via GpIb-vWF (ristocetin) is preserved, but aggregation in response to ADP, thrombin, or collagen fails. Patients have mucocutaneous bleeding with a normal platelet count and morphology.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the glycoprotein deficient in Glanzmann thrombasthenia based on platelet aggregation studies
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Normal ristocetin aggregation (vWF/GPIb intact) + no aggregation with ADP/collagen/thrombin = platelet AGGREGATION defect not ADHESION defect. GPIIb/IIIa is required for platelet aggregation (fibrinogen bridging). GPIb is required for adhesion (vWF binding).
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Factor VII. Prolongs PT; this patient's PT is normal, and it is not a platelet disorder.
Glycoprotein Ia (alpha-2 integrin, collagen receptor). GPIa deficiency causes impaired response to collagen specifically. In Glanzmann, ALL agonists (ADP, collagen, thrombin, epinephrine) fail to aggregate. This is not a single-agonist deficiency.
(B)
Fibrinogen. Afibrinogenemia prolongs PT/aPTT/TT; normal coags here.
Glycoprotein Ib (Bernard-Soulier receptor). GPIb is required for vWF-mediated platelet ADHESION to subendothelium. Bernard-Soulier syndrome (GPIb deficiency) causes ABNORMAL ristocetin aggregation (because ristocetin agglutination requires GPIb-vWF interaction). This patient has NORMAL ristocetin response → GPIb is intact → Bernard-Soulier excluded.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Glycoprotein IIb-IIIa. Aggregation fails; adhesion (ristocetin) intact.
(D)
Granule storage pool disease. Abnormal platelets on EM, decreased aggregation with secondary wave; ristocetin normal but morphology may show dense granule loss. And key distractor. But the classic dichotomy of normal ristocetin + absent thrombin aggregation is Glanzmann.
Glycoprotein IV (CD36). Binds collagen and thrombospondin. Deficiency causes abnormal collagen-induced aggregation specifically, not global aggregation failure.
(E)
von Willebrand factor. Abnormal ristocetin aggregation; this patient's ristocetin response is normal.
Glycoprotein VI. Primary collagen receptor on platelets. Deficiency causes impaired collagen-induced aggregation specifically. Not global aggregation failure with all agonists.
Costume Change
Reappears as: Bernard-Soulier vs Glanzmann comparison. Bernard-Soulier: GPIb deficiency → adhesion defect → abnormal ristocetin → normal aggregation with ADP/collagen. Glanzmann: GPIIb/IIIa deficiency → aggregation defect → normal ristocetin → NO aggregation with any agonist. Both: autosomal recessive, mucocutaneous bleeding, prolonged bleeding time, normal PT/PTT.
Variable Pivot
If ristocetin aggregation is abnormal → vWD type 2B or Bernard-Soulier syndrome. If ristocetin aggregation is normal + no aggregation with all other agonists → Glanzmann thrombasthenia. If PT and PTT both prolonged → coagulation factor deficiency (not platelet defect). If only PTT prolonged → factor VIII/IX/XI deficiency (hemophilia A, B, or C).
Tid BitBernard-Soulier = big platelets, GpIb defect, ristocetin fails. Glanzmann = normal-sized platelets, GpIIb/IIIa defect, ristocetin works.
Q114 Block 6 Answer: C
GeneticsPopulation Genetics

Founder effect in Iceland

Vignette Summary
Icelandic woman with BRCA2 999del5; the same mutation accounts for 9% of Icelandic breast cancers.
The Discriminator
Single mutation explains a large fraction of disease in a geographically/historically isolated population = founder effect.
Why the Answer is Correct
Iceland was settled by a small founding population and remained geographically isolated, with limited gene flow. A founder who carried the BRCA2 999del5 allele passed it to a disproportionate share of descendants; the small effective population size amplified the allele frequency through genetic drift. This is the textbook founder effect (a form of bottleneck not driven by selection or by the disease itself).
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism responsible for high frequency of BRCA2 999del5 in Iceland
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Small founding population + geographic isolation + high frequency of one specific allele = FOUNDER EFFECT. A small group of founders carried this allele at high frequency, and their descendants (the modern Icelandic population) inherited it at much higher rates than the general population.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Bottleneck from breast cancer deaths. BRCA2 cancers occur post- reproduction; couldn't bottleneck the population.
Genetic drift. Random fluctuations in allele frequency from generation to generation due to chance. Not directed selection. Genetic drift can cause alleles to become fixed or eliminated. It is a broader concept than founder effect. Founder effect is actually a type of extreme genetic drift in a very small founding population. However, "founder effect" is the more specific and correct term when a population descends from a small identifiable group of founders.
(B)
Genetic heterogeneity. Argues the opposite (many different mutations), not one allele dominating.
Heterozygote advantage. Occurs when heterozygous carriers of a disease allele have higher fitness than either homozygote (e.g., sickle cell trait provides malaria resistance). There is no established survival or reproductive advantage for BRCA2 999del5 carriers.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Geographically isolated population. Founder effect + restricted gene flow.
(D)
Heterozygote advantage. No known fitness benefit of BRCA2 carriage.
Mutation rate increase. Natural populations can experience increased mutation rates from radiation or chemical exposure, but Iceland has no specific carcinogen explaining selective new mutations in exactly this BRCA2 locus. The allele was present in the founding population.
(E)
Random mating in a large population. Would dilute, not concentrate, any single allele.
Natural selection. Positive selection would mean carriers of BRCA2 999del5 have higher reproductive fitness. There is no evidence that BRCA2 mutation carriers have higher reproductive fitness. They actually have higher cancer risk.
Costume Change
Reappears as: other founder effect examples. BRCA1/2 in Ashkenazi Jews (three specific mutations at high frequency), Huntington disease high prevalence in Lake Maracaibo Venezuela, hemochromatosis HFE mutation in Northern European populations. Or: consanguinity increases risk of any recessive disease in geographically isolated populations.
Variable Pivot
If the high allele frequency were in a large heterogeneous population without geographic isolation → more likely natural selection or heterozygote advantage. If the allele were in a population with documented intermixing → founder effect still possible if the mixing occurred after the founding bottleneck.
Tid BitFounder effect = small starting population + isolation → one allele over-represented (Afrikaner porphyria, Ashkenazi Tay-Sachs, Icelandic BRCA2).
Q115 Block 6 Answer: B
CardiologyOncology

Cardiac myxoma

Vignette Summary
65F with syncope, S3/S4, pedunculated intracardiac mass → location.
The Discriminator
A pedunculated, ball-valve cardiac mass causing positional syncope is virtually always an atrial myxoma, and \~75-80% arise in the left atrium attached to the fossa ovalis.
Why the Answer is Correct
Myxoma is the most common primary adult cardiac tumor. A benign neoplasm of mesenchymal origin composed of stellate/lepidic cells in a myxoid stroma. A pedunculated mass on a stalk near the interatrial septum prolapses into the mitral orifice during diastole, intermittently obstructing flow → syncope, mitral "tumor plop," constitutional symptoms, and embolic events.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the cardiac location of the most common primary cardiac tumor
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Positional syncope + "tumor plop" on auscultation + left atrial mass = cardiac myxoma = most common primary cardiac tumor = 75% in LEFT ATRIUM.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Interventricular septum. Fibromas/rhabdomyomas live here, not pedunculated myxomas.
Right atrium. The right atrium can harbor myxomas (about 18% of cases) and is the most common location for cardiac metastases. But PRIMARY cardiac myxomas are predominantly in the LEFT atrium.
(B)
CORRECT ANSWER
Left atrium. \~75% of cardiac myxomas; fossa ovalis attachment.
(C)
Left ventricle. Rare site for myxoma; rhabdomyoma (peds) or fibroma instead.
Right ventricle. Rhabdomyoma (pediatric, associated with tuberous sclerosis) can involve the right ventricle. Myxomas in the RV are rare (< 2%).
(D)
Right atrium. Second most common site (\~20%) but less likely than LA.
Left ventricle. Myxomas rarely occur in the left ventricle. LV primary tumors are more likely to be papillary fibroelastomas or rhabdomyomas in children.
(E)
Right ventricle. Uncommon for myxoma.
Interventricular septum. Cardiac myxomas do not typically arise from the interventricular septum. Rhabdomyomas can be intramural and septal in tuberous sclerosis.
Costume Change
Reappears as: cardiac myxoma classic presentation. Positional syncope (syncope when changing position, tumor obstructs mitral valve), constitutional symptoms (fever, weight loss, elevated ESR. From IL-6 secretion), systemic emboli. Treatment: surgical excision. Rare but tested frequently on NBME.
Variable Pivot
If RV mass + carcinoid valvular disease → carcinoid heart disease (from serotonin affecting tricuspid and pulmonary valves). If cardiac tumor in child + tuberous sclerosis → rhabdomyoma. If malignant cardiac tumor → metastasis (much more common than primary malignant cardiac tumor). Most common malignant PRIMARY cardiac tumor in adults → angiosarcoma.
Tid BitBall-valve obstruction + tumor plop + 65-year-old = left atrial myxoma.
Q116 Block 6 Answer: D
ImmunologyTransplant Medicine

Hyperacute renal transplant rejection

Vignette Summary
Multiparous/prior-dialysis ESRD recipient → within 10 minutes of revascularization, graft swells, hemorrhages.
The Discriminator
Rejection within minutes = hyperacute, mediated by recipient preformed antibodies against donor antigens (ABO/HLA).
Why the Answer is Correct
Sensitization (prior transfusions, pregnancy, transplant) generates preformed IgG against donor HLA or ABO antigens. On revascularization, these antibodies bind donor endothelium and fix complement → endothelial injury, platelet activation, thrombosis of small vessels, and hemorrhagic necrosis of the graft within minutes to hours. Crossmatch testing is designed to prevent this; treatment is graft removal.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism of hyperacute transplant rejection occurring within minutes of reperfusion
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Minutes after transplant + immediate organ failure = preformed antibodies in the recipient against donor HLA antigens. These antibodies activate complement immediately upon reperfusion, causing hemorrhagic necrosis.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Perfusate hypersensitivity in donor. Donor cannot mount a reaction to the perfusate inside the recipient.
Cytotoxic T lymphocyte activation. This is the mechanism of ACUTE cellular rejection, occurring 1-3 weeks post-transplant. CTL activation requires antigen recognition + clonal expansion, which takes days, not minutes.
(B)
Perfusate hypersensitivity in recipient. Wouldn't selectively destroy the graft endothelium.
Natural killer cell cytotoxicity. NK cells can attack cells lacking self-MHC but this is a delayed process and not the mechanism of the immediate (within minutes) antibody-driven hyperacute rejection.
(C)
Preformed antibodies in donor. Donor antibodies are minimal and not directed at recipient tissues.
T helper cell sensitization. CD4+ T helper cell activation drives acute and chronic rejection processes. T cell sensitization requires days to weeks. Not the mechanism of hyperacute rejection within minutes.
(D)
CORRECT ANSWER
Preformed antibodies in recipient. Recipient anti- donor HLA/ABO IgG → type II hypersensitivity → hyperacute rejection.
(E)
Sensitized donor CD8⁺ T cells. Too few to mediate this; donor T cells can cause GVHD elsewhere, not hyperacute graft loss.
Toll-like receptor activation. Innate immune pattern recognition by TLRs contributes to ischemia-reperfusion injury and can accelerate rejection, but hyperacute rejection is specifically antibody-mediated, not TLR-mediated.
(F)
Sensitized recipient CD8⁺ T cells. Mechanism of acute cellular rejection (days-weeks), not minutes.
Costume Change
Reappears as: rejection timeline. Hyperacute (minutes to hours, preformed Ab, complement, fibrin thrombi in vessels → hemorrhagic necrosis, no treatment), acute cellular (1-3 weeks, T cell mediated, treated with steroids/anti-T-cell therapy), chronic (months-years, combined antibody + cellular, intimal proliferation, no effective treatment).
Variable Pivot
If fever + rising creatinine at 2 weeks → acute rejection → biopsy → treat with pulse steroids. If chronic allograft dysfunction at 2 years → chronic rejection → limited treatment options. If immediate organ failure at reperfusion → hyperacute rejection → remove allograft. Prevention: cross-match testing before transplant to detect preformed antibodies.
Tid BitMinutes = preformed antibody (type II). Days = T cells. Months-years = chronic vasculopathy.
Q117 Block 6 Answer: A
PharmacologyBone metabolism

Atypical femoral fracture on bisphosphonate

Vignette Summary
65M with multiple myeloma on long-term bisphosphonate → atraumatic midshaft femur fracture.
The Discriminator
Long-term bisphosphonate use → suppressed bone turnover → atypical (subtrochanteric/midshaft) femoral fracture.
Why the Answer is Correct
Bisphosphonates bind hydroxyapatite and are taken up by osteoclasts, where they inhibit farnesyl pyrophosphate synthase (mevalonate pathway), preventing prenylation of GTPases needed for the ruffled border → osteoclast apoptosis. Prolonged suppression of osteoclast-mediated resorption stalls the coupled remodeling cycle: microdamage in cortical bone is not repaired, microcracks accumulate, and a transverse atypical fracture occurs with minimal trauma. Calcium, PTH, and vitamin D are unaffected.
Cognitive Demand
Identify which physiologic process is disrupted by prolonged bisphosphonate therapy to produce an atypical femoral fracture
NBME Trap Fingerprint
The vignette names multiple myeloma in the history, which baits a RANKL/lytic-lesion answer. The fracture pattern (atraumatic, transverse, midshaft or subtrochanteric) and the drug history (long-term bisphosphonate) are the discriminators. Atypical femur fractures are an oversuppression injury, not a lytic injury. The myeloma is the reason the patient is on the drug, not the reason for the fracture.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
CORRECT ANSWER
Bone remodeling. Oversuppressed remodeling → accumulated microdamage → atypical fracture.
(B)
Osteoblast generation. Bisphosphonates target osteoclasts, not osteoblast genesis.
Osteoblast generation. Bisphosphonates target osteoclasts, not osteoblast genesis. Impaired osteoblast generation is the picture of adynamic bone disease (renal failure, oversuppressed PTH) or chronic glucocorticoid use. Not what is happening here.
(C)
Serum calcium. Typically normal or low-normal; not the lesion mechanism.
Serum calcium. Can drop transiently after an IV bisphosphonate dose (especially zoledronate without adequate calcium and vitamin D loading), but this is an acute pharmacologic effect, not the structural cause of the midshaft transverse fracture. The fracture pattern is mechanical bone fragility, not a calcium-driven event.
(D)
25-OH vitamin D. Not altered by bisphosphonate use.
25-hydroxyvitamin D. The marker for vitamin D stores. Deficiency causes osteomalacia, which produces pseudofractures (Looser zones) at sites of cortical stress, not the transverse atypical fracture pattern of subtrochanteric or midshaft femur. Vitamin D status is not altered by bisphosphonate therapy.
(E)
PTH. Unchanged; would not produce a midshaft transverse fracture pattern.
Parathyroid hormone. Elevated PTH gives osteitis fibrosa cystica with brown tumors, generalized osteopenia, and high bone turnover. PTH is unchanged by bisphosphonates and the question stem has no PTH-driven signs.
Costume Change
Reappears as: postmenopausal woman on alendronate for 8 years presents with thigh pain and a transverse midshaft fracture (same answer). Reappears as: bisphosphonate patient with jaw pain after a tooth extraction (osteonecrosis of the jaw, same drug-class). Reappears as: zoledronate patient with perioral tingling shortly after infusion (transient hypocalcemia). All three are bisphosphonate adverse effects on different organ systems.
Variable Pivot
If the question gives a focal punched-out lytic lesion on skull or vertebra plus hypercalcemia plus suppressed PTH in a myeloma patient, that is the RANKL-driven lytic mechanism (a different mechanism, different question). If the question gives chronic vitamin D deficiency plus pseudofractures plus elevated alkaline phosphatase, that is osteomalacia. The discriminator here is the DRUG, not the disease behind it.
Tid BitAtypical femur fracture (atraumatic, transverse, subtrochanteric or midshaft) on long-term bisphosphonate is an OVERSUPPRESSION injury. Microdamage cannot be cleared. The fix is a drug holiday plus intramedullary nailing. Same drug class also causes ONJ and pill esophagitis.
Q118 Block 6 Answer: C
OrthopedicsAnatomyTrauma

Lateral ankle sprain

Vignette Summary
45M twisted ankle, pain inferior to lateral malleolus → mechanism.
The Discriminator
Tenderness just distal to the lateral malleolus = anterior talofibular ligament injury, the most commonly sprained ankle ligament, caused by inversion.
Why the Answer is Correct
With the foot plantarflexed and inverted, body weight loads the lateral ankle ligaments (ATFL → CFL → PTFL in order of severity). Because the lateral ligamentous complex is weaker than the deltoid medially, inversion injuries dominate and produce tenderness over the ATFL just anteroinferior to the lateral malleolus. Eversion injuries (less common) damage the deltoid ligament medially.
Cognitive Demand
Identify the mechanism causing lateral ankle sprain
NBME Trap Fingerprint
Inversion injury → lateral ankle sprain → anterior talofibular ligament (ATFL) is the most commonly injured, followed by calcaneofibular ligament. The mechanism is plantar flexion + inversion.
Distractor Autopsies
(A)
Dorsiflexion. High ankle (syndesmotic) sprain, tenderness above the joint line.
Eversion mechanism. Eversion of the foot sprains the MEDIAL (deltoid) ligament, which is much stronger and less commonly injured than the lateral ligaments. A strong deltoid ligament injury often results in fibular fracture before the ligament tears.
(B)
Eversion. Medial (deltoid) ligament injury, not lateral.
Dorsiflexion mechanism. Forced dorsiflexion can cause posterior capsule strain or anterior impingement but is not the typical mechanism for ankle sprain. Dorsiflexion injuries more commonly cause osteochondral lesions of the talar dome.
(C)
CORRECT ANSWER
Inversion. Mechanism for ATFL sprain.
(D)
Plantar flexion. Alone doesn't sprain ligaments; combines with inversion typically.
External rotation mechanism. External rotation injuries are more associated with syndesmotic (high ankle) sprains. Injury to the tibiofibular ligament complex. These are higher on the ankle, take longer to heal, and have a different clinical presentation (pain proximal to the ankle, positive squeeze test).
Costume Change
Reappears as: Ottawa ankle rules. X-ray needed if point tenderness over posterior distal 6 cm of fibula or medial malleolus or base of 5th metatarsal, OR inability to bear weight. ATFL is most commonly injured. Treatment: RICE, early mobilization, PT. Or: grade I (stretch), II (partial tear), III (complete rupture).
Variable Pivot
If pain proximal to lateral malleolus + positive squeeze test + external rotation mechanism → high ankle (syndesmotic) sprain.
